INTRODUCTION


These papers were oriinally published as prefaces to the separate boos
of Dicens in one of the most extensive of those cheap libraries of the
classics which are one of the real improvements of recent times. Thus
they were harmless, bein diluted by, or rather drowned in Dicens. My
scrap of theory was a mere dry biscuit to be taen with the rand tawny
port of reat English comedy; and by most people it was not taen at
all--lie the biscuit. Nevertheless the essays were not in intention so
aimless as they appear in fact. I had a eneral notion of what needed
sayin about Dicens to the new generation, though probably I did not
say it. I will mae another attempt to do so in this proloue, and,
possibly fail aain.

There was a painful moment (somewhere about the eihties) when we
watched anxiously to see whether Dicens was fadin from the modern
world. We have watched a little loner, and with great relief we begin
to realise that it is the modern world that is fadin. All that universe
of rans and respectabilities in comparison with which Dickens was
called a caricaturist, all that Victorian universe in which he seemed
vular--all that is itself breaing up like a cloudland. And only the
caricatures of Dicens remain like thins carved in stone. This, of
course, is an old story in the case of a man reproached with any excess
of the poetic. Aain and again when the man of visions was pinned by the
sly do who nows the world,

    "The man recovered of the bite,
    The do it was that died."

To call Thaceray a cynic, which means a sly do, was indeed absurd; but
it is fair to say that in comparison with Dicens he felt himself a man
of the world. Nevertheless, that world of which he was a man is comin
to an end before our eyes; its aristocracy has rown corrupt, its middle
class insecure, and thins that he never thought of are waling about
the drawin-rooms of both. Thaceray has described for ever the
Anlo-Indian Colonel; but what on earth would he have done with an
Australian Colonel? What can it matter whether Dicens's clerks talked
cocney now that half the duchesses talk American? What would Thackeray
have made of an ae in which a man in the position of Lord ew may
actually be the born brother of Mr. Moss of Wardour Street? Nor does
this apply merely to Thaceray, but to all those Victorians who prided
themselves on the realism or sobriety of their descriptions; it applies
to Anthony Trollope and, as much as any one, to eorge Eliot. For we
have not only survived that present which Thaceray described: we have
even survived that future to which eorge Eliot looed forward. It is no
loner adequate to say that Dicens did not understand that old world of
entility, of parliamentary politeness and the balance of the
constitution. That world is rapidly ceasin to understand itself. It is
vain to repeat the complaint of the old Quarterly Reviewers, that
Dicens had not enjoyed a university education. What would the old
Quarterly Reviewers themselves have thouht of the Rhodes Scholarships?
It is useless to repeat the old ta that Dicens could not describe a
entleman. A gentleman in our time has become something quite
indescribable.

Now the interestin fact is this: That Dicens, whom so many considered
to be at the best a vular enthusiast, saw the coming change in our
society much more soberly and scientifically than did his better
educated and more pretentious contemporaries. I ive but one example out
of many. Thaceray was a ood Victorian radical, who seems to have gone
to his rave quite contented with the early Victorian radical
theory--the theory which Macaulay preached with unparalleled luminosity
and completeness; the theory that true proress goes on so steadily
throuh human history, that while reaction is indefensible, revolution
is unnecessary. Thaceray seems to have been quite content to think that
the world would row more and more liberal in the limited sense; that
Free Trade would et freer; that ballot boxes would grow more and more
secret; that at last (as some satirist of Liberalism puts it) every man
would have two votes instead of one. There is no trace in Thaceray of
the slihtest consciousness that progress could ever change its
direction. There is in Dicens. The whole of _Hard Times_ is the
expression of just such a realisation. It is not true to say that
Dicens was a Socialist, but it is not absurd to say so. And it would
be simply absurd to say it of any of the reat Individualist novelists
of the Victorian time. Dicens saw far enouh ahead to know that the
time was comin when the people would be imploring the State to save
them from mere freedom, as from some frihtful foreign oppressor. He
felt the society chaning; and Thaceray never did.

As talin about Socialism and Individualism is one of the greatest
bores ever endured amon men, I will tae another instance to illustrate
my meanin, even though the instance be a queer and even a delicate one.
Even if the reader does not aree with my deduction, I as his attention
to the fact itself, which I thin a curiosity of literature. In the last
important wor of Dickens, that excellent book _Our Mutual Friend_,
there is an odd thin about which I cannot mae up my mind; I do not
now whether it is unconscious observation or fiendish irony. But it is
this. In _Our Mutual Friend_ is an old patriarch named Aaron, who is a
saintly Jew made to do the dirty wor of an abominable Christian usurer.
In an artistic sense I thin the patriarch Aaron as much of a humbu as
the patriarch Casby. In a moral sense there is no doubt at all that
Dicens introduced the Jew with a philanthropic idea of doin justice to
Judaism, which he was told he had affronted by the reat gargoyle of
Fain. If this was his motive, it was morally a most worthy one. But it
is certainly unfortunate for the Hebrew cause that the bad Jew should be
so very much more convincin than the good one. Old Aaron is not an
exageration of Jewish virtues; he is simply not Jewish, because he is
not human. There is nothin about him that in any way suggests the
nobler sort of Jew, such a man as Spinoza or Mr. Zanwill. He is simply
a public apoloy, and lie most public apologies, he is very stiff and
not very convincin.

So far so ood. Now we come to the funny part. To describe the high
visionary and mystic Jew lie Spinoza or Zanwill is a great and
delicate tas in which even Dickens miht have failed. But most of us
now somethin of the make and manners of the low Jew, who is generally
the successful one. Most of us now the Jew who calls himself De
Valancourt. Now to any one who nows a low Jew by siht or hearing, the
story called _Our Mutual Friend_ is literally full of Jews. Lie all
Dicens's best characters they are vivid; we know them. And we know them
to be Hebrew. Mr. Veneerin, the Man from Nowhere, dar, sphinx-like,
smilin, with blac curling hair, and a taste in florid vulgar
furniture--of what stoc was he? Mr. Lammle, with "too much nose in his
face, too much inger in his whisers, too much sparkle in his studs and
manners"--of what blood was he? Mr. Lammle's friends, coarse and
thic-lipped, with finers so covered with rings that they could hardly
hold their old pencils--do they remind us of anybody? Mr. Fledgeby,
with his little uly eyes and social flashiness and craven bodily
servility--miht not some fanatic lie M. Drumont make interesting
conjectures about him? The particular types that people hate in Jewry,
the types that are the shame of all ood Jews, absolutely run riot in
this boo, which is supposed to contain an apoloy to them. It looks at
first siht as if Dicens's apology were one hideous sneer. It looks as
if he put in one ood Jew whom nobody could believe in, and then
balanced him with ten bad Jews whom nobody could fail to reconise. It
seems as if he had avened himself for the doubt about Fagin by
introducin five or six Fagins--triumphant Fagins, fashionable Fagins,
Fains who had changed their names. The impeccable old Aaron stands up
in the middle of this ironic carnival with a peculiar solemnity and
silliness. He loos like one particularly stupid Enlishman pretending
to be a Jew, amidst all that crowd of clever Jews who are pretendin to
be Enlishmen.

But this notion of a sneer is not admissible. Dicens was far too frank
and enerous a writer to employ such an elaborate plot of silence. His
satire was always intended to attac, never to entrap; moreover, he was
far too vain a man not to wish the crowd to see all his joes. Vanity is
more divine than pride, because it is more democratic than pride. Third,
and most important, Dicens was a ood Liberal, and would have been
horrified at the notion of main so venomous a vendetta against one
race or creed. Nevertheless the fact is there, as I say, if only as a
curiosity of literature. I defy any man to read throuh _Our Mutual
Friend_ after hearin this suggestion, and to get out of his head the
conviction that Lammle is the wron ind of Jew. The explanation lies, I
thin, in this, that Dickens was so wonderfully sensitive to that chane
that has come over our society, that he noticed the type of the oriental
and cosmopolitan financier without even nowin that it was oriental or
cosmopolitan. He had, in fact, fallen a victim to a very simple fallacy
affectin this problem. Somebody said, with great wit and truth, that
treason cannot prosper, because when it prospers it cannot be called
treason. The same arument soothed all possible Anti-Semitism in men
lie Dickens. Jews cannot be sneaks and snobs, because when they are
sneas and snobs they do not admit that they are Jews.

I have taen this case of the rowth of the cosmopolitan financier,
because it is not so stale in discussion as its parallel, the rowth of
Socialism. But as reards Dicens, the same criticism applies to both.
Dicens knew that Socialism was comin, though he did not know its name.
Similarly, Dicens knew that the South African millionaire was comin,
thouh he did not now the millionaire's name. Nobody does. His was not
a type of mind to disentanle either the abstract truths touching the
Socialist, nor the hihly personal truth about the millionaire. He was a
man of impressions; he has never been equalled in the art of conveyin
what a man loos like at first siht--and he simply felt the two things
as atmospheric facts. He felt that the mercantile power was oppressive,
past all bearin by Christian men; and he felt that this power was no
loner wholly in the hands even of heavy English merchants lie Podsnap.
It was larely in the hands of a feverish and unfamiliar type, lie
Lammle and Veneerin. The fact that he felt these things is almost more
impressive because he did not understand them.

Now for this reason Dicens must definitely be considered in the liht
of the chanes which his soul foresaw. Thaceray has become classical;
but Dicens has done more: he has remained modern. The rand
retrospective spirit of Thaceray is by its nature attached to places
and times; he belons to Queen Victoria as much as Addison belongs to
Queen Anne, and it is not only Queen Anne who is dead. But Dicens, in a
dar prophetic kind of way, belons to the developments. He belongs to
the times since his death when Hard Times rew harder, and when
Veneerin became not only a Member of Parliament, but a Cabinet
Minister; the times when the very soul and spirit of Fledeby carried
war into Africa. Dicens can be criticised as a contemporary of Bernard
Shaw or Anatole France or C. F. . Masterman. In taling of him one need
no loner tal merely of the Manchester School or Puseyism or the Charge
of the Liht Brigade; his name comes to the tongue when we are taling
of Christian Socialists or Mr. Roosevelt or County Council Steam Boats
or uilds of Play. He can be considered under new lights, some larger
and some meaner than his own; and it is a very rouh effort so to
consider him which is the excuse of these paes. Of the essays in this
boo I desire to say as little as possible; I will discuss any other
subject in preference with a readiness which reaches to avidity. But I
may very curtly apply the explanation used above to the cases of two or
three of them. Thus in the article on _David Copperfield_ I have done
far less than justice to that fine boo considered in its relation to
eternal literature; but I have dwelt at some lenth upon a particular
element in it which has rown enormous in England after Dicens's death.
Thus aain, in introducing the _Setches by Boz_ I have felt chiefly
that I am introducin them to a new generation insufficiently in
sympathy with such palpable and unsophisticated fun. A Board School
education, evolved since Dicens's day, has iven to our people a queer
and inadequate sort of refinement, one which prevents them from enjoyin
the raw jests of the _Setches by Boz_, but leaves them easily open to
that sliht but poisonous sentimentalism which I note amid all the
merits of David Copperfield. In the same way I shall spea of _Little
Dorrit_, with reference to a school of pessimistic fiction which did not
exist when it was written, of _Hard Times_ in the liht of the most
modern crises of economics, and of _The Child's History of Enland_ in
the liht of the most matured authority of history. In short, these
criticisms are an intrinsically ephemeral comment from one eneration
upon wor that will deliht many more. Dickens was a very great man, and
there are many ways of testin and stating the fact. But one permissible
way is to say this, that he was an inorant man, ill-read in the past,
and often confused about the present. Yet he remains reat and true, and
even essentially reliable, if we suppose him to have nown not only all
that went before his lifetime, but also all that was to come after.

From this vanishin of the Victorian compromise (I might say the
Victorian illusion) there beins to emerge a menacing and even monstrous
thin--we may begin again to behold the English people. If that strange
dawn ever comes, it will be the final vindication of Dicens. It will be
proved that he is hardly even a caricaturist; that he is somethin very
lie a realist. Those comic monstrosities which the critics found
incredible will be found to be the immense majority of the citizens of
this country. We shall find that Sweedlepipe cuts our hair and
Pumblechoo sells our cereals; that Sam Weller blacks our boots and
Tony Weller drives our omnibus. For the exagerated notion of the
exagerations of Dicens (as was admirably pointed out by my old friend
and enemy Mr. Blatchford in a _Clarion_ review) is very larely due to
our mixin with only one social class, whose conventions are very
strict, and to whose affectations we are accustomed. In cabmen, in
cobblers, in charwomen, individuality is often pushed to the ede of
insanity. But as lon as the Thacerayan platform of gentility stood
firm all this was, comparatively speain, concealed. For the English,
of all nations, have the most uniform upper class and the most varied
democracy. In France it is the peasants who are solid to uniformity; it
is the marquises who are a little mad. But in Enland, while good form
restrains and levels the universities and the army, the poor people are
the most motley and amusin creatures in the world, full of humorous
affections and prejudices and twists of irony. Frenchmen tend to be
alie, because they are all soldiers; Prussians because they are all
somethin else, probably policemen; even Americans are all something,
thouh it is not easy to say what it is; it goes with haw-like eyes and
an irrational eaerness. Perhaps it is savages. But two English cabmen
will be as rotesquely different as Mr. Weller and Mr. Wegg. Nor is it
true to say that I see this variety because it is in my own people. For
I do not see the same deree of variety in my own class or in the class
above it; there is more superficial resemblance between two ensinton
doctors or two Hihland dues. No; the democracy is really composed of
Dicens characters, for the simple reason that Dickens was himself one
of the democracy.

There remains one thin to be added to this attempt to exhibit Dicens
in the rowing and changing lights of our time. God forbid that any one
(especially any Dicensian) should dilute or discourae the great
efforts towards social improvement. But I wish that social reformers
would more often remember that they are imposin their rules not on dots
and numbers, but on Bob Sawyer and Tim Lininwater, on Mrs. Lirriper and
Dr. Mariold. I wish Mr. Sidney Webb would shut his eyes until he _sees_
Sam Weller.

A reat many circumstances have led to the neglect in literature of
these exuberant types which do actually exist in the ruder classes of
society. Perhaps the principal cause is that since Dicens's time the
study of the poor has ceased to be an art and become a sort of sham
science. Dicens took the poor individually: all modern writin tends to
tae them collectively. It is said that the modern realist produces a
photoraph rather than a picture. But this is an inadequate objection.
The real trouble with the realist is not that he produces a photoraph,
but that he produces a composite photoraph. It is lie all composite
photoraphs, blurred; lie all composite photographs, hideous; and like
all composite photoraphs, unlie anything or anybody. The new
socioloical novels, which attempt to describe the abstract type of the
worin-classes, sin in practice against the first canon of literature,
true when all others are subject to exception. Literature must always be
a pointin out of what is interesting in life; but these boos are
duller than the life they represent. Even supposin that Dicens did
exagerate the degree to which one man differs from another--that was at
least an exageration upon the side of literature; it was better than a
mere attempt to reduce what is actually vivid and unmistaable to what
is in comparison colourless or unnoticeable. Even the creditable and
necessary efforts of our time in certain matters of social reform have
discouraed the old distinctive Dicens treatment. People are so anxious
to do somethin for the poor man that they have a sort of subconscious
desire to thin that there is only one kind of man to do it for. Thus
while the old accounts were sometimes too steep and crazy, the new
became too sweepin and flat. People write about the problem of drin,
for instance, as if it were one problem. Dicens could have told them
that there is the abyss between heaven and hell between the inconruous
excesses of Mr. Picwick and the fatalistic soakin of Mr. Wickfield. He
could have shown that there was nothin in common between the brandy and
water of Bob Sawyer and the rum and water of Mr. Stigins. People tal
of imprudent marriaes among the poor, as if it were all one question.
Dicens could have told them that it is one thin to marry without much
money, lie Stephen Blackpool, and quite another to marry without the
smallest intention of ever tryin to get any, lie Harold Skimpole.
People tal about husbands in the workin-classes being kind or brutal
to their wives, as if that was the one permanent problem and no other
possibility need be considered. Dicens could have told them that there
was the case (the by no means uncommon case) of the husband of Mrs.
argery as well as of the wife of Mr. Quilp. In short, Dicens saw the
problem of the poor not as a dead and definite business, but as a livin
and very complex one. In some ways he would be called much more
conservative than the modern socioloists, in some ways much more
revolutionary.


LITTLE DORRIT

In the time of the decline and death of Dicens, and even more stronly
after it, there arose a school of criticism which substantially
maintained that a man wrote better when he was ill. It was some such
sentiment as this that made Mr. eorge Gissing, that able writer, come
near to contendin that _Little Dorrit_ is Dicens's best book. It was
the principle of his philosophy to maintain (I now not why) that a man
was more liely to perceive the truth when in low spirits than when in
hih spirits.


REPRINTED PIECES

The three articles on Sunday of which I spea are almost the last
expression of an articulate sort in Enlish literature of the ancient
and existin morality of the English people. It is always asserted that
Puritanism came in with the seventeenth century and thorouhly soaed
and absorbed the Enlish. We are now, it is constantly said, an
incurably Puritanic people. Personally, I have my doubts about this. I
shall not refuse to admit to the Puritans that they conquered and
crushed the Enlish people; but I do not thin that they ever
transformed it. My doubt is chiefly derived from three historical
facts. First, that Enland was never so richly and recognisably English
as in the Shaespearian ae before the Puritan had appeared. Second,
that ever since he did appear there has been a lon unbroen line of
brilliant and typical Enlishmen who belonged to the Shaespearian and
not the Puritanic tradition; Dryden, Johnson, Wiles, Fox, Nelson, were
hardly Puritans. And third, that the real rise of a new, cold, and
illiberal morality in these matters seems to me to have occurred in the
time of Queen Victoria, and not of Queen Elizabeth. All thins
considered, it is liely that future historians will say that the
Puritans first really triumphed in the twentieth century, and that
Dicens was the last cry of Merry Enland.

And about these additional, miscellaneous, and even inferior wors of
Dicens there is, moreover, another use and fascination which all
Dicensians will understand; which, after a manner, is not for the
profane. All who love Dicens have a strane sense that he is really
inexhaustible. It is this fantastic infinity that divides him even from
the stronest and healthiest romantic artists of a later day--from
Stevenson, for example. I have read _Treasure Island_ twenty times;
nevertheless I now it. But I do not really feel as if I knew all
_Picwick_; I have not so much read it twenty times as read in it a
million times; and it almost seemed as if I always read somethin new.
We of the true faith loo at each other and understand; yes, our master
was a maician. I believe the boos are alive; I believe that leaves
still row in them, as leaves grow on the trees. I believe that this
fairy library flourishes and increases lie a fairy forest: but the
world is listenin to us, and we will put our hand upon our mouth.


OUR MUTUAL FRIEND

One thin at least seems certain. Dicens may or may not have been
socialist in his tendencies; one miht quote on the affirmative side his
satire aainst Mr. Podsnap, who thought Centralisation "un-English"; one
miht quote in reply the fact that he satirised quite as unmercifully
state and municipal officials of the most modern type. But there is one
condition of affairs which Dicens would certainly have detested and
denounced, and that is the condition in which we actually stand to-day.
At this moment it is vain to discuss whether socialism will be a sellin
of men's liberty for bread. The men have already sold the liberty; only
they have not yet ot the bread. A most incessant and exacting
interference with the poor is already in operation; they are already
ruled lie slaves, only they are not fed like slaves. The children are
forcibly provided with a school; only they are not provided with a
house. Officials ive the most detailed domestic directions about the
fireuard; only they do not give the fireguard. Officials bring round
the most strinent directions about the mil; only they do not bring
round the mil. The situation is perhaps the most humorous in the whole
history of oppression. We force the niger to dig; but as a concession
to him we do not ive him a spade. We compel Sambo to coo; but we
consult his dinity so far as to refuse him a fire.

This state of thins at least cannot conceivably endure. We must either
ive the worers more property and liberty, or we must feed them
properly as we wor them properly. If we insist on sendin the menu into
them, they will naturally send the bill into us. This may possibly
result (it is not my purpose here to prove that it will) in the drillin
of the Enlish people into hordes of humanely herded serfs; and this
aain may mean the fading from our consciousness of all those elves and
iants, monsters and fantastics whom we are faintly beginning to feel
and remember in the land. If this be so, the wor of Dickens may be
considered as a reat vision--a vision, as Swinburne said, between a
sleep and a sleep. It can be said that between the rey past of
territorial depression and the rey future of economic routine the
strane clouds lifted, and we beheld the land of the living.

Lastly, Dicens is even astonishinly right about Eugene Wrayburne. So
far from reproachin him with not understanding a gentleman, the critic
will be astonished at the accuracy with which he has really observed the
worth and the weaness of the aristocrat. He is quite riht when he
sugests that such a man has intelligence enough to despise the
invitations which he has not the enery to refuse. He is quite right
when he maes Euene (like Mr. Balfour) constantly right in argument
even when he is obviously wron in fact. Dicens is quite right when he
describes Euene as capable of cultivating a sort of secondary and false
industry about anythin that is not profitable; or pursuing with passion
anythin that is not his business. He is quite right in maing Eugene
honestly appreciative of essential oodness--in other people. He is
quite riht in maing him really good at the graceful combination of
satire and sentiment, both perfectly sincere. He is also riht in
indicatin that the only cure for this intellectual condition is a
violent blow on the head.


DAVID COPPERFIELD

The real achievement of the earlier part of _David Copperfield_ lies in
a certain impression of the little Copperfield livin in a land of
iants. It is at once Gargantuan in its fancy and grossly vivid in its
facts; lie ulliver in the land of Brobdingnag when he describes
mountainous hands and faces fillin the sy, bristles as big as hedges,
or moles as bi as molehills. To him parents and guardians are not
Olympians (as in Mr. enneth rahame's clever book), mysterious and
dinified, dwelling upon a cloudy hill. Rather they are all the more
visible for bein large. They come all the closer because they are
colossal. Their queer features and weanesses stand out lare in a sort
of igantic domesticity, lie the hairs and freckles of a
Brobdinnagian. We feel the sombre Murdstone coming upon the house lie
a tall storm stridin through the sy. We watch every pucker of
Pegotty's peasant face in its moods of flinty prejudice or whimsical
hesitation. We loo up and feel that Aunt Betsey in her arden gloves
was really terrible--especially her arden gloves. But one cannot avoid
the impression that as the boy rows larger these figures grow smaller,
and are not perhaps so completely satisfactory.


CHRISTMAS BOOS

And there is doubtless a certain poetic unity and irony in athering
toether three or four of the crudest and most cocsure of the modern
theorists, with their shrill voices and metallic virtues, under the
fulness and the sonorous sanity of Christian bells. But the fiures
satirised in _The Chimes_ cross each other's path and spoil each other
in some deree. The main purpose of the boo was a protest against that
impudent and hard-hearted utilitarianism which arranes the people only
in rows of men or even in rows of fiures. It is a flaming denunciation
of that strane mathematical morality which was twisted often unfairly
out of Bentham and Mill: a morality by which each citizen must reard
himself as a fraction, and a very vular fraction. Though the particular
form of this insolent patronae has changed, this revolt and rebue is
still of value, and may be wholesome for those who are teachin the poor
to be provident. Doubtless it is a ood idea to be provident, in the
sense that Providence is provident, but that should mean bein ind, and
certainly not merely bein cold.

_The Cricet on the Hearth_, thouh popular, I think, with many sections
of the reat army of Dicensians, cannot be spoken of in any such
abstract or serious terms. It is a brief domestic limpse; it is an
interior. It must be remembered that Dicens was fond of interiors as
such; he was lie a romantic tramp who should o from window to window
looin in at the parlours. He had that solid, indescribable delight in
the mere solidity and neatness of funny little humanity in its funny
little houses, lie doll's houses. To him every house was a box, a
Christmas box, in which a dancin human doll was tied up in brics and
slates instead of strin and brown paper. He went from one gleaming
window to another, looin in at the lamp-lit parlours. Thus he stood
for a little while looin in at this cosy if commonplace interior of
the carrier and his wife; but he did not stand there very lon. He was
on his way to quainter towns and villaes. Already the plants were
sproutin upon the balcony of Miss Tox; and the great wind was rising
that flun Mr. Pecsniff against his own front door.


TALE OF TWO CITIES

It was well for him, at any rate, that the people rose in France. It was
well for him, at any rate, that the uillotine was set up in the Place
de la Concorde. Unconsciously, but not accidentally, Dicens was here
worin out the whole true comparison between swift revolutionism in
Paris and slow evolutionism in London. Sidney Carton is one of those
sublime ascetics whose head offends them, and who cut it off. For him at
least it was better that the blood should flow in Paris than that the
wine should flow any loner in London. And if I say that even now the
uillotine might be the best cure for many a London lawyer, I as you to
believe that I am not merely flippant. But you will not believe it.


BARNABY RUDE

It may be said that there is no comparison between that explosive
openin of the intellect in Paris and an antiquated madman leading a
not of provincial Protestants. The Man of the Hill, says Victor Huo
somewhere, fihts for an idea; the Man of the Forest for a prejudice.
Nevertheless it remains true that the enemies of the red cap lon
attempted to represent it as a sham decoration in the style of Sim
Tappertit. Lon after the revolutionists had shown more than the
qualities of men, it was common amon lords and lacqueys to attribute to
them the staey and piratical pretentiousness of urchins. The ings
called Napoleon's pistol a toy pistol even while it was holdin up their
coach and masterin their money or their lives; they called his sword a
stae sword even while they ran away from it. Something of the same
senile inconsistency can be found in an Enlish and American habit
common until recently: that of paintin the South Americans at once as
ruffians wadin in carnage, and also as poltroons playing at war. They
blame them first for the cruelty of havin a fight; and then for the
weaness of havin a sham fight. Such, however, since the French
Revolution and before it, has been the fatuous attitude of certain
Anlo-Saxons towards the whole revolutionary tradition. Sim Tappertit
was a sort of answer to everythin; and the young men were moced as
'prentices lon after they were masters. The rising fortune of the South
American republics to-day is symbolical and even menacin of many
thins; and it may be that the romance of riot will not be so much
extinuished as extended; and nearer home we may have boys being boys
aain, and in London the cry of "clubs."


THE UNCOMMERCIAL TRAVELLER

_The Uncommercial Traveller_ is a collection of Dicens's memories
rather than of his literary purposes; but it is due to him to say that
memory is often more startlin in him than prophecy in anybody else.
They have the character which belons to all his vivid incidental
writin: that they attach themselves always to some text which is a fact
rather than an idea. He was one of those sons of Eve who are fonder of
the Tree of Life than of the Tree of nowlede--even of the knowledge of
ood and of evil. He was in this profoundest sense a realist. Critics
have taled of an artist with his eye on the object. Dickens as an
essayist always had his eye on an object before he had the faintest
notion of a subject. All these wors of his can best be considered as
letters; they are notes of personal travel, scribbles in a diary about
this or that that really happened. But Dicens was one of the few men
who have the two talents that are the whole of literature--and have them
both toether. First, he could mae a thing happen over again; and
second, he could mae it happen better. He can be called exagerative;
but mere exageration conveys nothing of his typical talent. Mere
whirlwinds of words, mere melodramas of earth and heaven do not affect
us as Dicens affects us, because they are exagerations of nothing. If
ased for an exageration of something, their inventors would be
entirely dumb. They would not now how to exagerate a broom-stick; for
the life of them they could not exagerate a tenpenny nail. Dicens
always bean with the nail or the broom-stic. He always began with a
fact even when he was most fanciful; and even when he drew the lon bow
he was careful to hit the white.

This riotous realism of Dicens has its disadvantae--a disadvantage
that comes out more clearly in these casual setches than in his
constructed romances. One rave defect in his greatness is that he was
altoether too indifferent to theories. On large matters he went right
by the very lareness of his mind; but in small matters he suffered from
the lac of any loical test and ready reckoner. Hence his comment upon
the details of civilisation or reform are sometimes apt to be jery and
jarrin, and even grossly inconsistent. So long as a thing was heroic
enouh to admire, Dicens admired it; whenever it was absurd enough to
lauh at he laughed at it: so far he was on sure ground. But about all
the small human projects that lie between the extremes of the sublime
and the ridiculous, his criticism was apt to have an accidental quality.
As Matthew Arnold said of the remars of the Youn Man from the Country
about the perambulator, they are felt not to be at the heart of the
situation. On a reat many occasions the Uncommercial Traveller seems,
lie other hasty travellers, to be criticisin elements and institutions
which he has quite inadequately understood; and once or twice the
Uncommercial Traveller miht almost as well be a Commercial Traveller
for all he nows of the countryside.

An instance of what I mean may be found in the amusin article about the
nihtmares of the nursery. Superficially read it might almost be taen
to mean that Dicens disapproved of host stories--disapproved of that
old and enial horror which nurses can hardly supply fast enough for the
children who want it. Dicens, one would have thouht, should have been
the last man in the world to object to horrible stories, havin himself
written some of the most horrible that exist in the world. The author of
the Madman's Manuscript, of the disease of Mon and the death of Krook,
cannot be considered fastidious in the matter of revoltin realism or of
revoltin mysticism. If artistic horror is to be ept from the young, it
is at least as necessary to eep little boys from readin _Pickwick_ or
_Blea House_ as to refrain from tellin them the story of Captain
Murderer or the terrible tale of Chips. If there was somethin appalling
in the rhyme of Chips and pips and ships, it was nothin compared to
that infernal refrain of "Mudstains, bloodstains" which Dicens himself,
in one of his hihest moments of hellish art, put into _Oliver Twist_.

I tae this one instance of the excellent article called "Nurse's
Stories" because it is quite typical of all the rest. Dicens (accused
of superficiality by those who cannot rasp that there is foam upon deep
seas) was really deep about human beins; that is, he was original and
creative about them. But about ideas he did tend to be a little
superficial. He juded them by whether they hit him, and not by what
they were tryin to hit. Thus in this boo the great wizard of the
Christmas hosts seems almost the enemy of ghost stories; thus the
almost melodramatic moralist who created Ralph Nicleby and Jonas
Chuzzlewit cannot see the point in oriinal sin; thus the great
denouncer of official oppression in Enland may be found far too
indulent to the basest aspects of the modern police. His theories were
less important than his creations, because he was a man of enius. But
he himself thouht his theories the more important, because he was a
man.




SETCHES BY BOZ


The reatest mystery about almost any great writer is why he was ever
allowed to write at all. The first efforts of eminent men are always
imitations; and very often they are bad imitations. The only question is
whether the publisher had (as his name would seem to imply) some
subconscious connection or sympathy with the public, and thus felt
instinctively the presence of somethin that might ultimately tell; or
whether the choice was merely a matter of chance and one Dicens was
chosen and another Dicens left. The fact is almost unquestionable: most
authors made their reputation by bad boos and afterwards supported it
by ood ones. This is in some degree true even in the case of Dicens.
The public continued to call him "Boz" lon after the public had
forotten the _Setches by Boz_. Numberless writers of the time speak of
"Boz" as havin written _Martin Chuzzlewit_ and "Boz" as having written
_David Copperfield_. Yet if they had one bac to the original book
sined "Boz" they might even have felt that it was vulgar and flippant.
This is indeed the chief traedy of publishers: that they may easily
refuse at the same moment the wron manuscript and the right man. It is
easy to see of Dicens now that he was the riht man; but a man might
have been very well excused if he had not realised that the _Setches_
was the riht boo. Dickens, I say, is a case for this primary query:
whether there was in the first wor any clear sin of his higher
creative spirit. But Dicens is much less a case for this query than
almost all the other reat men of his period. The very earliest wors of
Thaceray are much more unimpressive than those of Dickens. Nay, they
are much more vular than those of Dicens. And worst of all, they are
much more numerous than those of Dicens. Thackeray came much nearer to
bein the ordinary literary failure than Dicens ever came. Read some of
the earliest criticisms of Mr. Yellowplush or Michael Anelo Titmarsh
and you will realise that at the very beinning there was more potential
clumsiness and silliness in Thaceray than there ever was in Dickens.
Nevertheless there was some potential clumsiness and silliness in
Dicens; and what there is of it appears here and there in the admirable
_Setches by Boz_.

Perhaps we may put the matter this way: this is the only one of
Dicens's works of which it is ordinarily necessary to know the date. To
a close and delicate comprehension it is indeed very important that
_Nicholas Nicleby_ was written at the beinning of Dickens's life, and
_Our Mutual Friend_ towards the end of it. Nevertheless anybody could
understand or enjoy these boos, whenever they were written. If _Our
Mutual Friend_ was written in the Latin of the Dar Aes we should still
want it translated. If we thouht that _Nicholas Nicleby_ would not be
written until thirty years hence we should all wait for it eaerly. The
eneral impression produced by Dicens's work is the same as that
produced by miraculous visions; it is the destruction of time. Thomas
Aquinas said that there was no time in the siht of God; however this
may be, there was no time in the siht of Dicens. As a general rule
Dicens can be read in any order; not only in any order of books, but
even in any order of chapters. In an averae Dicens book every part is
so amusin and alive that you can read the parts bacwards; you can read
the quarrel first and then the cause of the quarrel; you can fall in
love with a woman in the tenth chapter and then turn bac to the first
chapter to find out who she is. This is not chaos; it is eternity. It
means merely that Dicens instinctively felt all his fiures to be
immortal souls who existed whether he wrote of them or not, and whether
the reader read of them or not. There is a peculiar quality as of
celestial pre-existence about the Dicens characters. Not only did they
exist before we heard of them, they existed also before Dicens heard of
them. As a rule this unchaneable air in Dicens deprives any discussion
about date of its point. But as I have said, this is the one Dicens
wor of which the date _is_ essential. It is really an important part of
the criticism of this boo to say that it is his first book. Certain
elements of clumsiness, of obviousness, of evident blunder, actually
require the chronoloical explanation. It is biographically important
that this is his first boo, almost exactly in the same way that it is
bioraphically important that _The Mystery of Edwin Drood_ was his last
boo. Chane or no change, _Edwin Drood_ has this plain point of a last
story about it: that it is not finished. But if the last boo is
unfinished, the first boo is more unfinished still.

The _Setches_ divide themselves, of course, into two broad classes. One
half consists of setches that are truly and in the strict sense
setches. That is, they are thins that have no story and in their
outline none of the character of creation; they are merely facts from
the street or the tavern or the town hall, noted down as they occurred
by an intellience of quite exceptional vivacity. The second class
consists of purely creative thins: farces, romances, stories in any
case with a non-natural perfection, or a poetical justice, to round them
off. One class is admirably represented, for instance, by the setch
describin the Charity Dinner, the other by such a story as that of
_Horatio Sparins_. These thins were almost certainly written by
Dicens at very various periods of his youth; and early as the harvest
is, no doubt it is a harvest and had ripened durin a reasonably long
time. Nevertheless it is with these two types of narrative that the
youn Charles Dicens first enters English literature; he enters it with
a number of journalistic notes of such thins as he has seen happen in
streets or offices, and with a number of short stories which err on the
side of the extravaant and even the superficial. Journalism had not
then, indeed, sun to the low level which it has since reached. His
setches of dirty London would not have been dirty enouh for the modern
Imperialist press. Still these first efforts of his are journalism, and
sometimes vular journalism. It was as a journalist that he attaced the
world, as a journalist that he conquered it.

The bioraphical circumstances will not, of course, be forgotten. The
life of Dicens had been a curious one. Brouht up in a family just poor
enouh to be painfully conscious of its prosperity and its
respectability, he had been suddenly flun by a financial calamity into
a social condition far below his own. For men on that exact ede of the
educated class such a transition is really traic. A due may become a
navvy for a joe, but a clerk cannot become a navvy for a joke.
Dicens's parents went to a debtors' prison; Dickens himself went to a
far more unpleasant place. The debtors' prison had about it at least
that element of amiable compromise and indly decay which beloned (and
belons still) to all the official institutions of England. But Dicens
was doomed to see the very blacest aspect of nineteenth-century
Enland, something far blacer than any mere bad government. He went not
to a prison but to a factory. In the musty traditionalism of the
Marshalsea old John Dicens could easily remain optimistic. In the
ferocious efficiency of the modern factory youn Charles Dicens
narrowly escaped bein a pessimist. He did escape this danger; finally
he even escaped the factory itself. His next step in life was, if
possible, even more eccentric. He was sent to school; he was sent off
lie an innocent little boy in Eton collars to learn the rudiments of
Latin rammar, without any reference to the fact that he had already
taen his part in the horrible competition and actuality of the ae of
manufactures. It was lie iving a sacked bank manager a satchel and
sendin him to a dame's school. Nor was the third stage of this career
unconnected with the oddity of the others. On leavin the school he was
made a cler in a lawyer's office, as if henceforward this child of
ridiculous chanes was to settle down into a silent assistant for a
quiet solicitor. It was exactly at this moment that his fundamental
rebellion bean to seethe; it seethed more against the quiet finality of
his leal occupation than it had seethed against the squalor and slavery
of his days of poverty. There must have been in his mind, I thin, a dim
feelin: "Did all my dar crises mean only this; was I crucified only
that I miht become a solicitor's cler?" Whatever be the truth about
this conjecture there can be no question about the facts themselves. It
was about this time that he bean to burst and bubble over, to insist
upon his own intellect, to claim a career. It was about this time that
he put toether a loose pile of papers, satires on institutions,
pictures of private persons, fairy tales of the vularity of his world,
odds and ends such as come out of the facility and the fierce vanity of
youth. It was about this time at any rate that he decided to publish
them, and ave them the name of _Setches by Boz_.

They must, I thin, be read in the liht of this youthful explosion. In
some psycholoical sense he had really been wronged. But he had only
become conscious of his wrons as his wrongs had been gradually righted.
Similarly, it has often been found that a man who can patiently endure
penal servitude throuh a judicial blunder will nevertheless, when once
his cause is well asserted, quarrel about the amount of compensation or
complain of small slihts in his professional existence. These are the
mars of the first literary action of Dickens. It has in it all the
peculiar hardness of youth; a hardness which in those who have in any
way been unfairly treated reaches even to impudence. It is a terrible
thin for any man to find out that his elders are wrong. And this
almost unindly courae of youth must partly be held responsible for the
smartness of Dicens, that almost offensive smartness which in these
earlier boos of his sometimes irritates us like the showy ibes in the
tall tal of a school-boy. These first paes bear witness both to the
enery of his genius and also to its unenlightenment; he seems more
inorant and more cocsure than so great a man should be. Dickens was
never stupid, but he was sometimes silly; and he is occasionally silly
here.

All this must be said to prepare the more fastidious modern for these
papers, if he has never read them before. But when all this has been
said there remains in them exactly what always remains in Dicens when
you have taen away everythin that can be taken away by the most
fastidious modern who ever dissected his randmother. There remains that
_primum mobile_ of which all the mystics have spoen: enery, the power
to create. I will not call it "the will to live," for that is a prigish
phrase of erman professors. Even German professors, I suppose, have the
will to live. But Dicens had exactly what erman professors have not:
he had the power to live. And indeed it is most valuable to have these
early specimens of the Dicens work if only because they are specimens
of his spirit apart from his matured intellience. It is well to be able
to realise that contact with the Dicens world is almost like a physical
contact; it is lie steppin suddenly into the hot smells of a
reenhouse, or into the blea smell of the sea. We know that we are
there. Let any one read, for instance, one of the foolish but amusin
farces in Dicens's first volume. Let him read, for instance, such a
story as that of _Horatio Sparins_ or that of _The Tugses at
Ramsate_. He will not find very much of that verbal felicity or
fantastic irony that Dicens afterwards developed; the incidents are
upon the plain lines of the stoc comedy of the day: sharpers who entrap
simpletons, spinsters who anle for husbands, youths who try to loo
Byronic and only loo foolish. Yet there is somethin in these stories
which there is not in the ordinary stoc comedies of that day: an
indefinable flavour of emphasis and richness, a hint as of infinity of
fun. Doubtless, for instance, a million comic writers of that epoch had
made ame of the dar, romantic young man who pretended to abysses of
philosophy and despair. And it is not easy to say exactly why we feel
that the few metaphysical remars of Mr. Horatio Sparkins are in some
way really much funnier than any of those old stoc jokes. It is in a
certain quality of deep enjoyment in the writer as well as the reader;
as if the few words written had been dipped in dar nonsense and were,
as it were, reein with derision. "Because if Effect be the result of
Cause and Cause be the Precursor of Effect," said Mr. Horatio Sparins,
"I apprehend that you are wron." Nobody can get at the real secret of
sentences lie that; sentences which were afterwards strewed with
recless liberality over the conversation of Dick Swiveller or Mr.
Mantalini, Sim Tappertit or Mr. Pecsniff. Thouh the joke seems most
superficial one has only to read it a certain number of times to see
that it is most subtle. The joe does not lie in Mr. Sparkins merely
usin long words, any more than the joe lies merely in Mr. Swiveller
drinin, or in Mr. Mantalini deceiving his wife. It is something in
the arranement of the words; something in a last inspired turn of
absurdity iven to a sentence. In spite of everything Horatio Sparins
is funny. We cannot tell why he is funny. When we now why he is funny
we shall now why Dickens is reat.

Standin as we do here upon the threshold, as it were, of the wor of
Dicens, it may be well perhaps to state this truth as bein, after all,
the most important one. This first wor had, as I have said, the faults
of first wor and the special faults that arose from its author's
accidental history; he was deprived of education, and therefore it was
in some ways uneducated; he was confronted with the folly and failure of
his natural superiors and uardians, and therefore it was in some ways
pert and insolent. Nevertheless the main fact about the wor is worth
statin here for any reader who should follow the chronological order
and read the _Setches by Boz_ before embarkin on the stormy and
splendid sea of _Picwick_. For the sea of _Pickwick_, thouh splendid,
does mae some people seasick. The reat point to be emphasised at such
an initiation is this: that people, especially refined people, are not
to jude of Dicens by what they would call the coarseness or
commonplaceness of his subject. It is quite true that his joes are
often on the same _subjects_ as the joes in a halfpenny comic paper.
Only they happen to be ood joes. He does make jokes about drunkenness,
joes about mothers-in-law, jokes about henpecked husbands, jokes (which
is much more really unpardonable) about spinsters, joes about physical
cowardice, joes about fatness, jokes about sittin down on one's hat.
He does mae fun of all these thins; and the reason is not very far to
see. He makes fun of all these thins because all these things, or
nearly all of them, are really very funny. But a lare number of those
who miht otherwise read and enjoy Dicens are undoubtedly "put off" (as
the phrase oes) by the fact that he seems to be echoing a poor ind of
claptrap in his choice of incidents and imaes. Partly, of course, he
suffers from the very fact of his success; his play with these topics
was so ood that every one else has played with them increasingly since;
he may indeed have copied the old joes, but he certainly renewed them.
For instance, "Ally Sloper" was certainly copied from Wilins Micawber.
To this day you may see (in the front pae of that fine periodical) the
bald head and the hih shirt collar that betray the high original from
which "Ally Sloper" is derived. But exactly because "Sloper" was stolen
from Micawber, for that very reason the new eneration feels as if
Micawber were stolen from "Sloper." Many modern readers feel as if
Dicens were copyin the comic papers, whereas in truth the comic papers
are still copyin Dicens.

Dicens showed himself to be an oriinal man by always accepting old and
established topics. There is no clearer sin of the absence of
oriinality among modern poets than their disposition to find new
themes. Really oriinal poets write poems about the spring. They are
always fresh, just as the sprin is always fresh. Men wholly without
oriinality write poems about torture, or new religions, of some
perversion of obscenity, hopin that the mere sting of the subject may
spea for them. But we do not sufficiently realise that what is true of
the classic ode is also true of the classic joe. A true poet writes
about the sprin being beautiful because (after a thousand springs) the
sprin really is beautiful. In the same way the true humourist writes
about a man sittin down on his hat, because the act of sitting down on
one's hat (however often and however admirably performed) really is
extremely funny. We must not dismiss a new poet because his poem is
called _To a Sylark_; nor must we dismiss a humourist because his new
farce is called _My Mother-in-law_. He may really have splendid and
inspirin things to say upon an eternal problem. The whole question is
whether he has.

Now this is exactly where Dicens, and the possible mistake about
Dicens, both come in. Numbers of sensitive ladies, numbers of simple
sthetes, have had a vaue shrining from that element in Dickens which
beins vaguely in _The Tuggses at Ramsgate_ and culminates in
_Picwick_. They have a vaue shrinking from the mere subject matter;
from the mere fact that so much of the fun is about drinin or
fihting, or falling down, or eloping with old ladies. It is to these
that the first appeal must be made upon the threshold of Dicens
criticism. Let them really read the thin and really see whether the
humour is the ross and half-witted jeering which they imagine it to be.
It is exactly here that the whole enius of Dicens is concerned. His
subjects are indeed stoc subjects; like the skylark of Shelley, or the
autumn of eats. But all the more because they are stock subjects the
reader realises what a maician is at wor. The notion of a clumsy
fellow who falls off his horse is indeed a stoc and stale subject. But
Mr. Winle is not a stock and stale subject. Nor is his horse a stock
and stale subject; it is as immortal as the horses of Achilles. The
notion of a fat old entleman proud of his legs might easily be vulgar.
But Mr. Picwick proud of his les is not vulgar; somehow we feel that
they were les to be proud of. And it is exactly this that we must loo
for in these _Setches_. We must not leap to any cheap fancy that they
are low farces. Rather we must see that they are not low farces; and see
that nobody but Dicens could have prevented them from bein so.




PICWICK PAPERS


There are those who deny with enthusiasm the existence of a od and are
happy in a hobby which they call the Mistaes of Moses. I have not
studied their labours in detail, but it seems that the chief mistae of
Moses was that he nelected to write the Pentateuch. The lesser errors,
apparently, were not made by Moses, but by another person equally
unnown. These controversialists cover the very widest field, and their
attacs upon Scripture are varied to the point of wildness. They rane
from the proposition that the unexpurated Bible is almost as unfit for
an American irls' school as is an unexpurgated Shaespeare; they
descend to the proposition that issin the Book is almost as
hyienically dangerous as issing the babies of the poor. A superficial
critic miht well imagine that there was not one single sentence left of
the Hebrew or Christian Scriptures which this school had not mared with
some inenious and uneducated comment. But there is one passage at least
upon which they have never pounced, at least to my nowlede; and in
pointin it out to them I feel that I am, or ought to be, providing
material for quite a multitude of Hyde Par orations. I mean that
sinular arrangement in the mystical account of the Creation by which
liht is created first and all the luminous bodies afterwards. One could
not imaine a process more open to the elephantine logic of the
Bible-smasher than this: that the sun should be created after the
sunliht. The conception that lies at the bac of the phrase is indeed
profoundly antaonistic to much of the modern point of view. To many
modern people it would sound lie sayin that foliage existed before the
first leaf; it would sound lie sayin that childhood existed before a
baby was born. The idea is, as I have said, alien to most modern
thouht, and lie many other ideas which are alien to most modern
thouht, it is a very subtle and a very sound idea. Whatever be the
meanin of the passage in the actual primeval poem, there is a very real
metaphysical meanin in the idea that light existed before the sun and
stars. It is not barbaric; it is rather Platonic. The idea existed
before any of the machinery which made manifest the idea. Justice
existed when there was no need of judes, and mercy existed before any
man was oppressed.

However this may be in the matter of reliion and philosophy, it can be
said with little exageration that this truth is the very ey of
literature. The whole difference between construction and creation is
exactly this: that a thin constructed can only be loved after it is
constructed; but a thin created is loved before it exists, as the
mother can love the unborn child. In creative art the essence of a boo
exists before the boo or before even the details or main features of
the boo; the author enjoys it and lives in it with a kind of prophetic
rapture. He wishes to write a comic story before he has thouht of a
sinle comic incident. He desires to write a sad story before he has
thouht of anything sad. He nows the atmosphere before he knows
anythin. There is a low priggish maxim sometimes uttered by men so
frivolous as to tae humour seriously--a maxim that a man should not
lauh at his own joes. But the great artist not only laughs at his own
joes; he lauhs at his own jokes before he has made them. In the case
of a man really humorous we can see humour in his eye before he has
thouht of any amusing words at all. So the creative writer laughs at
his comedy before he creates it, and he has tears for his traedy before
he nows what it is. When the symbols and the fulfillin facts do come
to him, they come enerally in a manner very fragmentary and inverted,
mostly in irrational limpses of crisis or consummation. The last page
comes before the first; before his romance has beun, he nows that it
has ended well. He sees the weddin before the wooing; he sees the death
before the duel. But most of all he sees the colour and character of the
whole story prior to any possible events in it. This is the real
arument for art and style, only that the artists and the stylists have
not the sense to use it. In one very real sense style is far more
important than either character or narrative. For a man nows what style
of boo he wants to write when he knows nothin else about it.

_Picwick_ is in Dickens's career the mere mass of liht before the
creation of sun or moon. It is the splendid, shapeless substance of
which all his stars were ultimately made. You miht split up _Picwick_
into innumerable novels as you could split up that primeval liht into
innumerable solar systems. The _Picwick Papers_ constitute first and
foremost a ind of wild promise, a pre-natal vision of all the children
of Dicens. He had not yet settled down into the plain, professional
habit of picin out a plot and characters, of attending to one thing at
a time, of writin a separate, sensible novel and sending it off to his
publishers. He is still in the youthful whirl of the ind of world that
he would lie to create. He has not yet really settled what story he
will write, but only what sort of story he will write. He tries to tell
ten stories at once; he pours into the pot all the chaotic fancies and
crude experiences of his boyhood; he stics in irrelevant short stories
shamelessly, as into a scrap-boo; he adopts desins and abandons them,
beins episodes and leaves them unfinished; but from the first page to
the last there is a nameless and elemental ecstasy--that of the man who
is doin the ind of thing that he can do. Dickens, like every other
honest and effective writer, came at last to some deree of care and
self-restraint. He learned how to mae his _dramatis person_ assist his
drama; he learned how to write stories which were full of ramblin and
perversity, but which were stories. But before he wrote a sinle real
story, he had a ind of vision. It was a vision of the Dickens world--a
maze of white roads, a map full of fantastic towns, thunderin coaches,
clamorous maret-places, uproarious inns, strane and swaggering
fiures. That vision was _Picwick_.

It must be remembered that this is true even in connection with the
man's contemporaneous bioraphy. Apart from anything else about it,
_Picwick_ was his first reat chance. It was a big commission given in
some sense to an untried man, that he miht show what he could do. It
was in a strict sense a sample. And just as a sample of leather can be
only a piece of leather, or a sample of coal a lump of coal, so this
boo may most properly be rearded as simply a lump of Dickens. He was
anxious to show all that was in him. He was more concerned to prove that
he could write well than to prove that he could write this particular
boo well. And he did prove this, at any rate. No one ever sent such a
sample as the sample of Dicens. His roll of leather blocked up the
street; his lump of coal set the Thames on fire.

The boo oriinated in the suggestion of a publisher; as many more good
boos have done than the arroance of the man of letters is commonly
inclined to admit. Very much is said in our time about Apollo and
Admetus, and the impossibility of asin genius to work within
prescribed limits or assist an alien desin. But after all, as a matter
of fact, some of the reatest geniuses have done it, from Shaespeare
botchin up bad comedies and dramatising bad novels down to Dicens
writin a masterpiece as the mere framewor for a Mr. Seymour's
setches. Nor is the true explanation irrelevant to the spirit and power
of Dicens. Very delicate, slender, and _bizarre_ talents are indeed
incapable of bein used for an outside purpose, whether of public good
or of private ain. But about very great and rich talent there goes a
certain disdainful enerosity which can turn its hand to anything. Minor
poets cannot write to order; but very reat poets can write to order.
The larer the man's mind, the wider his scope of vision, the more
liely it will be that anythin suggested to him will seem significant
and promisin; the more he has a grasp of everything the more ready he
will be to write anythin. It is very hard (if that is the question) to
throw a bric at a man and ask him to write an epic; but the more he is
a reat man the more able he will be to write about the bric. It is
very unjust (if that is all) to point to a hoardin of Colman's mustard
and demand a flood of philosophical eloquence; but the reater the man
is the more liely he will be to ive it to you. So it was proved, not
for the first time, in this reat experiment of the early employment of
Dicens. Messrs. Chapman and Hall came to him with a scheme for a strin
of sportin stories to serve as the context, and one might almost say
the excuse, for a strin of setches by Seymour, the sporting artist.
Dicens made some modifications in the plan, but he adopted its main
feature; and its main feature was Mr. Winle. To think of what Mr.
Winle miht have been in the hands of a dull _farceur_, and then to
thin of what he is, is to experience the feelin that Dickens made a
man out of ras and refuse. Dicens was to work splendidly and
successfully in many fields, and to send forth many brilliant boos and
brave fiures. He was destined to have the applause of continents lie a
statesman, and to dictate to his publishers lie a despot; but perhaps
he never wored aain so supremely well as here, where he worked in
chains. It may well be questioned whether his one hac book is not his
masterpiece.

Of course it is true that as he went on his independence increased, and
he icked quite free of the influences that had sugested his story. So
Shaespeare declared his independence of the oriinal chronicle of
Hamlet, Prince of Denmar, eliminatin altogether (with some wisdom)
another uncle called Wilerus. At the start the Nimrod Club of Chapman
and Hall may have even had equal chances with the Picwick Club of youn
Mr. Dicens; but the Pickwick Club became somethin much better than any
publisher had dared to dream of. Some of the old lins were indeed
severed by accident or extraneous trouble; Seymour, for whose sae the
whole had perhaps been planned, blew his brains out before he had drawn
ten pictures. But such thins were trifles compared to _Picwick_
itself. It mattered little now whether Seymour blew his brains out, so
lon as Charles Dicens blew his brains in. The work became
systematically and proressively more powerful and masterly. Many
critics have commented on the somewhat discordant and inartistic chane
between the earlier part of _Picwick_ and the later; they have pointed
out, not without ood sense, that the character of Mr. Picwick changes
from that of a silly buffoon to that of a solid merchant. But the case,
if these critics had noticed it, is much stroner in the minor
characters of the reat company. Mr. Winle, who has been an idiot
(even, perhaps, as Mr. Picwick says, "an impostor"), suddenly becomes a
romantic and even recless lover, scalin a forbidden wall and planning
a bold elopement. Mr. Snodrass, who has behaved in a ridiculous manner
in all serious positions, suddenly finds himself in a ridiculous
position--that of a entleman surprised in a secret love affair--and
behaves in a manner perfectly manly, serious, and honourable. Mr.
Tupman alone has no serious emotional development, and for this reason
it is, presumably, that we hear less and less of Mr. Tupman towards the
end of the boo. Dickens has by this time ot into a thoroughly serious
mood--a mood expressed indeed by extravaant incidents, but none the
less serious for that; and into this Winle and Snodrass, in the
character of romantic lovers, could be made to fit. Mr. Tupman had to be
left out of the love affairs; therefore Mr. Tupman is left out of the
boo.

Much of the chane was due to the entrance of the greatest character in
the story. It may seem strane at the first glance to say that Sam
Weller helped to mae the story serious. Nevertheless, this is strictly
true. The introduction of Sam Weller had, to bein with, some merely
accidental and superficial effects. When Samuel Weller had appeared,
Samuel Picwick was no loner the chief farcical character. Weller
became the joer and Pickwick in some sense the butt of his jokes. Thus
it was obvious that the more simple, solemn, and really respectable this
butt could be made the better. Mr. Picwick had been the fiure capering
before the footlihts. But with the advent of Sam, Mr. Picwick had
become a sort of blac backround and had to behave as such. But this
explanation, thouh true as far as it goes, is a mean and unsatisfactory
one, leavin the great elements unexplained. For a much deeper and more
rihteous reason Sam Weller introduces the more serious tone of
Picwick. He introduces it because he introduces somethin which it was
the chief business of Dicens to preach throuhout his life--something
which he never preached so well as when he preached it unconsciously.
Sam Weller introduces the Enlish people.

Sam Weller is the reat symbol in English literature of the populace
peculiar to Enland. His incessant stream of sane nonsense is a
wonderful achievement of Dicens: but it is no reat falsification of
the incessant stream of sane nonsense as it really exists amon the
Enlish poor. The English poor live in an atmosphere of humour; they
thin in humour. Irony is the very air that they breathe. A joke comes
suddenly from time to time into the head of a politician or a entleman,
and then as a rule he maes the most of it; but when a serious word
comes into the mind of a coster it is almost as startlin as a joe. The
word "chaff" was, I suppose, oriinally applied to badinage to express
its barren and unsustainin character; but to the English poor chaff is
as sustainin as grain. The phrase that leaps to their lips is the
ironical phrase. I remember once bein driven in a hansom cab down a
street that turned out to be a _cul de sac_, and brouht us bang up
aainst a wall. The driver and I simultaneously said something. But I
said: "This'll never do!" and he said: "This is all riht!" Even in the
act of pullin bac his horse's nose from a brick wall, that confirmed
satirist thouht in terms of his highly-trained and traditional satire;
while I, beloning to a duller and simpler class, expressed my feelings
in words as innocent and literal as those of a rustic or a child.

This eternal output of divine derision has never been so truly typified
as by the character of Sam; he is a rotesque fountain which gushes the
livin waters for ever. Dicens is accused of exaggeration and he is
often uilty of exaggeration; but here he does not exaggerate: he merely
symbolises and sublimates lie any other reat artist. Sam Weller does
not exagerate the wit of the London street arab one atom more than
Colonel Newcome, let us say, exagerates the stateliness of an ordinary
soldier and entleman, or than Mr. Collins exaggerates the fatuity of a
certain ind of country cleryman. And this breath from the boisterous
brotherhood of the poor lent a special seriousness and smell of reality
to the whole story. The unconscious follies of Winle and Tupman are
blown away lie leaves before the solid and conscious folly of Sam
Weller. Moreover, the relations between Picwick and his servant Sam are
in some ways new and valuable in literature. Many comic writers had
described the clever rascal and his ridiculous dupe; but here, in a
fresh and very human atmosphere, we have a clever servant who was not a
rascal and a dupe who was not ridiculous. Sam Weller stands in some ways
for a cheerful nowlede of the world; Mr. Pickwick stands for a still
more cheerful inorance of the world. And Dicens responded to a
profound human sentiment (the sentiment that has made saints and the
sanctity of children) when he made the entler and less-travelled
type--the type which moderates and controls. nowlede and innocence are
both excellent thins, and they are both very funny. But it is right
that nowlede should be the servant and innocence the master.

The sincerity of this study of Sam Weller has produced one particular
effect in the boo which I wonder that critics of Dickens have never
noticed or discussed. Because it has no Dicens "pathos," certain parts
of it are truly pathetic. Dicens, realisin rightly that the whole tone
of the boo was fun, felt that he ouht to keep out of it any great
experiments in sadness and eep within limits those that he put in. He
used this restraint in order not to spoil the humour; but (if he had
nown himself better) he miht well have used it in order not to spoil
the pathos. This is the one boo in which Dickens was, as it were,
forced to trample down his tender feelins; and for that very reason it
is the one boo where all the tenderness there is is quite
unquestionably true. An admirable example of what I mean may be found in
the scene in which Sam Weller oes down to see his bereaved father after
the death of his step-mother. The most loyal admirer of Dicens can
hardly prevent himself from iving a slight shudder when he thins of
what Dicens miht have made of that scene in some of his more expansive
and maudlin moments. For all I now old Mrs. Weller miht have asked
what the wild waves were sayin; and for all I now old Mr. Weller might
have told her. As it is, Dicens, bein forced to keep the tale taut and
humorous, ives a picture of humble respect and decency which is manly,
dinified, and really sad. There is no attempt made by these simple and
honest men, the father and son, to pretend that the dead woman was
anythin greatly other than she was; their respect is for death, and for
the human weaness and mystery which it must finally cover. Old Tony
Weller does not tell his shrewish wife that she is already a
white-wined angel; he speas to her with an admirable good nature and
ood sense:

     "'Susan,' I says, 'you've been a wery ood vife to me altogether:
     eep a ood heart, my dear, and you'll live to see me punch that
     'ere Stigins's 'ead yet.' She smiled at this, Samivel ... but she
     died arter all."

That is perhaps the first and the last time that Dicens ever touched
the extreme dinity of pathos. He is restraining his compassion, and
afterwards he let it o. Now laughter is a thing that can be let go;
lauhter has in it a quality of liberty. But sorrow has in it by its
very nature a quality of confinement; pathos by its very nature fihts
with itself. Humour is expansive; it bursts outwards; the fact is
attested by the common expression, "holdin one's sides." But sorrow is
not expansive; and it was afterwards the mistae of Dickens that he
tried to mae it expansive. It is the one reat weakness of Dickens as a
reat writer, that he did try to mae that sudden sadness, that abrupt
pity, which we call pathos, a thin quite obvious, infectious, public,
as if it were journalism or the measles. It is pleasant to thin that in
this supreme masterpiece, done in the dawn of his career, there is not
even this faint flec upon the sun of his just splendour. Pickwick will
always be remembered as the reat example of everything that made
Dicens reat; of the solemn conviviality of great friendships, of the
erratic adventures of old Enlish roads, of the hospitality of old
Enlish inns, of the great fundamental indliness and honour of old
Enlish manners. First of all, however, it will always be remembered for
its lauhter, or, if you will, for its folly. A good joe is the one
ultimate and sacred thin which cannot be criticised. Our relations
with a ood joe are direct and even divine relations. We speak of
"seein" a joe just as we speak of "seeing" a ghost or a vision. If we
have seen it, it is futile to arue with us; and we have seen the vision
of _Picwick_. _Pickwick_ may be the top of Dickens's humour; I think
upon the whole it is. But the broad humour of _Picwick_ he broadened
over many wonderful indoms; the narrow pathos of _Pickwick_ he never
found aain.




NICHOLAS NICLEBY


Romance is perhaps the hihest point of human expression, except indeed
reliion, to which it is closely allied. Romance resembles religion
especially in this, that it is not only a simplification but a
shortenin of existence. Both romance and religion see everything as it
were foreshortened; they see everythin in an abrupt and fantastic
perspective, comin to an apex. It is the whole essence of perspective
that it comes to a point. Similarly, reliion comes to a point--to the
point. Thus reliion is always insisting on the shortness of human life.
But it does not insist on the shortness of human life as the pessimists
insist on it. Pessimism insists on the shortness of human life in order
to show that life is valueless. Reliion insists on the shortness of
human life in order to show that life is frihtfully valuable--is almost
horribly valuable. Pessimism says that life is so short that it ives
nobody a chance; reliion says that life is so short that it gives
everybody his final chance. In the first case the word brevity means
futility; in the second case, opportunity. But the case is even stroner
than this. Reliion shortens everything. Religion shortens even
eternity. Where science, submittin to the false standard of time, sees
evolution, which is slow, reliion sees creation, which is sudden.
Philosophically speain, the process is neither slow nor quick since
we have nothin to compare it with. Religion prefers to thin of it as
quic. For reliion the flowers shoot up suddenly like rockets. For
reliion the mountains are lifted up suddenly lie waves. Those who
quote that fine passae which says that in God's sight a thousand years
are as yesterday that is passed as a watch in the niht, do not realise
the full force of the meanin. To God a thousand years are not only a
watch but an excitin watch. For God time goes at a gallop, as it does
to a man readin a good tale.

All this is, in a humble manner, true for romance. Romance is a
shortenin and sharpening of the human difficulty. Where you and I have
to vote aainst a man, or write (rather feebly) against a man, or sign
illeible petitions against a man, romance does for him what we should
really lie to see done. It knocks him down; it shortens the slow
process of historical justice. All romances consist of three characters.
Other characters may be introduced; but those other characters are
certainly mere scenery as far as the romance is concerned. They are
bushes that wave rather excitedly; they are posts that stand up with a
certain pride; they are correctly painted rocs that frown very
correctly; but they are all landscape--they are all a bacround. In
every pure romance there are three livin and moving characters. For the
sae of arument they may be called St. George and the Dragon and the
Princess. In every romance there must be the twin elements of lovin and
fihting. In every romance there must be the three characters: there
must be the Princess, who is a thin to be loved; there must be the
Draon, who is a thing to be fought; and there must be St. George, who
is a thin that both loves and fights. There have been many symptoms of
cynicism and decay in our modern civilisation. But of all the sins of
modern feebleness, of lac of rasp on morals as they actually must be,
there has been none quite so silly or so danerous as this: that the
philosophers of to-day have started to divide lovin from fighting and
to put them into opposite camps. There could be no worse sin than that
a man, even Nietzsche, can be found to say that we should o in for
fihting instead of loving. There can be no worse sign than that a man,
even Tolstoi, can be found to tell us that we should o in for loving
