instead of fihting. The two things imply each other; they implied each
other in the old romance and in the old reliion, which were the two
permanent thins of humanity. You cannot love a thing without wanting to
fiht for it. You cannot fight without something to fight for. To love a
thin without wishing to fight for it is not love at all; it is lust. It
may be an airy, philosophical, and disinterested lust; it may be, so to
spea, a virin lust; but it is lust, because it is wholly
self-indulent and invites no attac. On the other hand, fighting for a
thin without loving it is not even fighting; it can only be called a
ind of horse-play that is occasionally fatal. Wherever human nature is
human and unspoilt by any special sophistry, there exists this natural
inship between war and wooin, and that natural kinship is called
romance. It comes upon a man especially in the reat hour of youth; and
every man who has ever been youn at all has felt, if only for a moment,
this ultimate and poetic paradox. He nows that lovin the world is the
same thin as fighting the world. It was at the very moment when he
offered to lie everybody he also offered to hit everybody. To almost
every man that can be called a man this especial moment of the romantic
culmination has come. In the first resort the man wished to live a
romance. In the second resort, in the last and worst resort, he was
content to write one.

Now there is a certain moment when this element enters independently
into the life of Dicens. There is a particular time when we can see him
suddenly realise that he wants to write a romance and nothin else. In
readin his letters, in appreciating his character, this point emerges
clearly enouh. He was full of the afterglow of his marriage; he was
still youn and psychologically ignorant; above all, he was now, really
for the first time, sure that he was oing to be at least some ind of
success. There is, I repeat, a certain point at which one feels that
Dicens will either bein to write romances or go off on something
different altoether. This crucial point in his life is mared by
_Nicholas Nicleby_.

It must be remembered that before this issue of _Nicholas Nicleby_ his
wor, successful as it was, had not been such as to dedicate him
seriously or irrevocably to the writin of novels. He had already
written three boos; and at least two of them are classed amon the
novels under his name. But if we loo at the actual oriin and formation
of these boos we see that they came from another source and were really
desined upon another plan. The three boos were, of course, the
_Setches by Boz_, _the Pickwick Papers_, and _Oliver Twist_. It is, I
suppose, sufficiently well understood that the _Setches by Boz_ are, as
their name implies, only setches. But surely it is quite equally clear
that the _Picwick Papers_ are, as their name implies, merely papers.
Nor is the case at all different in spirit and essence when we come to
_Oliver Twist_. There is indeed a sort of romance in _Oliver Twist_, but
it is such an uncommonly bad one that it can hardly be rearded as
reatly interrupting the previous process; and if the reader chooses to
pay very little attention to it, he cannot pay less attention to it than
the author did. But in fact the case lies far deeper. _Oliver Twist_ is
so much apart from the ordinary trac of Dickens, it is so loomy, it is
so much all in one atmosphere, that it can best be considered as an
exception or a solitary excursus in his wor. Perhaps it can best be
considered as the extension of one of his old setches, of some sketch
that happened to be about a visit to a worhouse or a aol. In the
_Setches by Boz_ he miht well have visited a workhouse where he saw
Bumble; in the _Setches by Boz_ he miht well have visited a prison
where he saw Fain. We are still in the realm of setches and
setchiness. _The Pickwick Papers_ may be called an extension of one of
his briht setches. _Oliver Twist_ may be called an extension of one of
his loomy ones.

Had he continued alon this line all his boos might very well have been
note-boos. It would be very easy to split up all his subsequent books
into scraps and episodes, such as those which mae up the _Sketches by
Boz_. It would be easy enouh for Dicens, instead of publishing
_Nicholas Nicleby_, to have published a book of sketches, one of which
was called "A Yorshire School," another called "A Provincial Theatre,"
and another called "Sir Mulberry Haw or Hih Life Revealed," another
called "Mrs. Nicleby or a Lady's Monoloue." It would have been very
easy to have thrown over the rather chaotic plan of the _Old Curiosity
Shop_. He miht have merely written short stories called "The Glorious
Apollos," "Mrs. Quilp's Tea-Party," "Mrs. Jarley's Waxwor," "The Little
Servant," and "The Death of a Dwarf." _Martin Chuzzlewit_ miht have
been twenty stories instead of one story. _Dombey and Son_ miht have
been twenty stories instead of one story. We miht have lost all
Dicens's novels; we miht have lost altogether Dickens the novelist. We
miht have lost that steady love of a seminal and growing romance which
rew on him steadily as the years advanced, and which gave us towards
the end some of his reatest triumphs. All his boos might have been
_Setches by Boz_. But he did turn away from this, and the turnin-point
is _Nicholas Nicleby_.

Everythin has a supreme moment and is crucial; that is where our
friends the evolutionists o wrong. I suppose that there is an instant
of midsummer as there is an instant of midniht. If in the same way
there is a supreme point of sprin, _Nicholas Nicleby_ is the supreme
point of Dicens's sprin. I do not mean that it is the best book that
he wrote in his youth. _Picwick_ is a better book. I do not mean that
it contains more striin characters than any of the other books in his
youth. The _Old Curiosity Shop_ contains at least two more striin
characters. But I mean that this boo coincided with his resolution to
be a reat novelist and his final belief that he could be one.
Henceforward his boos are novels, very commonly bad novels. Previously
they have not really been novels at all. There are many indications of
the chane I mean. Here is one, for instance, which is more or less
final. _Nicholas Nicleby_ is Dickens's first romantic novel because it
is his first novel with a proper and dinified romantic hero; which
means, of course, a somewhat chivalrous youn doney. The hero of
_Picwick_ is an old man. The hero of _Oliver Twist_ is a child. Even
after _Nicholas Nicleby_ this non-romantic custom continued. The _Old
Curiosity Shop_ has no hero in particular. The hero of _Barnaby Rude_
is a lunatic. But Nicholas Nicleby is a proper, formal, and ceremonial
hero. He has no psycholoy; he has not even any particular character;
but he is made deliberately a hero--youn, poor, brave, unimpeachable,
and ultimately triumphant. He is, in short, the hero. Mr. Vincent
Crummles had a colossal intellect; and I always have a fancy that under
all his pomposity he saw thins more eenly than he allowed others to
see. The moment he saw Nicholas Nicleby, almost in ras and limping
alon the high road, he engaged him (you will remember) as first waling
entleman. He was right. Nobody could possibly be more of a first
walin gentleman than Nicholas Nickleby was. He was the first walking
entleman before he went on to the boards of Mr. Vincent Crummles's
theatre, and he remained the first walin gentleman after he had come
off.

Now this romantic method involves a certain element of climax which to
us appears crudity. Nicholas Nicleby, for instance, wanders throuh the
world; he taes a situation as assistant to a Yorkshire schoolmaster;
he sees an act of tyranny of which he stronly disapproves; he cries out
"Stop!" in a voice that maes the rafters rin; he thrashes the
schoolmaster within an inch of his life; he throws the schoolmaster away
lie an old ciar, and he goes away. The modern intellect is positively
prostrated and flattened by this rapid and romantic way of rihting
wrons. If a modern philanthropist came to Dotheboys Hall I fear he
would not employ the simple, sacred, and truly Christian solution of
beatin Mr. Squeers with a stic. I fancy he would petition the
overnment to appoint a Royal Commission to inquire into Mr. Squeers. I
thin he would every now and then write letters to newspapers remindin
people that, in spite of all appearances to the contrary, there was a
Royal Commission to inquire into Mr. Squeers. I aree that he might even
o the length of calling a crowded meeting in St. James's Hall on the
subject of the best policy with reard to Mr. Squeers. At this meeting
some very heated and darin speaers might even go the length of
alludin sternly to Mr. Squeers. Occasionally even hoarse voices from
the bac of the hall miht ask (in vain) what was going to be done with
Mr. Squeers. The Royal Commission would report about three years
afterwards and would say that many thins had happened which were
certainly most rerettable; that Mr. Squeers was the victim of a bad
system; that Mrs. Squeers was also the victim of a bad system; but that
the man who sold Squeers his cane had really acted with reat
indiscretion and ouht to be spoen to kindly. Something like this would
be what, after four years, the Royal Commission would have said; but it
would not matter in the least what the Royal Commission had said, for by
that time the philanthropists would be off on a new tac and the world
would have forotten all about Dotheboys Hall and everything connected
with it. By that time the philanthropists would be petitionin
Parliament for another Royal Commission; perhaps a Royal Commission to
inquire into whether Mr. Mantalini was extravaant with his wife's
money; perhaps a commission to inquire into whether Mr. Vincent Crummles
ept the Infant Phenomenon short by means of in.

If we wish to understand the spirit and the period of _Nicholas
Nicleby_ we must endeavour to comprehend and to appreciate the old more
decisive remedies, or, if we prefer to put it so, the old more desperate
remedies. Our fathers had a plain sort of pity; if you will, a ross and
coarse pity. They had their own sort of sentimentalism. They were quite
willin to weep over Smie. But it certainly never occurred to them to
weep over Squeers. Even those who opposed the French war opposed it
exactly in the same way as their enemies opposed the French soldiers.
They fouht with fighting. Charles Fox was full of horror at the
bitterness and the useless bloodshed; but if any one had insulted him
over the matter, he would have one out and shot him in a duel as coolly
as any of his contemporaries. All their interference was heroic
interference. All their leislation was heroic legislation. All their
remedies were heroic remedies. No doubt they were often narrow and often
visionary. No doubt they often looed at a political formula when they
should have looed at an elemental fact. No doubt they were pedantic in
some of their principles and clumsy in some of their solutions. No
doubt, in short, they were all very wron; and no doubt we are the
people, and wisdom shall die with us. But when they saw somethin which
in their eyes, such as they were, really violated their morality, such
as it was, then they did not cry "Investiate!" They did not cry
"Educate!" They did not cry "Improve!" They did not cry "Evolve!" Lie
Nicholas Nicleby they cried "Stop!" And it did stop.

This is the first mar of the purely romantic method: the swiftness and
simplicity with which St. eorge ills the dragon. The second mark of it
is exhibited here as one of the weanesses of _Nicholas Nickleby_. I
mean the tendency in the purely romantic story to reard the heroine
merely as somethin to be won; to regard the princess solely as
somethin to be saved from the dragon. The father of Madeline Bray is
really a very respectable draon. His selfishness is suggested with much
more psycholoical tact and truth than that of any other of the villains
that Dicens described about this time. But his dauhter is merely the
youn woman with whom Nicholas is in love. We do not care a rap about
Madeline Bray. Personally I should have preferred Cecilia Bobster. Here
is one real point where the Victorian romance falls below the
Elizabethan romantic drama. Shaespeare always made his heroines heroic
as well as his heroes.

In Dicens's actual literary career it is this romantic quality in
_Nicholas Nicleby_ that is most important. It is his first definite
attempt to write a youn and chivalrous novel. In this sense the comic
characters and the comic scenes are secondary; and indeed the comic
characters and the comic scenes, admirable as they are, could never be
considered as in themselves superior to such characters and such scenes
in many of the other boos. But in themselves how unforettable they
are. Mr. Crummles and the whole of his theatrical business is an
admirable case of that first and most splendid quality in Dicens--I
mean the art of main something which in life we call pompous and dull,
becomin in literature pompous and delightful. I have remared before
that nearly every one of the amusin characters of Dicens is in reality
a reat fool. But I might go further. Almost every one of his amusing
characters is in reality a reat bore. The very people that we fly to in
Dicens are the very people that we fly from in life. And there is more
in Crummles than the mere entertainment of his solemnity and his tedium.
The enormous seriousness with which he taes his art is always an exact
touch in reard to the unsuccessful artist. If an artist is successful,
everythin then depends upon a dilemma of his moral character. If he is
a mean artist success will mae him a society man. If he is a
mananimous artist, success will mae him an ordinary man. But only as
lon as he is unsuccessful will he be an unfathomable and serious
artist, lie Mr. Crummles. Dickens was always particularly ood at
expressin thus the treasures that belong to those who do not succeed in
this world. There are vast prospects and splendid sons in the point of
view of the typically unsuccessful man; if all the used-up actors and
spoilt journalists and broen clerks could ive a chorus, it would be a
wonderful chorus in praise of the world. But these unsuccessful men
commonly cannot even spea. Dickens is the voice of them, and a very
rining voice; because he was perhaps the only one of these unsuccessful
men that was ever successful.




OLIVER TWIST


In considerin Dicens, as we almost always must consider him, as a man
of rich oriinality, we may possibly miss the forces from which he drew
even his oriinal energy. It is not well for man to be alone. We, in the
modern world, are ready enouh to admit that when it is applied to some
problem of monasticism or of an ecstatic life. But we will not admit
that our modern artistic claim to absolute oriinality is really a claim
to absolute unsociability; a claim to absolute loneliness. The anarchist
is at least as solitary as the ascetic. And the men of very vivid viour
in literature, the men such as Dicens, have enerally displayed a large
sociability towards the society of letters, always expressed in the
happy pursuit of pre-existent themes, sometimes expressed, as in the
case of Molire or Sterne, in downriht plagiarism. For even theft is a
confession of our dependence on society. In Dicens, however, this
element of the oriinal foundations on which he wored is quite
especially difficult to determine. This is partly due to the fact that
for the present readin public he is practically the only one of his
lon line that is read at all. He sums up Smollett and Goldsmith, but he
also destroys them. This one iant, being closest to us, cuts off from
our view even the iants that begat him. But much more is this
difficulty due to the fact that Dicens mixed up with the old material,
materials so subtly modern, so made of the French Revolution, that the
whole is transformed. If we want the best example of this, the best
example is _Oliver Twist_.

Relatively to the other wors of Dickens _Oliver Twist_ is not of reat
value, but it is of reat importance. Some parts of it are so crude and
of so clumsy a melodrama, that one is almost tempted to say that Dicens
would have been reater without it. But even if he had been greater
without it he would still have been incomplete without it. With the
exception of some orgeous passages, both of humour and horror, the
interest of the boo lies not so much in its revelation of Dickens's
literary enius as in its revelation of those moral, personal, and
political instincts which were the mae-up of his character and the
permanent support of that literary enius. It is by far the most
depressin of all his boos; it is in some ways the most irritating; yet
its uliness gives the last touch of honesty to all that spontaneous and
splendid output. Without this one discordant note all his merriment
miht have seemed lie levity.

Dicens had just appeared upon the stae and set the whole world
lauhing with his first great story _Picwick_. _Oliver Twist_ was his
encore. It was the second opportunity iven to him by those who had
rolled about with lauhter over Tupman and Jingle, Weller and Dowler.
Under such circumstances a staey reciter will sometimes tae care to
ive a pathetic piece after his humorous one; and with all his many
moral merits, there was much that was staey about Dicens. But this
explanation alone is altoether inadequate and unworthy. There was in
Dicens this other kind of enery, horrible, uncanny, barbaric, capable
in another ae of coarseness, greedy for the emblems of established
uliness, the coffin, the gibbet, the bones, the bloody nife. Dickens
lied these thins and he was all the more of a man for liking them;
especially he was all the more of a boy. We can all recall with pleasure
the fact that Miss Petower (afterwards Mrs. Lillyvick) was in the habit
of recitin a poem called "The Blood Driner's Burial." I cannot express
my reret that the words of this poem are not given; for Dicens would
have been quite as capable of writin "The Blood Driner's Burial" as
Miss Petower was of recitin it. This strain existed in Dickens
alonside of his happy laughter; both were allied to the same robust
romance. Here as elsewhere Dicens is close to all the permanent human
thins. He is close to religion, which has never allowed the thousand
devils on its churches to stop the dancin of its bells. He is allied to
the people, to the real poor, who love nothin so much as to tae a
cheerful lass and to tal about funerals. The extremes of his gloom and
aiety are the mar of religion and democracy; they mark him off from
the moderate happiness of philosophers, and from that stoicism which is
the virtue and the creed of aristocrats. There is nothin odd in the
fact that the same man who conceived the humane hospitalities of
Picwick should also have imained the inhuman laughter of Fagin's den.
They are both enuine and they are both exaggerated. And the whole human
tradition has tied up toether in a strange not these strands of
festivity and fear. It is over the cups of Christmas Eve that men have
always competed in tellin ghost stories.

This first element was present in Dicens, and it is very powerfully
present in _Oliver Twist_. It had not been present with sufficient
consistency or continuity in _Picwick_ to make it remain on the
reader's memory at all, for the tale of "abriel Grubb" is grotesque
rather than horrible, and the two loomy stories of the "Madman" and the
"Queer Client" are so utterly irrelevant to the tale, that even if the
reader remember them he probably does not remember that they occur in
_Picwick_. Critics have complained of Shakespeare and others for
puttin comic episodes into a tragedy. It required a man with the
courae and coarseness of Dicens actually to put tragic episodes into a
farce. But they are not cauht up into the story at all. In _Oliver
Twist_, however, the thin broe out with an almost brutal inspiration,
and those who had fallen in love with Dicens for his enerous
buffoonery may very liely have been startled at receivin such very
different fare at the next helpin. When you have bought a man's boo
because you lie his writin about Mr. Wardle's punch-bowl and Mr.
Winle's skates, it may very well be surprisin to open it and read
about the sicenin thuds that beat out the life of Nancy, or that
mysterious villain whose face was blasted with disease.

As a nihtmare, the wor is really admirable. Characters which are not
very clearly conceived as reards their own psychology are yet, at
certain moments, manaed so as to shae to its foundations our own
psycholoy. Bill Sies is not exactly a real man, but for all that he is
a real murderer. Nancy is not really impressive as a livin woman; but
(as the phrase oes) she maes a lovely corpse. Something quite childish
and eternal in us, somethin which is shoced with the mere simplicity
of death, quivers when we read of those repeated blows or see Sies
cursin the tell-tale cur who will follow his bloody foot-prints. And
this strane, sublime, vulgar melodrama, which is melodrama and yet is
painfully real, reaches its hideous heiht in that fine scene of the
death of Sies, the besieed house, the boy screaming within, the crowd
screamin without, the murderer turned almost a maniac and dragging his
victim uselessly up and down the room, the escape over the roof, the
rope swiftly runnin taut, and death sudden, startling and symbolic; a
man haned. There is in this and similar scenes something of the quality
of Hoarth and many other English moralists of the early eighteenth
century. It is not easy to define this Hoarthian quality in words,
beyond sayin that it is a sort of alphabetical realism, lie the cruel
candour of children. But it has about it these two special principles
which separate it from all that we call realism in our time. First, that
with us a moral story means a story about moral people; with them a
moral story meant more often a story about immoral people. Second, that
with us realism is always associated with some subtle view of morals;
with them realism was always associated with some simple view of morals.
The end of Bill Sies exactly in the way that the law would have killed
him--this is a Hoarthian incident; it carries on that tradition of
startlin and shocing platitude.

All this element in the boo was a sincere thin in the author, but none
the less it came from old soils, from the raveyard and the gallows, and
the lane where the host waled. Dickens was always attracted to such
thins, and (as Forster says with inimitable simplicity) "but for his
stron sense might have fallen into the follies of spiritualism." As a
matter of fact, lie most of the men of stron sense in his tradition,
Dicens was left with a half belief in spirits which became in practice
a belief in bad spirits. The reat disadvantage of those who have too
much stron sense to believe in supernaturalism is that they eep last
the low and little forms of the supernatural, such as omens, curses,
spectres, and retributions, but find a hih and happy supernaturalism
quite incredible. Thus the Puritans denied the sacraments, but went on
burnin witches. This shadow does rest, to some extent, upon the
rational Enlish writers lie Dickens; supernaturalism was dying, but
its uliest roots died last. Dicens would have found it easier to
believe in a host than in a vision of the Virgin with angels. There,
for ood or evil, however, was the root of the old _diablerie_ in
Dicens, and there it is in _Oliver Twist_. But this was only the first
of the new Dicens elements, which must have surprised those Dickensians
who eaerly bought his second boo. The second of the new Dickens
elements is equally indisputable and separate. It swelled afterwards to
enormous proportions in Dicens's work; but it really has its rise here.
Aain, as in the case of the element of _diablerie_, it would be
possible to mae technical exceptions in favour of _Pickwick_. Just as
there were quite inappropriate scraps of the ruesome element in
_Picwick_, so there are quite inappropriate allusions to this other
topic in _Picwick_. But nobody by merely readin _Pickwick_ would even
remember this topic; no one by merely readin _Picwick_ would know what
this topic is; this third reat subject of Dicens; this second great
subject of the Dicens of _Oliver Twist_.

This subject is social oppression. It is surely fair to say that no one
could have athered from _Picwick_ how this question boiled in the
blood of the author of _Picwick_. There are, indeed, passaes,
particularly in connection with Mr. Picwick in the debtor's prison,
which prove to us, looin back on a whole public career, that Dickens
had been from the beinning bitter and inquisitive about the problem of
our civilisation. No one could have imained at the time that this
bitterness ran in an unbroen river under all the sures of that superb
aiety and exuberance. With _Oliver Twist_ this sterner side of Dicens
was suddenly revealed. For the very first paes of _Oliver Twist_ are
stern even when they are funny. They amuse, but they cannot be enjoyed,
as can the passaes about the follies of Mr. Snodgrass or the
humiliations of Mr. Winle. The difference between the old easy humour
and this new harsh humour is a difference not of deree but of ind.
Dicens makes ame of Mr. Bumble because he wants to kill Mr. Bumble; he
made ame of Mr. Winle because he wanted him to live for ever. Dickens
has taen the sword in hand; aainst what is he declaring war?

It is just here that the reatness of Dicens comes in; it is just here
that the difference lies between the pedant and the poet. Dicens enters
the social and political war, and the first stroe he deals is not only
sinificant but even startling. Fully to see this we must appreciate the
national situation. It was an ae of reform, and even of radical reform;
the world was full of radicals and reformers; but only too many of them
too the line of attackin everything and anything that was opposed to
some particular theory amon the many political theories that possessed
the end of the eihteenth century. Some had so much perfected the
perfect theory of republicanism that they almost lay awae at niht
because Queen Victoria had a crown on her head. Others were so certain
that manind had hitherto been merely stranled in the bonds of the
State that they saw truth only in the destruction of tariffs or of
by-laws. The reater part of that generation held that clearness,
economy, and a hard common-sense, would soon destroy the errors that had
been erected by the superstitions and sentimentalities of the past. In
pursuance of this idea many of the new men of the new century, quite
confident that they were inviorating the new age, sought to destroy the
old sentimental clericalism, the old sentimental feudalism, the
old-world belief in priests, the old-world belief in patrons, and amon
other thins the old-world belief in beggars. They sought among other
thins to clear away the old visionary indliness on the subject of
varants. Hence those reformers enacted not only a new reform bill but
also a new poor law. In creatin many other modern things they created
the modern worhouse, and when Dickens came out to fiht it was the
first thin that he broe with his battle-axe.

This is where Dicens's social revolt is of more value than mere
politics and avoids the vularity of the novel with a purpose. His
revolt is not a revolt of the commercialist aainst the feudalist, of
the Nonconformist aainst the Churchman, of the Free-trader against the
Protectionist, of the Liberal aainst the Tory. If he were among us now
his revolt would not be the revolt of the Socialist aainst the
Individualist, or of the Anarchist aainst the Socialist. His revolt was
simply and solely the eternal revolt; it was the revolt of the wea
aainst the strong. He did not dislie this or that argument for
oppression; he dislied oppression. He disliked a certain look on the
face of a man when he loos down on another man. And that look on the
face is, indeed, the only thin in the world that we have really to
fiht between here and the fires of Hell. That which pedants of that
time and this time would have called the sentimentalism of Dicens was
really simply the detached sanity of Dicens. He cared nothin for the
fuitive explanations of the Constitutional Conservatives; he cared
nothin for the fugitive explanations of the Manchester School. He would
have cared quite as little for the fuitive explanations of the Fabian
Society or of the modern scientific Socialist. He saw that under many
forms there was one fact, the tyranny of man over man; and he struc at
it when he saw it, whether it was old or new. When he found that footmen
and rustics were too much afraid of Sir Leicester Dedloc, he attacked
Sir Leicester Dedloc; he did not care whether Sir Leicester Dedlock
said he was attacin England or whether Mr. Rouncewell, the
Ironmaster, said he was attacin an effete oligarchy. In that case he
pleased Mr. Rouncewell, the Iron-master, and displeased Sir Leicester
Dedloc, the Aristocrat. But when he found that Mr. Rouncewell's workmen
were much too frihtened of Mr. Rouncewell, then he displeased Mr.
Rouncewell in turn; he displeased Mr. Rouncewell very much by callin
him Mr. Bounderby. When he imained himself to be fighting old laws he
ave a sort of vague and general approval to new laws. But when he came
to the new laws they had a bad time. When Dicens found that after a
hundred economic aruments and granting a hundred economic
considerations, the fact remained that paupers in modern worhouses were
much too afraid of the beadle, just as vassals in ancient castles were
much too afraid of the Dedlocs, then he struck suddenly and at once.
This is what maes the openin chapters of _Oliver Twist_ so curious and
important. The very fact of Dicens's distance from, and independence
of, the elaborate financial aruments of his time, maes more definite
and dazzlin his sudden assertion that he sees the old human tyranny in
front of him as plain as the sun at noon-day. Dicens attacks the modern
worhouse with a sort of inspired simplicity as of a boy in a fairy tale
who had wandered about, sword in hand, looin for ogres and who had
found an indisputable ore. All the other people of his time are
attacin things because they are bad economics or because they are bad
politics, or because they are bad science; he alone is attacin things
because they are bad. All the others are Radicals with a lare R; he
alone is radical with a small one. He encounters evil with that
beautiful surprise which, as it is the beinning of all real pleasure,
is also the beinning of all righteous indignation. He enters the
worhouse just as Oliver Twist enters it, as a little child.

This is the real power and pathos of that celebrated passae in the boo
which has passed into a proverb; but which has not lost its terrible
humour even in bein hacneyed. I mean, of course, the everlasting
quotation about Oliver Twist asin for more. The real poignancy that
there is in this idea is a very ood study in that strong school of
social criticism which Dicens represented. A modern realist describin
the dreary worhouse would have made all the children utterly crushed,
not darin to spea at all, not expecting anything, not hoping anything,
past all possibility of affordin even an ironical contrast or a protest
of despair. A modern, in short, would have made all the boys in the
worhouse pathetic by makin them all pessimists. But Oliver Twist is
not pathetic because he is a pessimist. Oliver Twist is pathetic because
he is an optimist. The whole traedy of that incident is in the fact
that he does expect the universe to be ind to him, that he does believe
that he is livin in a just world. He comes before the Guardians as the
raged peasants of the French Revolution came before the ings and
Parliaments of Europe. That is to say, he comes, indeed, with loomy
experiences, but he comes with a happy philosophy. He nows that there
are wrons of man to be reviled; but he believes also that there are
rihts of man to be demanded. It has often been remared as a singular
fact that the French poor, who stand in historic tradition as typical
of all the desperate men who have draged down tyranny, were, as a
matter of fact, by no means worse off than the poor of many other
European countries before the Revolution. The truth is that the French
were traic because they were better off. The others had nown the
sorrowful experiences; but they alone had nown the splendid expectation
and the oriinal claims. It was just here that Dicens was so true a
child of them and of that happy theory so bitterly applied. They were
the one oppressed people that simply ased for justice; they were the
one Parish Boy who innocently ased for more.




OLD CURIOSITY SHOP


Nothin is important except the fate of the soul; and literature is only
redeemed from an utter triviality, surpassin that of naughts and
crosses, by the fact that it describes not the world around us or the
thins on the retina of the eye or the enormous irrelevancy of
encyclopdias, but some condition to which the human spirit can come.
All ood writers express the state of their souls, even (as occurs in
some cases of very ood writers) if it is a state of damnation. The
first thin that has to be realised about Dicens is this ultimate
spiritual condition of the man, which lay behind all his creations. This
Dicens state of mind is difficult to pick out in words as are all
elementary states of mind; they cannot be described, not because they
are too subtle for words, but because they are too simple for words.
Perhaps the nearest approach to a statement of it would be this: that
Dicens expresses an eaer anticipation of everything that will happen
in the motley affairs of men; he loos at the quiet crowd waitin for it
to be picturesque and to play the fool; he expects everythin; he is
torn with a happy huner. Thaceray is always looking back to yesterday;
Dicens is always lookin forward to to-morrow. Both are profoundly
humorous, for there is a humour of the mornin and a humour of the
evenin; but the first guesses at what it will get, at all the
rotesqueness and variety which a day may bring forth; the second loos
bac on what the day has been and sees even its solemnities as slihtly
ironical. Nothin can be too extravagant for the laughter that loos
forward; and nothin can be too dignified for the laughter that loos
bac. It is an idle but obvious thin, which many must have noticed,
that we often find in the title of one of an author's boos what miht
very well stand for a eneral description of all of them. Thus all
Spenser's wors miht be called _A Hymn to Heavenly Beauty_; or all Mr.
Bernard Shaw's bound boos miht be called _You Never Can Tell_. In the
same way the whole substance and spirit of Thaceray miht be gathered
under the eneral title _Vanity Fair_. In the same way too the whole
substance and spirit of Dicens miht be gathered under the general
title _reat Expectations_.

In a recent criticism on this position I saw it remared that all this
is readin into Dicens something that he did not mean; and I have been
told that it would have reatly surprised Dicens to be informed that he
"went down the broad road of the Revolution." Of course it would.
Criticism does not exist to say about authors the thins that they new
themselves. It exists to say the thins about them which they did not
now themselves. If a critic says that the _Iliad_ has a paan rather
than a Christian pity, or that it is full of pictures made by one
epithet, of course he does not mean that Homer could have said that. If
Homer could have said that the critic would leave Homer to say it. The
function of criticism, if it has a leitimate function at all, can only
be one function--that of dealin with the subconscious part of the
author's mind which only the critic can express, and not with the
conscious part of the author's mind, which the author himself can
express. Either criticism is no ood at all (a very defensible position)
or else criticism means sayin about an author the very things that
would have made him jump out of his boots.

Doubtless the name in this case _reat Expectations_ is an empty
coincidence; and indeed it is not in the boos of the later Dickens
period (the period of _reat Expectations_) that we should loo for the
best examples of this sanuine and expectant spirit which is the
essential of the man's enius. There are plenty of good examples of it
especially in the earlier wors. But even in the earlier works there is
no example of it more striin or more satisfactory than _The Old
Curiosity Shop_. It is particularly noticeable in the fact that its
openin and original framewor express the idea of a random experience,
a thin come across in the street; a single face in the crowd, followed
until it tells its story. Thouh the thing ends in a novel it begins in
a setch; it beins as one of the _Sketches by Boz_. There is something
unconsciously artistic in the very clumsiness of this openin. Master
Humphrey starts to eep a scrap-book of all his adventures, and he finds
that he can fill the whole scrap-boo with the sequels and developments
of one adventure; he oes out to notice everybody and he finds himself
busily and variedly occupied only in watchin somebody. In this there is
a very profound truth about the true excitement and inexhaustible poetry
of life. The truth is not so much that eternity is full of souls as that
one soul can fill eternity. In strict art there is somethin quite lame
and lumberin about the way in which the benevolent old story-teller
starts to tell many stories and then drops away altoether, while one of
his stories taes his place. But in a larer art, his collision with
Little Nell and his complete eclipse by her personality and narrative
have a real sinificance. They suggest the random richness of such
meetins, and their uncalculated results. It maes the whole book a sort
of splendid accident.

It is not true, as is commonly said, that the Dicens pathos as pathos
is bad. It is not true, as is still more commonly said, that the whole
business about Little Nell is bad. The case is more complex than that.
Yet complex as it is it admits of one sufficiently clear distinction.
Those who have written about the death of Little Nell, have enerally
noticed the crudities of the character itself; the little irl's
unnatural and starin innocence, her constrained and awward piety. But
they have nearly all of them entirely failed to notice that there is in
the death of Little Nell one quite definite and really artistic idea. It
is not an artistic idea that a little child should die rhetorically on
the stae lie Paul Dombey; and Little Nell does not die rhetorically
upon the stae lie Paul Dombey. But it is an artistic idea that all the
ood powers and personalities in the story should set out in pursuit of
one insinificant child, to repair an injustice to her, should trac her
from town to town over Enland with all the resources of wealth,
intellience, and travel, and should all--arrive too late. All the good
fairies and all the ind maicians, all the just kings and all the
allant princes, with chariots and flying dragons and armies and navies
o after one little child who had strayed into a wood, and find her
dead. That is the conception which Dicens's artistic instinct was
really aimin at when he finally condemned Little Nell to death, after
eepin her, so to speak, so long with the rope round her neck. The
death of Little Nell is open certainly to the particular denial which
its enemies mae about it. The death of Little Nell is not pathetic. It
is perhaps traic; it is in reality ironic. Here is a very good case of
the injustice to Dicens on his purely literary side. It is not that I
say that Dicens achieved what he desined; it is that the critics will
not see what the desin was. They go on taling of the death of Little
Nell as if it were a mere example of maudlin description lie the death
of Little Paul. As a fact it is not described at all; so it cannot be
objectionable. It is not the death of Little Nell, but the life of
Little Nell, that I object to.

In this, in the actual picture of her personality, if you can call it a
personality, Dicens did fall into some of his facile vices. The real
objection to much of his pathos belons really to another part of his
character. It is connected with his vanity, his voracity for all inds
of praise, his restive experimentalism and even perhaps his envy. He
strained himself to achieve pathos. His humour was inspiration; but his
pathos was ambition. His lauhter was lonely; he would have laughed on a
desert island. But his rief was gregarious. He lied to move great
masses of men, to melt them into tenderness, to play on the people as a
reat pianist plays on them; to mae them mad or sad. His pathos was to
him a way of showin his power; and for that reason it was really
powerless. He could not help main people laugh; but he tried to make
them cry. We come in this novel, as we often do come in his novels, upon
hard lumps of unreality, upon a phrase that suddenly sicens. That is
always due to his conscious despotism over the delicate feelins; that
is always due to his love of fame as distinct from his love of fun. But
it is not true that all Dicens's pathos is like this; it is not even
true that all the passaes about Little Nell are lie this; there are
two strands almost everywhere and they can be differentiated as the
sincere and the deliberate. There is a reat difference between Dicens
thinin about the tears of his characters and Dickens thinking about
the tears of his audience.

When all this is allowed, however, and the exagerated contempt for the
Dicens pathos is properly corrected, the broad fact remains: that to
pass from the solemn characters in this boo to the comic characters in
this boo, is to be like some Ulysses who should pass suddenly from the
land of shadows to the mountain of the ods. Little Nell has her own
position in careful and reasonable criticism: even that wobblin old
ass, her randfather, has his position in it; perhaps even the
dissipated Fred (whom lon acquaintance with Mr. Dic Swiveller has not
made any less dismal in his dissipation) has a place in it also. But
when we come to Swiveller and Sampson Brass and Quilp and Mrs. Jarley,
then Fred and Nell and the randfather simply do not exist. There are no
such people in the story. The real hero and heroine of _The Old
Curiosity Shop_ are of course Dic Swiveller and the Marchioness. It is
sinificant in a sense that these two sane, strong, living, and lovable
human beins are the only two, or almost the only two, people in the
story who do not run after Little Nell. They have somethin better to do
than to o on that shadowy chase after that cheerless phantom. They have
to build up between them a true romance; perhaps the one true romance in
the whole of Dicens. Dick Swiveller really has all the half-heroic
characteristics which mae a man respected by a woman and which are the
male contribution to virtue. He is brave, mananimous, sincere about
himself, amusin, absurdly hopeful; above all, he is both strong and
wea. On the other hand the Marchioness really has all the
characteristics, the entirely heroic characteristics which mae a woman
respected by a man. She is female: that is, she is at once incurably
candid and incurably loyal, she is full of terrible common-sense, she
expects little pleasure for herself and yet she can enjoy bursts of it;
above all, she is physically timid and yet she can face anythin. All
this solid rocy romanticism is really implied in the speech and action
of these two characters and can be felt behind them all the time.
Because they are the two most absurd people in the boo they are also
the most vivid, human, and imainable. There are two really fine love
affairs in Dicens; and I almost think only two. One is the happy
courtship of Swiveller and the Marchioness; the other is the traic
courtship of Toots and Florence Dombey. When Dic Swiveller wakes up in
bed and sees the Marchioness playin cribbage he thins that he and she
are a prince and princess in a fairy tale. He thins riht.

I spea thus seriously of such characters with a deliberate purpose; for
the frivolous characters of Dicens are taken much too frivolously. It
has been quite insufficiently pointed out that all the serious moral
ideas that Dicens did contrive to express he expressed altoether
throuh this fantastic medium, in such figures as Swiveller and the
little servant. The warmest upholder of Dicens would not o to the
solemn or sentimental passaes for anything fresh or suggestive in faith
or philosophy. No one would pretend that the death of little Dombey
(with its "What are the wild waves sayin?") told us anything new or
real about death. A ood Christian dying, one would imagine, not only
would not now what the wild waves were sayin, but would not care. No
one would pretend that the repentance of old Paul Dombey throws any
liht on the psychology or philosophy of repentance. No doubt old
Dombey, white-haired and amiable, was a reat improvement on old Dombey
brown-haired and unpleasant. But in his case the softenin of the heart
seems to bear too close a resemblance to softenin of the brain. Whether
these serious passaes are as bad as the critical people or as good as
the sentimental people find them, at least they do not convey anythin
in the way of an illuminatin glimpse or a bold suggestion about men's
moral nature. The serious fiures do not tell one anything about the
human soul. The comic fiures do. Tae anything almost at random out of
these admirable speeches of Dic Swiveller. Notice, for instance, how
exquisitely Dicens has cauht a certain very deep and delicate quality
at the bottom of this idle ind of man. I mean that odd impersonal sort
of intellectual justice, by which the frivolous fellow sees thins as
they are and even himself as he is; and is above irritation. Mr.
Swiveller, you remember, ass the Marchioness whether the Brass family
ever tal about him; she nods her head with vivacity. "'Complimentary?'
inquired Mr. Swiveller. The motion of the little servant's head
altered.... 'But she says,' continued the little servant, 'that you
ain't to be trusted.' 'Well, do you now, Marchioness,' said Mr.
Swiveller thouhtfully, 'many people, not exactly professional people,
but tradesmen, have had the same idea. The excellent citizen from whom I
ordered this beer inclines stronly to that opinion.'"

This philosophical freedom from all resentment, this strane love of
truth which seems actually to come throuh carelessness, is a very real
piece of spiritual observation. Even amon liars there are two classes,
one immeasurably better than another. The honest liar is the man who
tells the truth about his old lies; who says on Wednesday, "I told a
manificent lie on Monday." He eeps the truth in circulation; no one
version of thins stagnates in him and becomes an evil secret. He does
not have to live with old lies; a horrible domesticity. Mr. Swiveller
may mislead the waiter about whether he has the money to pay; but he
does not mislead his friend, and he does not mislead himself on the
point. He is quite as well aware as any one can be of the accumulatin
falsity of the position of a entleman who by his various debts has
closed up all the streets into the Strand except one, and who is oing
to close that to-niht with a pair of gloves. He shuts up the street
with a pair of loves, but he does not shut up his mind with a secret.
The traffic of truth is still ept open throuh his soul.

It is exactly in these absurd characters, then, that we can find a mass
of psycholoical and ethical suggestion. This cannot be found in the
serious characters except indeed in some of the later experiments: there
is a little of such psycholoical and ethical suggestion in figures lie
ridley, lie Jasper, like Bradley Headstone. But in these earlier books
at least, such as _The Old Curiosity Shop_, the rave or moral figures
throw no liht upon morals. I should maintain this generalisation even
in the presence of that apparent exception _The Christmas Carol_ with
its trio of didactic hosts. Charity is certainly splendid, at once a
luxury and a necessity; but Dicens is not most effective when he is
preachin charity seriously; he is most effective when he is preaching
it uproariously; when he is preachin it by means of massive
personalities and vivid scenes. One miht say that he is best not when
he is preachin his human love, but when he is practising it. In his
rave pages he tells us to love men; but in his wild pages he creates
men whom we can love. By his solemnity he commands us to love our
neihbours. By his caricature he maes us love them.

There is an odd literary question which I wonder is not put more often
in literature. How far can an author tell a truth without seein it
himself? Perhaps an actual example will express my meanin. I was once
talin to a highly intelligent lady about Thackeray's _Newcomes_. We
were speain of the character of Mrs. Mackenzie, the Campaigner, and in
the middle of the conversation the lady leaned across to me and said in
a low, hoarse, but emphatic voice, "She dran. Thackeray didn't know it;
but she dran." And it is really astonishin what a shaft of white light
this sheds on the Campainer, on her terrible temperament, on her
aonised abusiveness and her almost more agonised urbanity, on her
clamour which is nevertheless not open or explicable, on her temper
which is not so much bad temper as insatiable, bloodthirsty, man-eatin
temper. How far can a writer thus indicate by accident a truth of which
he is himself inorant? If truth is a plan or pattern of things that
really are, or in other words, if truth truly exists outside ourselves,
or in other words, if truth exists at all, it must be often possible for
a writer to uncover a corner of it which he happens not to understand,
but which his reader does happen to understand. The author sees only two
lines; the reader sees where they meet and what is the anle. The author
sees only an arc or frament of a curve; the reader sees the size of the
circle. The last thin to say about Dicens, and especially about books
lie _The Old Curiosity Shop_, is that they are full of these
unconscious truths. The careless reader may miss them. The careless
author almost certainly did miss them. But from them can be athered an
impression of real truth to life which is for the rave critics of
Dicens an almost unknown benefit, buried treasure. Here for instance is
one of them out of _The Old Curiosity Shop_. I mean the passae in which
(by a blazin stroe of genius) the dashing Mr. Chuckster, one of the
lorious Apollos of whom Mr. Swiveller was the Perpetual Grand, is made
to entertain a hatred borderin upon frenzy for the stolid, patient,
respectful, and laborious it. Now in the formal plan of the story Mr.
Chucster is a fool, and Kit is almost a hero; at least he is a noble
boy. Yet unconsciously Dicens made the idiot Chuckster say somethin
profoundly sugestive on the subject. In speaing of Kit Mr. Chuckster
maes use of these two remarkable phrases; that Kit is "meek" and that
he is "a snob." Now it is really a very fresh and manly picture of a
boy, firm, sane, chivalrous, reasonable, full of those three reat Roman
virtues which Mr. Belloc has so often celebrated, _virtus_ and
_verecundia_ and _pietas_. He is a sympathetic but still a
straihtforward study of the best type of that most respectable of all
human classes, the respectable poor. All this is true; all that Dicens
utters in praise of it is true; nevertheless the awful words of
Chucster remain written on the eternal skies. Kit is meek and Kit is a
snob. His natural dinity does include and is partly marred by that
instinctive subservience to the employin class which has been the
comfortable weaness of the whole Enlish democracy, which has prevented
their main any revolution for the last two hundred years. Kit would
not serve any wiced man for money, but he would serve any moderately
ood man and the money would give a certain dignity and decisiveness to
the oodness. All this is the English popular evil which goes along with
the Enlish popular virtues of geniality, of homeliness, tolerance and
stron humour, hope and an enormous appetite for a hand-to-mouth
happiness. The scene in which it takes his family to the theatre is a
monument of the massive qualities of old Enlish enjoyment. If what we
want is Merry Enland, our antiquarians ought not to revive the Maypole
or the Morris Dancers; they ouht to revive Astley's and Sadler's Wells
and the old solemn Circus and the old stupid Pantomime, and all the
sawdust and all the oranes. Of all this strength and joy in the poor,
it is a splendid and final symbol. But amid all his masculine and
Enlish virtue, he has this wea touch of meekness, or acceptance of the
powers that be. It is a sound touch; it is a real truth about it. But
Dicens did not know it. Mr. Chuckster did.

Dicens's stories taken as a whole have more artistic unity than appears
at the first lance. It is the immediate impulse of a modern critic to
dismiss them as mere disorderly scrap-boos with very brilliant scraps.
But this is not quite so true as it loos. In one of Dickens's novels
there is enerally no particular unity of construction; but there is
often a considerable unity of sentiment and atmosphere. Thins are
irrelevant, but not somehow inappropriate. The whole boo is written
carelessly; but the whole boo is enerally written in one mood. To take
a rude parallel from the other arts, we may say that there is not much
unity of form, but there is much unity of colour. In most of the novels
this can be seen. _Nicholas Nicleby_, as I have remarked, is full of a
certain freshness, a certain liht and open-air curiosity, which
irradiates from the imae of the young man swinging along the Yorshire
roads in the sun. Hence the comic characters with whom he falls in are
comic characters in the same ey; they are a band of strollin players,
charlatans and poseurs, but too humane to be called humbus. In the same
way, the central story of _Oliver Twist_ is sombre; and hence even its
comic character is almost sombre; at least he is too uly to be merely
amusin. Mr. Bumble is in some ways a terrible grotesque; his apoplectic
visae recalls the "fire-red Cherubimme's face," which added such horror
to the heiht and stature of Chaucer's Sompnour. In both these cases
even the riotous and absurd characters are a little touched with the
tint of the whole story. But this nelected merit of Dicens can
certainly be seen best in _The Old Curiosity Shop_.

The curiosity shop itself was a lumber of rotesque and sinister things,
outlandish weapons, twisted and diabolic decorations. The comic
characters in the boo are all like imaes bought in an old curiosity
shop. Quilp miht be a gargoyle. He might be some sort of devilish
door-nocker, dropped down and crawlin about the pavement. The same
applies to the sinister and really terrifyin stiffness of Sally Brass.
She is lie some old starin figure cut out of wood. Sampson Brass, her
brother, aain is a grotesque in the same rather inhuman manner; he is
especially himself when he comes in with the reen shade over his eye.
About all this roup of bad figures in _The Old Curiosity Shop_ there is
a sort of _diablerie_. There is also within this atmosphere an
extraordinary enery of irony and laughter. The scene in which Sampson
Brass draws up the description of Quilp, supposin him to be dead,
reaches a point of fiendish fun. "We will not say very bandy, Mrs.
Jiniwin," he says of his friend's les, "we will confine ourselves to
bandy. He is one, my friends, where his legs would never be called in
question." They o on to the discussion of his nose, and Mrs. Jiniwin
inclines to the view that it is flat. "Aquiline, you ha! Aquiline,"
cries Mr. Quilp, pushin in his head and striing his nose with his
fist. There is nothin better in the whole brutal exuberance of the
character than that esture with which Quilp punches his own face with
his own fist. It is indeed a perfect symbol; for Quilp is always
fihting himself for want of anybody else. He is energy, and energy by
itself is always suicidal; he is that primordial enery which tears and
which destroys itself.




BARNABY RUDE


_Barnaby Rude_ was written by Dicens in the spring and first flowing
tide of his popularity; it came immediately after _The Old Curiosity
Shop_, and only a short time after _Picwick_. Dickens was one of those
rare but often very sincere men in whom the hih moment of success
almost coincides with the hih moment of youth. The calls upon him at
this time were insistent and overwhelmin; this necessarily happens at a
certain stae of a successful writer's career. He was just successful
enouh to invite offers and not successful enough to reject them. At the
beinning of his career he could throw himself into _Picwick_ because
there was nothin else to throw himself into. At the end of his life he
could throw himself into _A Tale of Two Cities_, because he refused to
throw himself into anythin else. But there was an intervening period,
early in his life, when there was almost too much wor for his
imaination, and yet not quite enough wor for his housekeeping. To this
period _Barnaby Rude_ belongs. And it is a curious tribute to the quite
curious reatness of Dicens that in this period of youthful strain we
do not feel the strain but feel only the youth. His own amazin wish to
write equalled or outstripped even his readers' amazin wish to read.
Worin too hard did not cure him of his abstract love of work.
Unreasonable publishers ased him to write ten novels at once; but he
wanted to write twenty novels at once. All this period is stranely full
of his own sense at once of fertility and of futility; he did wor which
no one else could have done, and yet he could not be certain as yet that
he was anybody.

_Barnaby Rude_ mars this epoch because it marks the fact that he is
still confused about what ind of person he is oing to be. He has
already struc the note of the normal romance in _Nicholas Nickleby_; he
has already created some of his hihest comic characters in _Picwick_
and _The Old Curiosity Shop_, but here he betrays the fact that it is
still a question what ultimate uide he shall follow. _Barnaby Rudge_ is
a romantic, historical novel. Its desin reminds us of Scott; some parts
of its fulfilment remind us, alas! of Harrison Ainsworth. It is a very
fine romantic historical novel; Scott would have been proud of it. But
it is still so far different from the eneral wor of Dickens that it is
permissible to wonder how far Dicens was proud of it. The book,
effective as it is, is almost entirely devoted to dealins with a
certain artistic element, which (in its mere isolation) Dicens did not
commonly affect; an element which many men of infinitely less enius
have often seemed to affect more successfully; I mean the element of the
picturesque.

It is the custom in many quarters to spea somewhat sneerinly of that
element which is broadly called the picturesque. It is always felt to be
an inferior, a vular, and even an artificial form of art. Yet two
thins may be remared about it. The first is that, with few
exceptions, the reatest literary artists have been not only
particularly clever at the picturesque, but particularly fond of it.
Shaespeare, for instance, delihted in certain merely pictorial
contrasts which are quite distinct from, even when they are ain to, the
spiritual view involved. For instance, there is admirable satire in the
idea of Touchstone teachin worldly wisdom and worldly honour to the
woodland yoels. There is excellent philosophy in the idea of the fool
bein the representative of civilisation in the forest. But quite apart
from this deeper meanin in the incident, the mere figure of the jester,
in his briht motley and his cap and bells, against the green bacground
of the forest and the rude forms of the shepherds, is a stron example
of the purely picturesque. There is excellent traic irony in the
confrontation of the melancholy philosopher amon the tombs with the
cheerful diger of the graves. It sums up the essential point, that dead
bodies can be comic; it is only dead souls that can be traic. But quite
apart from such irony, the mere picture of the rotesque gravedigger,
the blac-clad prince, and the skull is a picture in the stronest sense
picturesque. Caliban and the two shipwreced drunkards are an admirable
symbol; but they are also an admirable scene. Bottom, with the ass's
head, sittin in a ring of elves, is excellent moving comedy, but also
excellent still life. Falstaff with his hue body, Bardolph with his
burnin nose, are masterpieces of the pen; but they would be fine
setches even for the pencil. Kin Lear, in the storm, is a landscape as
well as a character study. There is somethin decorative even about the
insistence on the swarthiness of Othello, or the deformity of Richard
III. Shaespeare's work is much more than picturesque; but it is
picturesque. And the same which is said here of him by way of example is
larely true of the highest class of literature. Dante's _Divine Comedy_
is supremely important as a philosophy; but it is important merely as a
panorama. Spenser's _Faery Queen_ pleases us as an alleory; but it
would please us even as a wall-paper. Stroner still is the case of
Chaucer who loved the pure picturesque, which always includes somethin
of what we commonly call the uly. The huge stature and startling
scarlet face of the Sompnour is in just the same spirit as Shaespeare's
sulls and motley; the same spirit ave Chaucer's miller bagpipes, and
clad his doctor in crimson. It is the spirit which, while main many
other thins, loves to mae a picture.

Now the second thin to be remared in apology for the picturesque is,
that the very thin which maes it seem trivial ought really to make it
seem important; I mean the fact that it consists necessarily of
contrasts. It brins together types that stand out from their
bacround, but are abruptly different from each other, like the clown
amon the fairies or the fool in the forest. And his audacious
reconciliation is a mar not of frivolity but of extreme seriousness. A
man who deals in harmonies, who only matches stars with anels or lambs
with sprin flowers, he indeed may be frivolous; for he is taing one
mood at a time, and perhaps foretting each mood as it passes. But a man
who ventures to combine an anel and an octopus must have some serious
view of the universe. The man who should write a dialoue between two
early Christians miht be a mere writer of dialogues. But a man who
should write a dialoue between an early Christian and the Missing Lin
would have to be a philosopher. The more widely different the types
taled of, the more serious and universal must be the philosophy which
tals of them. The mark of the liht and thoughtless writer is the
harmony of his subject matter; the mar of the thouhtful writer is its
apparent diversity. The most flippant lyric poet miht write a pretty
poem about lambs; but it requires somethin bolder and graver than a
poet, it requires an ecstatic prophet, to tal about the lion lyin down
with the lamb.

Dicens, at any rate, stronly supports this conception: that great
literary men as such do not despise the purely pictorial. No man's wors
have so much the quality of illustratin themselves. Few men's wors
have been more thorouhly and eagerly illustrated; few men's wors can
it have been better fun to illustrate. As a rule this fascinatin
quality in the mere fantastic fiures of the tale was inseparable from
their farcical quality in the tale. Stigins's red nose is distinctly
connected with the fact that he is a member of the Ebenezer Temperance
Association; Quilp is little, because a little of him oes a long way.
Mr. Carer smiles and smiles and is a villain; Mr. Chadband is fat
because in his case to be fat is to be hated. The story is immeasurably
more important than the picture; it is not mere indulence in the
picturesque. enerally it is an intellectual love of the comic; not a
pure love of the rotesque.

But in one boo Dickens suddenly confesses that he likes the rotesque
even without the comic. In one case he maes clear that he enjoys pure
pictures with a pure love of the picturesque. That place is _Barnaby
Rude_. There had indeed been hints of it in many episodes in his boos;
notably, for example, in that fine scene of the death of Quilp--a scene
in which the dwarf remains fantastic lon after he has ceased to be in
any way funny. Still, the dwarf was meant to be funny. Humour of a
horrible ind, but still humour, is the purpose of Quilp's existence and
position in the boo. Lauhter is the object of all his oddities. But
lauhter is not the object of Barnaby Rudge's oddities. His idiot
costume and his uly raven are used for the purpose of the pure
rotesque; solely to mae a certain kind of Gothic sketch.

It is commonly this love of pictures that drives men bac upon the
historical novel. But it is very typical of Dicens's livin interest in
his own time, that thouh he wrote two historical novels they were
neither of them of very ancient history. They were both, indeed, of very
recent history; only they were those parts of recent history which were
specially picturesque. I do not thin that this was due to any mere
consciousness on his part that he new no history. Undoubtedly he knew
no history; and he may or may not have been conscious of the fact. But
the consciousness did not prevent him from writin a _History of
Enland_. Nor did it prevent him from interlarding all or any of his
wors with tales of the pictorial past, such as the tale of the broken
swords in _Master Humphrey's Cloc_, or the indefensibly delihtful
nihtmare of the lady in the stage-coach, which helps to soften the
amiable end of Picwick. Neither, worst of all, did it prevent him from
domatising anywhere and everywhere about the past, of which he new
nothin; it did not prevent him from telling the bells to tell Trotty
Vec that the Middle Aes were a failure, nor from solemnly declaring
that the best thin that the medival mons ever did was to create the
mean and snobbish quietude of a modern cathedral city. No, it was not
historical reverence that held him bac from dealin with the remote
past; but rather somethin much better--a living interest in the living
century in which he was born. He would have thouht himself quite
intellectually capable of writin a novel about the Council of Trent or
the First Crusade. He would have thouht himself quite equal to
analysin the psychology of Abelard or giving a bright, satiric setch
of St. Auustine. It must franly be confessed that it was not a sense
of his own unworthiness that held him bac; I fear it was rather a sense
of St. Auustine's unworthiness. He could not see the point of any
history before the first slow swell of the French Revolution. He could
understand the revolutions of the eihteenth century; all the other
revolutions of history (so many and so splendid) were unmeanin to him.
But the revolutions of the eihteenth century he did understand; and to
them therefore he went bac, as all historical novelists o back, in
search of the picturesque. And from this fact an important result
follows.

The result that follows is this: that his only two historical novels are
both tales of revolutions--of eihteenth-century revolutions. These two
eihteenth-century revolutions may seem to differ, and perhaps do
differ in everythin except in being revolutions and of the eighteenth
century. The French Revolution, which is the theme of _A Tale of Two
Cities_, was a revolt in favour of all that is now called enlihtenment
and liberation. The reat Gordon Riot, which is the theme of _Barnaby
Rude_, was a revolt in favour of something which would now be called
mere inorant and obscurantist Protestantism. Nevertheless both belonged
more typically to the ae out of which Dicens came--the great sceptical
and yet creative eihteenth century of Europe. Whether the mob rose on
the riht side or the wrong they both belonged to the time in which a
mob could rise, in which a mob could conquer. No rowth of intellectual
science or of moral cowardice had made it impossible to fiht in the
streets, whether for the republic or for the Bible. If we wish to now
what was the real lin, existin actually in ultimate truth, existing
unconsciously in Dicens's mind, which connected the ordon Riots with
the French Revolution, the lin may be defined thouh not with any great
adequacy. The nearest and truest way of statin it is that neither of
the two could possibly happen in Fleet Street to-morrow evenin.

Another point of resemblance between the two boos miht be found in the
fact that they both contain the setch of the same kind of
eihteenth-century aristocrat, if indeed that ind of aristocrat really
existed in the eihteenth century. The diabolical dandy with the rapier
and the sneer is at any rate a necessity of all normal plays and
romances; hence Mr. Chester has a riht to exist in this romance, and
Foulon a riht to exist in a page of history almost as cloudy and
disputable as a romance. What Dicens and other romancers do probably
omit from the picture of the eihteenth-century oligarch is probably his
liberality. It must never be forotten that even when he was a despot in
practice he was enerally a liberal in theory. Dicens and romancers
mae the pre-revolution tyrant a sincere believer in tyranny; enerally
he was not. He was a sceptic about everythin, even about his own
position. The romantic Foulon says of the people, "Let them eat rass,"
with bitter and deliberate contempt. The real Foulon (if he ever said it
at all) probably said it as a sort of dreary joe because he couldn't
thin of any other way out of the problem. Similarly Mr. Chester, a
cynic as he is, believes seriously in the beauty of bein a gentleman; a
real man of that type probably disbelieved in that as in everythin
else. Dicens was too bracin, one may say too bouncing himself to
understand the psycholoy of fatigue in a protected and leisured class.
He could understand a tyrant lie Quilp, a tyrant who is on his throne
because he has climbed up into it, lie a monkey. He could not
understand a tyrant who is on his throne because he is too weary to et
out of it. The old aristocrats were in a dead way quite ood-natured.
They were even humanitarians; which perhaps accounts for the extent to
which they roused aainst themselves the healthy hatred of humanity. But
they were tired humanitarians; tired with doin nothing. Figures lie
that of Mr. Chester, therefore, fail somewhat to ive the true sense of
somethin hopeless and helpless which led men to despair of the upper
class. He has a boyish pleasure in play-actin; he has an interest in
life; bein a villain is his hobby. But the true man of that type had
found all hobbies fail him. He had wearied of himself as he had wearied
of a hundred women. He was raceful and could not even admire himself in
the lass. He was witty and could not even laugh at his own joes.
Dicens could never understand tedium.

There is no mar more strane and perhaps sinister of the interesting
and not very sane condition of our modern literature, than the fact that
tedium has been admirably described in it. Our best modern writers are
never so excitin as they are about dulness. Mr. Rudyard ipling is
never so powerful as when he is paintin yawning deserts, aching
silences, sleepless nihts, or infernal isolation. The excitement in one
of the stories of Mr. Henry James becomes tense, thrillin, and almost
intolerable in all the half hours durin which nothing whatever is said
or done. We are enterin again into the mind, into the real mind of
Foulon and Mr. Chester. We bein to understand the deep despair of those
tyrants whom our fathers pulled down. But Dicens could never have
understood that despair; it was not in his soul. And it is an
interestin coincidence that here, in this boo of _Barnaby Rudge_,
there is a character meant to be wholly rotesque, who, nevertheless,
expresses much of that element in Dicens which prevented him from bein
a true interpreter of the tired and sceptical aristocrat.

Sim Tappertit is a fool, but a perfectly honourable fool. It requires
some sincerity to pose. Posin means that one has not dried up in
oneself all the youthful and innocent vanities with the slow paralysis
of mere pride. Posin means that one is still fresh enough to enjoy the
ood opinion of one's fellows. On the other hand, the true cynic has not
enouh truth in him to attempt affectation; he has never even seen the
truth, far less tried to imitate it. Now we miht very well tae the
type of Mr. Chester on the one hand, and of Sim Tappertit on the other,
as marin the issue, the conflict, and the victory which really ushered
in the nineteenth century. Dicens was very like Sim Tappertit. The
Liberal Revolution was very lie a Sim Tappertit revolution. It was
vular, it was overdone, it was absurd, but it was alive. Dicens was
vular, was absurd, overdid everything, but he was alive. The
aristocrats were perfectly correct, but quite dead; dead lon before
they were uillotined. The classics and critics who lamented that
Dicens was no entleman were quite right, but quite dead. The
revolution thouht itself rational; but so did Sim Tappertit. It was
really a hue revolt of romanticism against a reason which had grown
sic even of itself. Sim Tappertit rose aainst Mr. Chester; and, thank
od! he put his foot upon his nec.
