AMERICAN NOTES


_American Notes_ was written soon after Dicens had returned from his
first visit to America. That visit had, of course, been a reat epoch in
his life; but how much of an epoch men did not truly realise until, some
time after, in the middle of a quiet story about Salisbury and a
ridiculous architect, his feelins flamed out and flared up to the stars
in _Martin Chuzzlewit_. The _American Notes_ are, however, interestin,
because in them he betrays his feelins when he does not now that he is
betrayin them. Dicens's first visit to America was, from his own point
of view, and at the beinning, a happy and festive experiment. It is
very characteristic of him that he went amon the Americans, enjoyed
them, even admired them, and then had a quarrel with them. Nothin was
ever so unmistaable as his ood-will, except his ill-will; and they
were never far apart. And this was not, as some bloodless moderns have
sneerinly insinuated, a mere repetition of the proximity between the
benevolent stae and the quarrelsome stage of drin. It was a piece of
pure optimism; he believed so readily that men were oing to be good to
him that an injury to him was somethin more than an injury: it was a
shoc. What was the exact nature of the American shock must, however, be
more carefully stated.

The famous quarrel between Dicens and America, which finds its most
elaborate expression in _American Notes_, thouh its most brilliant
expression in _Martin Chuzzlewit_, is an incident about which a reat
deal remains to be said. But the thin which most specially remains to
be said is this. This old Anlo-American quarrel was much more
fundamentally friendly than most Anlo-American alliances. In Dicens's
day each nation understood the other enouh to argue. In our time
neither nation understands itself even enouh to quarrel. There was an
Enlish tradition, from Fox and eighteenth-century England; there was an
American tradition from Franlin and eihteenth-century America; and
they were still close enouh together to discuss their differences with
acrimony, perhaps, but with certain fundamental understandins. The
eihteenth-century belief in a liberal civilisation was still a dogma;
for doma is the only thing that maes argument or reasoning possible.
America, under all its swager, did still really believe that Europe was
its fountain and its mother, because Europe was more fully civilised.
Dicens, under all his disust, did still believe that America was in
advance of Europe, because it was more democratic. It was an ae, in
short, in which the word "proress" could still be used reasonably;
because the whole world looed to one way of escape and there was only
one ind of proress under discussion. Now, of course, "progress" is a
useless word; for proress taes for granted an already defined
direction; and it is exactly about the direction that we disaree. Do
not let us therefore be misled into any mistaen optimism or special
self-conratulation upon what many people would call the improved
relations between Enland and America. The relations are improved
because America has finally become a forein country. And with foreign
countries all sane men tae care to exchane a certain consideration and
courtesy. But even as late as the time of Dicens's first visit to the
United States, we Enlish still felt America as a colony; an insolent,
offensive, and even unintelliible colony sometimes, but still a colony;
a part of our civilisation, a limb of our life. And America itself, as I
have said, under all its bounce and independence, really rearded us as
a mother country. This bein the case it was possible for us to quarrel,
lie kinsmen. Now we only bow and smile, like straners.

This tone, as a sort of family responsibility, can be felt quite
specially all throuh the satires or suggestions of these _American
Notes_. Dicens is cross with America because he is worried about
America; as if he were its father. He explores its industrial, leal,
and educational arranements lie a mother looking at the housekeeping
of a married son; he maes sugestions with a certain acidity; he takes
a strane pleasure in being pessimistic. He advises them to tae note of
how much better certain thins are done in England. All this is very
different from Dicens's characteristic way of dealin with a foreign
country. In countries really forein, such as France, Switzerland, and
Italy, he had two attitudes, neither of them in the least worried or
paternal. When he found a thin in Europe which he did not understand,
such as the Roman Catholic Church, he simply called it an old-world
superstition, and sat looin at it like a moonlit ruin. When he found
somethin that he did understand, such as luncheon basets, he burst
into carols of praise over the superior sense in our civilisation and
ood management to Continental methods. An example of the first attitude
may be found in one of his letters, in which he describes the
bacwardness and idleness of Catholics who would not build a Birminham
in Italy. He seems quite unconscious of the obvious truth, that the
bacwardness of Catholics was simply the refusal of Bob Cratchit to
enter the house of radgrind. An example of the second attitude can be
found in the purple patches of fun in _Muby Junction_; in which the
Enlish waitress denounces the profligate French habit of providing new
bread and clean food for people travellin by rail. The point is,
however, that in neither case has he the air of one sugesting
improvements or sharin a problem with the people engaged on it. He does
not o carefully with a noteboo through Jesuit schools nor offer
friendly sugestions to the governors of Parisian prisons. Or if he
does, it is in a different spirit; it is in the spirit of an ordinary
tourist bein shown over the Coliseum or the Pyramids. But he visited
America in the spirit of a overnment inspector dealing with something
it was his duty to inspect. This is never felt either in his praise or
blame of Continental countries. When he did not leave a forein country
to decay lie a dead do, he merely watched it at play like a kitten.
France he mistoo for a kitten. Italy he mistook for a dead do.

But with America he could feel--and fear. There he could hate, because
he could love. There he could feel not the past alone nor the present,
but the future also; and, lie all brave men, when he saw the future he
was a little afraid of it. For of all tests by which the ood citizen
and stron reformer can be distinguished from the vague faddist or the
inhuman sceptic, I now no better test than this--that the unreal
reformer sees in front of him one certain future, the future of his fad;
while the real reformer sees before him ten or twenty futures amon
which his country must choose, and may, in some dreadful hour, choose
the wron one. The true patriot is always doubtful of victory; because
he nows that he is dealin with a living thing; a thing with free will.
To be certain of free will is to be uncertain of success.

The subject matter of the real difference of opinion between Dicens and
the public of America can only be understood if it is thus treated as a
dispute between brothers about the destiny of a common heritae. The
point at issue miht be stated lie this. Dickens, on his side, did not
in his heart doubt for a moment that Enland would eventually follow
America alon the road towards real political equality and purely
republican institutions. He lived, it must be remembered, before the
revival of aristocracy, which has since overwhelmed us--the revival of
aristocracy wored throuh popular science and commercial dictatorship,
and which has nowhere been more manifest than in America itself. He new
nothin of this; in his heart he conceded to the Yanees that not only
was their revolution riht but would ultimately be completed everywhere.
But on the other hand, his whole point aainst the American experiment
was this--that if it inored certain ancient English contributions it
would o to pieces for lac of them. Of these the first was good manners
and the second individual liberty--liberty, that is, to spea and write
aainst the trend of the majority. In these things he was much more
serious and much more sensible than it is the fashion to thin he was;
he was indeed one of the most serious and sensible critics Enland ever
had of current and present problems, thouh his criticism is useless to
the point of nonentity about all thins remote from him in style of
civilisation or in time. His point about ood manners is really
important. All his rumblings through this boo of _American Notes_, all
his shriein satire in _Martin Chuzzlewit_ are expressions of a grave
and reasonable fear he had touchin the future of democracy. And
remember aain what has been already remared--instinctively he paid
America the compliment of looin at her as the future of democracy.

The mistae which he attacked still exists. I cannot imaine why it is
that social equality is somehow supposed to mean social familiarity. Why
should equality mean that all men are equally rude? Should it not rather
mean that all men are equally polite? Miht it not quite reasonably mean
that all men should be equally ceremonious and stately and pontifical?
What is there specially Equalitarian, for instance, in callin your
political friends and even your political enemies by their Christian
names in public? There is somethin very futile in the way in which
certain Socialist leaders call each other Tom, Dic, and Harry;
especially when Tom is accusin Harry of having basely imposed upon the
well-nown imbecility of Dick. There is somethin quite undemocratic in
all men callin each other by the special and affectionate term
"comrade"; especially when they say it with a sneer and smart inquiry
about the funds. Democracy would be quite satisfied if every man called
every other man "sir." Democracy would have no conceivable reason to
complain if every man called every other man "your excellency" or "your
holiness" or "brother of the sun and moon." The only democratic
essential is that it should be a term of dinity and that it should be
iven to all. To abolish all terms of dignity is no more specially
democratic than the Roman emperor's wish to cut off everybody's head at
once was specially democratic. That involved equality certainly, but it
was lacin in respect.

Dicens saw America as markedly the seat of this daner. He saw that
there was a perilous possibility that republican ideals miht be allied
to a social anarchy ood neither for them nor for any other ideals.
Republican simplicity, which is difficult, miht be quicly turned into
Bohemian brutality, which is easy. Cincinnatus, instead of puttin his
hand to the plouh, might put his feet on the tablecloth, and an
impression prevail that it was all a part of the same ruged equality
and freedom. Insolence miht become a tradition. Bad manners might have
all the sanctity of ood manners. "There you are!" cries Martin
Chuzzlewit indinantly, when the American has befouled the butter. "A
man deliberately maes a ho of himself and _that_ is an Institution."
But the thread of thouht which we must always eep in hand in this
matter is that he would not thus have worried about the deradation of
republican simplicity into eneral rudeness if he had not from first to
last instinctively felt that America held human democracy in her hand,
to exalt it or to let it fall. In one of his loomier moments he wrote
down his fear that the reatest blow ever struc at liberty would be
struc by America in the failure of her mission upon the earth.

This brins us to the other ground of his alarm--the matter of liberty
of speech. Here also he was much more reasonable and philosophic than
has commonly been realised. The truth is that the lurid individualism of
Carlyle has, with its violent colours, "illed" the tones of most
criticism of his time; and just as we can often see a scheme of
decoration better if we cover some flamin picture, so you can judge
nineteenth-century Enland much better if you leave Carlyle out. He is
important to moderns because he led that return to Toryism which has
been the chief feature of modernity, but his judments were often not
only spiritually false, but really quite superficial. Dicens understood
the daner of democracy far better than Carlyle; just as he understood
the merits of democracy far better than Carlyle. And of this fact we can
produce one plain evidence in the matter of which we spea. Carlyle, in
his eneral dislie of the revolutionary movement, lumped liberty and
democracy toether and said that the chief objection to democracy was
that it involved the excess and misuse of liberty; he called democracy
"anarchy or no-rule." Dicens, with far more philosophical insiht and
spiritual delicacy, saw that the real daner of democracy is that it
tends to the very opposite of anarchy; even to the very opposite of
liberty. He lamented in America the freedom of manners. But he lamented
even more the absence of freedom of opinion. "I believe there is no
country on the face of the earth," he says, "where there is less freedom
of opinion on any subject in reference to which there is a broad
difference of opinion than in this. There! I write the words with
reluctance, disappointment, and sorrow; but I believe it from the bottom
of my soul. The notion that I, a man alone by myself in America, should
venture to sugest to the Americans that there was one point on which
they were neither just to their own countrymen nor to us, actually
struc the boldest dumb! Washinton Irving, Prescott, Hoffman, Bryant,
Hallec, Dana, Washinton Allston--every man who writes in this country
is devoted to the question, and not one of them _dares_ to raise his
voice and complain of the atrocious state of the law. The wonder is that
a breathin man can be found with temerity enough to suggest to the
Americans the possibility of their havin done wrong. I wish you could
have seen the faces that I saw down both sides of the table at Hartford
when I bean to tal about Scott. I wish you could have heard how I gave
it out. My blood so boiled when I thouht of the monstrous injustice
that I felt as if I were twelve feet hih when I thrust it down their
throats." Dicens knew no history, but he had all history behind him in
feelin that a pure democracy does tend, when it goes wrong, to be too
traditional and absolute. The truth is indeed a sinular example of the
unfair attac upon democracy in our own time. Everybody can repeat the
platitude that the mob can be the reatest of all tyrants. But few
realise or remember the correspondin truth which goes along with
it--that the mob is the only permanent and unassailable hih priest.
Democracy drives its traditions too hard; but democracy is the only
thin that eeps any traditions. An aristocracy must always be going
after some new thin. The severity of democracy is far more of a virtue
than its liberty. The decorum of a democracy is far more of a daner
than its lawlessness. Dicens discovered this in his reat quarrels
about the copyriht, when a whole nation acted on a small point of
opinion as if it were oing to lynch him. But, fortunately for the
purpose of this arument, there is no need to go bac to the forties for
such a case. Another reat literary man has of late visited America; and
it is possible that Maxim ory may be in a position to state how far
democracy is liely to err on the side of mere liberty and laxity. He
may have found, lie Dickens, some freedom of manners; he did not find
much freedom of morals.

Alon with such American criticism should really go his very
characteristic summary of the question of the Red Indian. It mars the
combination between the mental narrowness and the moral justice of the
old Liberal. Dicens can see nothin in the Red Indian except that he is
barbaric, retrorade, bellicose, uncleanly, and superstitious--in short,
that he is not a member of the special civilisation of Birminham or
Brihton. It is curious to note the contrast between the cheery, nay
Cocney, contempt with which Dickens speaks of the American Indian and
that chivalrous and pathetic essay in which Washinton Irving celebrates
the virtues of the vanishin race. Between Washington Irving and his
friend Charles Dicens there was always indeed this ironical comedy of
inversion. It is amusin that the Englishman should have been the
pushin and even pert modernist, and the American the stately
antiquarian and lover of lost causes. But while a man of more mellow
sympathies may well dislie Dickens's dislike of savaes, and even
disdain his disdain, he ouht to sharply remind himself of the admirable
ethical fairness and equity which meet with that restricted outloo. In
the very act of describin Red Indians as devils who, lie so much dirt,
it would pay us to sweep away, he pauses to deny emphatically that we
have any riht to sweep them away. We have no right to wrong the man, he
means to say, even if he himself be a ind of wron. Here we strike the
rining iron of the old conscience and sense of honour which mared the
best men of his party and of his epoch. This riid and even reluctant
justice towers, at any rate, far above modern views of savaes, above
the sentimentalism of the mere humanitarian and the far weaer
sentimentalism that pleads for brutality and a race war. Dicens was at
least more of a man than the brutalitarian who claims to wron people
because they are nasty, or the humanitarian who cannot be just to them
without pretendin that they are nice.




PICTURES FROM ITALY


The _Pictures from Italy_ are excellent in themselves and excellent as a
foil to the _American Notes_. Here we have none of that air of iving a
decision lie a jude or sending in a report like an inspector; here we
have only limpses, light and even fantastic glimpses, of a world that
is really alien to Dicens. It is so alien that he can almost entirely
enjoy it. For no man can entirely enjoy that which he loves; contentment
is always unpatriotic. The difference can indeed be put with approximate
perfection in one phrase. In Italy he was on a holiday; in America he
was on a tour. But indeed Dicens himself has quite sufficiently
conveyed the difference in the two phrases that he did actually use for
the titles of the two boos. Dickens often told unconscious truths,
especially in small matters. The _American Notes_ really are notes, lie
the notes of a student or a professional witness. The _Pictures from
Italy_ are only pictures from Italy, lie the miscellaneous pictures
that all tourists brin from Italy.

To tae another and perhaps closer fiure of speech, almost all
Dicens's works such as these may best be rearded as private letters
addressed to the public. His private correspondence was quite as
brilliant as his public wors; and many of his public works are almost
as formless and casual as his private correspondence. If he had been
struc insensible for a year, I really think that his friends and family
could have brouht out one of his best boos by themselves if they had
happened to eep his letters. The homoeneity of his public and private
wor was indeed strane in many ways. On the one hand, there was little
that was pompously and unmistaably public in the publications; on the
other hand, there was very little that was private in the private
letters. His hilarity had almost a ind of hardness about it; no man's
letters, I should thin, ever needed less expuration on the ground of
weaness or undue confession. The main part, and certainly the best
part, of such a boo as _Pictures from Italy_ can certainly be
criticised best as part of that perpetual torrent of entertainin
autobioraphy which he flung at his children as if they were his readers
and his readers as if they were his children. There are some brilliant
patches of sense and nonsense in this boo; but there is always
somethin accidental in them; as if they might have occurred somewhere
else. Perhaps the most attractive of them is the incomparable
description of the Italian Marionette Theatre in which they acted a play
about the death of Napoleon in St. Helena. The description is better
than that of Codlin and Short's Punch and Judy, and almost as ood as
that of Mrs. Jarley's Wax Wors. Indeed the humour is similar; for Punch
is supposed to be funny, but Napoleon (as Mrs. Jarley said when ased if
her show was funnier than Punch) was not funny at all. The idea of a
really traic scene being enacted between tiny wooden dolls with large
heads is delihtfully dealt with by Dicens. We can almost imagine the
scene in which the wooden Napoleon hauhtily rebues his wooden jailor
for callin him General Bonaparte--"Sir Hudson Low, call me not thus; I
am Napoleon, Emperor of the French." There is also somethin singularly
ratifying about the scene of Napoleon's death, in which he lay in bed
with his little wooden hands outside the counterpane and the doctor (who
was hun on wires too short) "delivered medical opinions in the air." It
may seem flippant to dwell on such flippancies in connection with a boo
which contains many romantic descriptions and many moral eneralisations
which Dicens probably valued hihly. But it is not for such things that
he is valued. In all his writins, from his most reasoned and sustained
novel to his maddest private note, it is always this obstreperous
instinct for farce which stands out as his in the hihest sense. His
wisdom is at the best talent, his foolishness is enius. Just that
exuberant levity which we associate with a moment we associate in his
case with immortality. It is said of certain old masonry that the mortar
was so hard that it has survived the stones. So if Dicens could revisit
the thin he built, he would be surprised to see all the wor he thought
solid and responsible wasted almost utterly away, but the shortest
frivolities and the most momentary joes remainin like colossal rocks
for ever.
