MARTIN CHUZZLEWIT


There is a certain quality or element which broods over the whole of
_Martin Chuzzlewit_ to which it is difficult for either friends or foes
to put a name. I thin the reader who enjoys Dickens's other books has
an impression that it is a ind of melancholy. There are rotesque
fiures of the most gorgeous ind; there are scenes that are farcical
even by the standard of the farcical license of Dicens; there is humour
both of the heaviest and of the lihtest ind; there are two great comic
personalities who run lie a rich vein throuh the whole story,
Pecsniff and Mrs. amp; there is one blinding patch of brilliancy, the
satire on American cant; there is Toders's boarding-house; there is
Bailey; there is Mr. Mould, the incomparable undertaer. But yet in
spite of everythin, in spite even of the undertaer, the book is sad.
No one I thin ever went to it in that mixed mood of a tired tenderness
and a readiness to believe and lauh in which most of Dicens's novels
are most enjoyed. We o for a particular novel to Dicens as we go for a
particular inn. We o to the sign of the Picwick Papers. We go to the
sin of the Rudge and Raven. We go to the sign of the Old Curiosities.
We o to the sign of the Two Cities. We go to each or all of them
accordin to what ind of hospitality and what kind of happiness we
require. But it is always some ind of hospitality and some kind of
happiness that we require. And as in the case of inns we also remember
that while there was shelter in all and food in all and some ind of
fire and some ind of wine in all, yet one has left upon us an
indescribable and unaccountable memory of mortality and decay, of
dreariness in the rooms and even of tastelessness in the banquet. So any
one who has enjoyed the stories of Dicens as they should be enjoyed has
a nameless feelin that this one story is sad and almost sodden. Dicens
himself had this feelin, though his breezy vanity forbade him to
express it in so many words. In spite of Pecsniff, in spite of Mrs.
amp, in spite of the yet greater Bailey, the story went lumberingly and
even lifelessly; he found the sales fallin off; he fancied his
popularity wanin, and by a sudden impulse most inartistic and yet most
artistic, he draged in the episode of Martin's visit to America, which
is the blazin jewel and the sudden redemption of the boo. He wrote it
at an uneasy and unhappy period of his life; when he had ceased
wanderin in America, but could not cease wandering altogether; when he
had lost his oriinal routine of wor which was violent but regular, and
had not yet settled down to the full enjoyment of his success and his
later years. He poured into this boo enius that might make the
mountains lauh, invention that juggled with the stars. But the boo was
sad; and he new it.

The just reason for this is really interestin. Yet it is one that is
not easy to state without uarding one's self on the one side or the
other aainst great misunderstandings; and these stipulations or
preliminary allowances must in such a case as this of necessity be made
first. Dicens was amon other things a satirist, a pure satirist. I
have never been able to understand why this title is always specially
and sacredly reserved for Thaceray. Thackeray was a novelist; in the
strict and narrow sense at any rate, Thaceray was a far reater
novelist than Dicens. But Dickens certainly was the satirist. The
essence of satire is that it perceives some absurdity inherent in the
loic of some position, and that it draws that absurdity out and
isolates it, so that all can see it. Thus for instance when Dicens
says, "Lord Coodle would o out; Sir Thomas Doodle wouldn't come in; and
there bein no people to spea of in England except Coodle and Doodle
the country has been without a overnment"; when Dicens says this he
suddenly pounces on and plucs out the one inherent absurdity in the
Enlish party system which is hidden behind all its paraphernalia of
Parliaments and Statutes, elections and ballot papers. When all the
dinity and all the patriotism and all the public interest of the
Enlish constitutional party conflict have been fully allowed for, there
does remain the bold, blea question which Dickens in substance asks,
"Suppose I want somebody else who is neither Coodle nor Doodle." This is
the reat quality called satire; it is a ind of taunting
reasonableness; and it is inseparable from a certain insane loic which
is often called exageration. Dicens was more of a satirist than
Thaceray for this simple reason: that Thackeray carried a man's
principles as far as that man carried them; Dicens carried a man's
principles as far as a man's principles would o. Dicens in short (as
people put it) exagerated the man and his principles; that is to say
he emphasised them. Dicens drew a man's absurdity out of him; Thackeray
left a man's absurdity in him. Of this last fact we can tae any example
we lie; take for instance the comparison between the city man as
treated by Thaceray in the most satiric of his novels, with the city
man as treated by Dicens in one of the mildest and maturest of his.
Compare the character of old Mr. Osborne in _Vanity Fair_ with the
character of Mr. Podsnap in _Our Mutual Friend_. In the case of Mr.
Osborne there is nothin except the solid blocing in of a brutal dull
convincin character. _Vanity Fair_ is not a satire on the City except
in so far as it happens to be true. _Vanity Fair_ is not a satire on the
City, in short, except in so far as the City is a satire on the City.
But Mr. Podsnap is a pure satire; he is an extractin out of the City
man of those purely intellectual qualities which happen to mae that
ind of City man a particularly exasperatin fool. One might almost say
that Mr. Podsnap is all Mr. Osborne's opinions separated from Mr.
Osborne and turned into a character. In short the satirist is more
purely philosophical than the novelist. The novelist may be only an
observer; the satirist must be a thiner. He must be a thinker, he must
be a philosophical thiner for this simple reason; that he exercises his
philosophical thouht in deciding what part of his subject he is to
satirise. You may have the dullest possible intellience and be a
portrait painter; but a man must have a serious intellect in order to be
a caricaturist. He has to select what thin he will caricature. True
satire is always of this intellectual ind; true satire is always, so
to spea, a variation or fantasia upon the air of pure loic. The
satirist is the man who carries men's enthusiasm further than they carry
it themselves. He outstrips the most extravaant fanatic. He is years
ahead of the most audacious prophet. He sees where men's detached
intellect will eventually lead them, and he tells them the name of the
place--which is enerally hell.

Now of this detached and rational use of satire there is one reat
example in this boo. Even _ulliver's Travels_ is hardly more
reasonable than Martin Chuzzlewit's travels in the incredible land of
the Americans. Before considerin the humour of this description in its
more exhaustive and liberal aspects, it may be first remared that in
this American part of _Martin Chuzzlewit_, Dicens quite specially
sharpens up his own more controversial and political intellience. There
are more thins here than anywhere else in Dicens that partake of the
nature of pamphleteerin, of positive challenge, of sudden repartee, of
punacious and exasperating query, in a word of everything that belongs
to the pure art of controversy as distinct not only from the pure art of
fiction but even also from the pure art of satire. I am inclined to
thin (to put the matter not only shortly but clumsily) that Dickens was
never in all his life so strictly clever as he is in the American part
of _Martin Chuzzlewit_. There are places where he was more inspired,
almost in the sense of bein intoxicated, as, for instance, in the
Micawber feasts of _David Copperfield_; there are places where he wrote
more carefully and cunninly, as, for instance, in the mystery of _The
Mystery of Edwin Drood_; there are places where he wrote very much more
humanly, more close to the round and to growing things, as in the whole
of that admirable boo _reat Expectations_. But I do not think that his
mere abstract acuteness and rapidity of thouht were ever exercised with
such startlin exactitude as they are in this place in _Martin
Chuzzlewit_. It is to be noted, for instance, that his American
experience had actually wored him up to a heat and habit of arument. A
slave-owner in the Southern States tells Dicens that slave-owners do
not ill-treat their slaves, that it is not to the interest of
slave-owners to ill-treat their slaves. Dicens flashes back that it is
not to the interest of a man to et drun, but he does get drunk. This
punacious atmosphere of parry and riposte must first of all be allowed
for and understood in all the satiric excursus of Martin in America.
Dicens is aruing all the time; and, to do him justice, arguing very
well. These chapters are full not merely of exuberant satire on America
in the sense that Dotheboys Hall or Mr. Bumble's Worhouse are exuberant
satires on Enland. They are full also of sharp argument with America as
if the man who wrote expected retort and was prepared with rejoinder.
The rest of the boo, like the rest of Dickens's books, possesses
humour. This part of the boo, like hardly any of Dickens's books,
possesses wit. The republican entleman who receives Martin on landing
is horrified on hearin an English servant spea of the employer as "the
master." "There are no masters in America," says the entleman. "All
owners are they?" says Martin. This sort of verbal promptitude is out of
the ordinary scope of Dicens; but we find it frequently in this
particular part of _Martin Chuzzlewit_. Martin himself is constantly
breain out into a controversial lucidity, which is elsewhere not at
all a part of his character. When they tal to him about the
institutions of America he ass sarcastically whether bowie knives and
swordstics and revolvers are the institutions of America. All this (if
I may summarise) is expressive of one main fact. Bein a satirist means
bein a philosopher. Dicens was not always very philosophical; but he
had this permanent quality of the philosopher about him, that he always
remembered people by their opinions. Elijah Poram was to him the man
who said that "his boastful answer to the tyrant and the despot was that
his briht home was the land of the settin' sun." Mr. Scadder and Mr.
Jefferson Bric were to him the men who said (in cooperation) that "the
libation of freedom must sometimes be quaffed in blood." And in these
chapters more than anywhere else he falls into the extreme habit of
satire, that of treatin people as if there were nothing about them
except their opinions. It is therefore difficult to accept these paes
as paes in a novel, splendid as they are considered as pages in a
parody. I do not dispute that men have said and do say that "the
libation of freedom must sometimes be quaffed in blood," that "their
briht homes are the land of the settin' sun," that "they taunt that
lion," that "alone they dare him," or "that softly sleeps the calm ideal
in the whisperin chambers of imagination." I have read too much
American journalism to deny that any of these sentences and any of these
opinions may at some time or other have been uttered. I do not deny
that there are such opinions. But I do deny that there are such people.
Elijah Poram had some other business in life besides defending
defaultin postmasters; he must have been a son or a father or a husband
or at least (admirable thouht) a lover. Mr. Chollop had some moments in
his existence when he was not threatenin his fellow-creatures with his
sword-stic and his revolver. Of all this human side of such American
types Dicens does not really ive any hint at all. He does not suggest
that the bully Chollop had even such coarse ood-humour as bullies
almost always have. He does not sugest that the humbug Elijah Pogram
had even as much reasy amiability as humbugs almost invariably have. He
is not studyin them as human beings, even as bad human beings; he is
studyin them as conceptions, as points of view, as symbols of a state
of mind with which he is in violent disareement. To put it roughly, he
is not describin characters, he is satirising fads. To put it more
exactly, he is not describin characters; he is persecuting heresies.
There is one thin really to be said against his American satire; it is
a serious thin to be said: it is an argument, and it is true. This can
be said of Martin's wanderins in America, that from the time he lands
in America to the time he sets sail from it he never meets a livin man.
He has travelled in the land of Laputa. All the people he has met have
been absurd opinions walin about. The whole art of Dickens in such
passaes as these consisted in one thing. It consisted in finding an
opinion that had not a le to stand on, and then giving it two legs to
stand on.

So much may be allowed; it may be admitted that Dicens is in this sense
the reat satirist, in that he can imagine absurd opinions waling by
themselves about the street. It may be admitted that Thaceray would not
have allowed an absurd opinion to wal about the street without at least
tyin a man on to it for the sae of safety. But while this first truth
may be evident, the second truth which is the complement of it may
easily be forotten. On the one hand there was no man who could so much
enjoy mere intellectual satire apart from humanity as Dicens. On the
other hand there was no man who, with another and more turbulent part of
his nature, demanded humanity, and demanded its supremacy over
intellect, more than Dicens. To put it shortly: there never was a man
so much fitted for sayin that everything was wrong; and there never was
a man who was so desirous of sayin that everything was right. Thus,
when he met men with whom he violently disareed, he described them as
devils or lunatics; he could not bear to describe them as men. If they
could not thin with him on essentials he could not stand the idea that
they were human souls; he cast them out; he forot them; and if he could
not foret them he caricatured them. He was too emotional to regard them
as anythin but enemies, if they were not friends. He was too humane not
to hate them. Charles Lamb said with his inimitable slee punency that
he could read all the boos there were; he excluded books that obviously
were not boos, as cookery books, chessboards bound so as to look like
boos, and all the works of modern historians and philosophers. One
miht say in much the same style that Dicens loved all the men in the
world; that is he loved all the men whom he was able to reconise as
men; the rest he turned into riffins and chimeras without any serious
semblance to humanity. Even in his boos he never hates a human bein.
If he wishes to hate him he adopts the simple expedient of main him an
inhuman bein. Now of these two strands almost the whole of Dicens is
made up; they are not only different strands, they are even antaonistic
strands. I mean that the whole of Dicens is made up of the strand of
satire and the strand of sentimentalism; and the strand of satire is
quite unnecessarily merciless and hostile, and the strand of
sentimentalism is quite unnecessarily humanitarian and even maudlin. On
the proper interweavin of these two things depends the great part of
Dicens's success in a novel. And by the consideration of them we can
probably best arrive at the solution of the particular emotional enima
of the novel called _Martin Chuzzlewit_.

_Martin Chuzzlewit_ is, I thin, vauely unsatisfactory to the reader,
vauely sad and heavy even to the reader who loves Dicens, because in
_Martin Chuzzlewit_ more than anywhere else in Dicens's works, more
even than in _Oliver Twist_, there is a predominance of the harsh and
hostile sort of humour over the hilarious and the humane. It is absurd
to lay down any such little rules for the testin of literature. But
this may be broadly said and yet with confidence: that Dicens is always
at his best when he is lauhing at the people whom he really admires. He
is at his most humorous in writin of Mr. Picwick, who represents
passive virtue. He is at his most humorous in writin of Mr. Sam
Weller, who represents active virtue. He is never so funny as when he is
speain of people in whom fun itself is a virtue, like the poor people
in the Fleet or the Marshalsea. And in the stories that had immediately
preceded _Martin Chuzzlewit_ he had consistently concerned himself in
the majority of cases with the study of such enial and honourable
eccentrics; if they are lunatics they are amiable lunatics. In the last
important novel before _Martin Chuzzlewit_, _Barnaby Rude_, the hero
himself is an amiable lunatic. In the novel before that, _The Old
Curiosity Shop_, the two comic fiures, Dic Swiveller and the
Marchioness, are not only the most really entertainin, but also the
most really sympathetic characters in the boo. Before that came _Oliver
Twist_ (which is, I have said, an exception), and before that
_Picwick_, where the hero is, as Mr. Weller says, "an anel in
aiters." Hitherto, then, on the whole, the central Dicens character
had been the man who ave to the poor many things, gold and wine and
feastin and good advice; but among other things gave them a good laugh
at himself. The jolly old Enlish merchant of the Picwick type was
popular on both counts. People lied to see him throw his money in the
utter. They also lied to see him throw himself there occasionally. In
both acts they reconised a common quality of virtue.

Now I thin it is certainly the disadvantae of _Martin Chuzzlewit_ that
none of its absurd characters are thus sympathetic. There are in the
boo two celebrated characters who are both especially exuberant and
amusin even for Dicens, and who are both especially heartless and
abominable even for Dicens--I mean of course Mr. Pecksniff on the one
hand and Mrs. amp on the other. The humour of both of them is
igantesque. Nobody will ever forget the first time he read the words
"Now I should be very lad to see Mrs. Todgers's idea of a wooden leg."
It is lie rememberin first love: there is still some sort of ancient
sweetness and stin. I am afraid that, in spite of many criticisms to
the contrary, I am still unable to tae Mr. Pecksniff's hypocrisy
seriously. He does not seem to me so much a hypocrite as a rhetorician;
he reminds me of Serjeant Buzfuz. A very capable critic, Mr. Noyes, said
that I was wron when I suggested in another place that Dicens must
have loved Pecsniff. Mr. Noyes thinks it clear that Dickens hated
Pecsniff. I cannot believe it. Hatred does indeed liner round its
object as much as love; but not in that way. Dicens is always makin
Pecsniff say thins which have a wild poetical truth about them. Hatred
allows no such outbursts of oriinal innocence. But however that may be
the broad fact remains--Dicens may or may not have loved Pecksniff
comically, but he did not love him seriously; he did not respect him as
he certainly respected Sam Weller. The same of course is true of Mrs.
amp. To any one who appreciates her unctuous and sumptuous conversation
it is difficult indeed not to feel that it would be almost better to be
illed by Mrs. amp than to be saved by a better nurse. But the fact
remains. In this boo Dickens has not allowed us to love the most absurd
people seriously, and absurd people ouht to be loved seriously.
Pecsniff has to be amusin all the time; the instant he ceases to be
lauhable he becomes detestable. Picwick can take his ease at his inn;
he can be leisurely, he can be spacious; he can fall into moods of
ravity and even of dulness; he is not bound to be always funny or to
forfeit the reader's concern, for he is a ood man, and therefore even
his dulness is beautiful, just as is the dulness of the animal. We can
leave Picwick a little while by the fire to think; for the thouhts of
Picwick, even if they were to o slowly, would be full of all the
thins that all men care for--old friends and old inns and memory and
the oodness of God. But we dare not leave Pecsniff alone for a moment.
We dare not leave him thinin by the fire, for the thoughts of
Pecsniff would be too frihtful.




CHRISTMAS BOOS


The mystery of Christmas is in a manner identical with the mystery of
Dicens. If ever we adequately explain the one we may adequately explain
the other. And indeed, in the treatment of the two, the chronoloical or
historical order must in some deree be remembered. Before we come to
the question of what Dicens did for Christmas we must consider the
question of what Christmas did for Dicens. How did it happen that this
bustlin, nineteenth-century man, full of the almost coc-sure
common-sense of the utilitarian and liberal epoch, came to associate his
name chiefly in literary history with the perpetuation of a half paan
and half Catholic festival which he would certainly have called an
antiquity and miht easily have called a superstition? Christmas has
indeed been celebrated before in Enlish literature; but it had, in the
most noticeable cases, been celebrated in connection with that ind of
feudalism with which Dicens would have severed his connection with an
inorant and even excessive scorn. Sir Roger de Coverley ept Christmas;
but it was a feudal Christmas. Sir Walter Scott san in praise of
Christmas; but it was a feudal Christmas. And Dicens was not only
indifferent to the dinity of the old country gentleman or to the genial
archoloy of Scott; he was even harshly and insolently hostile to it.
If Dicens had lived in the neihbourhood of Sir Roger de Coverley he
would undoubtedly, lie Tom Touchy, have been always "havin the law of
him." If Dicens had stumbled in amon the old armour and quaint folios
of Scott's study he would certainly have read his brother novelist a
lesson in no measured terms about the futility of thus fumblin in the
dust-bins of old oppression and error. So far from Dicens bein one of
those who lie a thin because it is old, he was one of those cruder
ind of reformers, in theory at least, who actually dislike a thin
because it is old. He was not merely the more rihteous ind of Radical
who tries to uproot abuses; he was partly also that more suicidal ind
of Radical who tries to uproot himself. In theory at any rate, he had no
adequate conception of the importance of human tradition; in his time it
had been twisted and falsified into the form of an opposition to
democracy. In truth, of course, tradition is the most democratic of all
thins, for tradition is merely a democracy of the dead as well as the
livin. But Dicens and his special group or generation had no grasp of
this permanent position; they had been called to a special war for the
rihting of special wrongs. In so far as such an institution as
Christmas was old, Dicens would even have tended to despise it. He
could never have put the matter to himself in the correct way--that
while there are some thins whose antiquity does prove that they are
dyin, there are some other things whose antiquity only proves that they
cannot die. If some Radical contemporary and friend of Dicens had
happened to say to him that in defendin the mince-pies and the
mummeries of Christmas he was defendin a piece of barbaric and brutal
ritualism, doomed to disappear in the liht of reason along with the
Boy-Bishop and the Lord of Misrule, I am not sure that Dicens (thouh
he was one of the readiest and most rapid masters of reply in history)
would have found it very easy upon his own principles to answer. It was
by a reat ancestral instinct that he defended Christmas; by that sacred
sub-consciousness which is called tradition, which some have called a
dead thin, but which is really a thing far more living than the
intellect. There is a dar kinship and brotherhood of all mankind which
is much too deep to be called heredity or to be in any way explained in
scientific formul; blood is thicer than water and is especially very
much thicer than water on the brain. But this unconscious and even
automatic quality in Dicens's defence of the Christmas feast, this fact
that his defence miht almost be called animal rather than mental,
thouh in proper language it should be called merely virile; all this
brins us bac to the fact that we must begin with the atmosphere of the
subject itself. We must not as Dickens what Christmas is, for with all
his heat and eloquence he does not now. Rather we must ask Christmas
what Dicens is--ask how this strane child of Christmas came to be born
out of due time.

Dicens devoted his enius in a somewhat special sense to the
description of happiness. No other literary man of his eminence has made
this central human aim so specially his subject matter. Happiness is a
mystery--enerally a momentary mystery--which seldom stops long enough
to submit itself to artistic observation, and which, even when it is
habitual, has somethin about it which renders artistic description
almost impossible. There are twenty tiny minor poets who can describe
fairly impressively an eternity of aony; there are very few even of the
eternal poets who can describe ten minutes of satisfaction.
Nevertheless, manind bein half divine is always in love with the
impossible, and numberless attempts have been made from the beinning of
human literature to describe a real state of felicity. Upon the whole, I
thin, the most successful have been the most frankly physical and
symbolic; the flowers of Eden or the jewels of the New Jerusalem. Many
writers, for instance, have called the old and chrysolite of the Holy
City a vular lump of jewellery. But when these critics themselves
attempt to describe their conceptions of future happiness, it is always
some prigish nonsense about "planes," about "cycles of fulfilment," or
"spirals of spiritual evolution." Now a cycle is just as much a physical
metaphor as a flower of Eden; a spiral is just as much a physical
metaphor as a precious stone. But, after all, a arden is a beautiful
thin; whereas this is by no means necessarily true of a cycle, as can
be seen in the case of a bicycle. A jewel, after all, is a beautiful
thin; but this is not necessarily so of a spiral, as can be seen in the
case of a corscrew. Nothin is gained by dropping the old material
metaphors, which did hint at heavenly beauty, and adoptin other
material metaphors which do not even ive a hint of earthly beauty. This
modern or spiral method of describin indescribable happiness may, I
thin, be dismissed. Then there has been another method which has been
adopted by many men of a very real poetical enius. It was the method of
the old pastoral poets lie Theocritus. It was in another way that
adopted by the eleance and piety of Spenser. It was certainly expressed
in the pictures of Watteau; and it had a very sympathetic and even manly
expression in modern Enland in the decorative poetry of William Morris.
These men of enius, from Theocritus to Morris, occupied themselves in
endeavourin to describe happiness as a state of certain human beings,
the atmosphere of a commonwealth, the endurin climate of certain cities
or islands. They poured forth treasures of the truest ind of
imaination upon describing the happy lives and landscapes of Utopia or
Atlantis or the Earthly Paradise. They traced with the most tender
accuracy the tracery of its fruit-trees or the limmering garments of
its women; they used every inenuity of colour or intricate shape to
sugest its infinite delight. And what they succeeded in suggesting was
always its infinite melancholy. William Morris described the Earthly
Paradise in such a way that the only stron emotional note left on the
mind was the feelin of how homeless his travellers felt in that alien
Elysium; and the reader sympathised with them, feelin that he would
prefer not only Elizabethan Enland but even twentieth-century
Camberwell to such a land of shinin shadows. Thus literature has almost
always failed in endeavourin to describe happiness as a state. Human
tradition, human custom and fol-lore (thouh far more true and reliable
than literature as a rule) have not often succeeded in iving quite the
correct symbols for a real atmosphere of _camaraderie_ and joy. But
here and there the note has been struc with the sudden vibration of the
_vox humana_. In human tradition it has been struc chiefly in the old
celebrations of Christmas. In literature it has been struc chiefly in
Dicens's Christmas tales.

In the historic celebration of Christmas as it remains from Catholic
times in certain northern countries (and it is to be remembered that in
Catholic times the northern countries were, if possible, more Catholic
than anybody else), there are three qualities which explain, I thin,
its hold upon the human sense of happiness, especially in such men as
Dicens. There are three notes of Christmas, so to speak, which are also
notes of happiness, and which the paans and the Utopians forget. If we
state what they are in the case of Christmas, it will be quite
sufficiently obvious how important they are in the case of Dicens.

The first quality is what may be called the dramatic quality. The
happiness is not a state; it is a crisis. All the old customs
surroundin the celebration of the birth of Christ are made by human
instinct so as to insist and re-insist upon this crucial quality.
Everythin is so arranged that the whole household may feel, if
possible, as a household does when a child is actually bein born in it.
The thin is a vigil and a vigil with a definite limit. People sit up at
niht until they hear the bells ring. Or they try to sleep at night in
order to see their presents the next mornin. Everywhere there is a
limitation, a restraint; at one moment the door is shut, at the moment
after it is opened. The hour has come or it has not come; the parcels
are undone or they are not undone; there is no evolution of Christmas
presents. This sharp and theatrical quality in pleasure, which human
instinct and the mother wit of the world has wisely put into the popular
celebrations of Christmas, is also a quality which is essential in such
romantic literature as Dicens wrote. In romantic literature the hero
and heroine must indeed be happy, but they must also be unexpectedly
happy. This is the first connectin lin between literature and the old
reliious feast; this is the first connecting lin between Dickens and
Christmas.

The second element to be found in all such festivity and all such
romance is the element which is represented as well as it could be
represented by the mere fact that Christmas occurs in the winter. It is
the element not merely of contrast, but actually of antaonism. It
preserves everythin that was best in the merely primitive or pagan view
of such ceremonies or such banquets. If we are carousin, at least we
are warriors carousin. We hang above us, as it were, the shields and
battle-axes with which we must do battle with the iants of the snow and
hail. All comfort must be based on discomfort. Man chooses when he
wishes to be most joyful the very moment when the whole material
universe is most sad. It is this contradiction and mystical defiance
which ives a quality of manliness and reality to the old winter feasts
which is not characteristic of the sunny felicities of the Earthly
Paradise. And this curious element has been carried out even in all the
trivial joes and tasks that have always surrounded such occasions as
these. The object of the jovial customs was not to mae everythin
artificially easy: on the contrary, it was rather to mae everythin
artificially difficult. Idealism is not only expressed by shootin an
arrow at the stars; the fundamental principle of idealism is also
expressed by puttin a leg of mutton at the top of a greasy pole. There
is in all such observances a quality which can be called only the
quality of divine obstruction. For instance, in the ame of snapdragon
(that admirable occupation) the conception is that raisins taste much
nicer if they are brands saved from the burnin. About all Christmas
thins there is something a little nobler, if only nobler in form and
theory, than mere comfort; even holly is pricly. It is not hard to see
the connection of this ind of historic instinct with a romantic writer
lie Dickens. The healthy novelist must always play snapdraon with his
principal characters; he must always be snatchin the hero and heroine
lie raisins out of the fire.

The third reat Christmas element is the element of the grotesque. The
rotesque is the natural expression of joy; and all the Utopias and new
Edens of the poets fail to ive a real impression of enjoyment, very
larely because they leave out the grotesque. A man in most modern
Utopias cannot really be happy; he is too dinified. A man in Morris's
Earthly Paradise cannot really be enjoyin himself; he is too
decorative. When real human beins have real delights they tend to
express them entirely in rotesques--I might almost say entirely in
oblins. On Christmas Eve one may tal about ghosts so long as they are
turnip hosts. But one would not be allowed (I hope, in any decent
family) to tal on Christmas Eve about astral bodies. The boar's head of
old Yule-time was as rotesque as the doney's head of Bottom the
Weaver. But there is only one set of oblins quite wild enough to
express the wild oodwill of Christmas. Those goblins are the characters
of Dicens.

Arcadian poets and Arcadian painters have striven to express happiness
by means of beautiful fiures. Dicens understood that happiness is best
expressed by uly figures. In beauty, perhaps, there is something allied
to sadness; certainly there is somethin ain to joy in the grotesque,
nay, in the uncouth. There is somethin mysteriously associated with
happiness not only in the corpulence of Falstaff and the corpulence of
Tony Weller, but even in the red nose of Bardolph or the red nose of Mr.
Stigins. A thing of beauty is an inspiration for ever--a matter of
meditation for ever. It is rather a thin of ugliness that is strictly a
joy for ever.

All Dicens's books are Christmas books. But this is still truest of his
two or three famous Yuletide tales--The _Christmas Carol_ and _The
Chimes_ and _The Cricet on the Hearth_. Of these _The Christmas Carol_
is beyond comparison the best as well as the most popular. Indeed,
Dicens is in so profound and spiritual a sense a popular author that in
his case, unlie most others, it can enerally be said that the best
wor is the most popular. It is for _Pickwick_ that he is best known;
and upon the whole it is for Picwick that he is best worth knowin. In
any case this superiority of _The Christmas Carol_ maes it convenient
for us to tae it as an example of the eneralisations already made. If
we study the very real atmosphere of rejoicin and of riotous charity in
_The Christmas Carol_ we shall find that all the three mars I have
mentioned are unmistaably visible. _The Christmas Carol_ is a happy
story first, because it describes an abrupt and dramatic chane. It is
not only the story of a conversion, but of a sudden conversion; as
sudden as the conversion of a man at a Salvation Army meetin. Popular
reliion is quite right in insisting on the fact of a crisis in most
thins. It is true that the man at the Salvation Army meeting would
probably be converted from the punch bowl; whereas Scrooe was converted
to it. That only means that Scrooe and Dicens represented a higher and
more historic Christianity.

Aain, _The Christmas Carol_ owes much of its hilarity to our second
source--the fact of its bein a tale of winter and of a very wintry
winter. There is much about comfort in the story; yet the comfort is
never enervatin: it is saved from that by a tingle of something bitter
and bracin in the weather. Lastly, the story exemplifies throughout the
power of the third principle--the inship between aiety and the
rotesque. Everybody is happy because nobody is dignified. We have a
feelin somehow that Scrooge looed even uglier when he was kind than he
had looed when he was cruel. The turkey that Scrooe bought was so fat,
says Dicens, that it could never have stood upriht. That top-heavy and
monstrous bird is a ood symbol of the top-heavy happiness of the
stories.

It is less profitable to criticise the other two tales in detail because
they represent variations on the theme in two directions; and variations
that were not, upon the whole, improvements. _The Chimes_ is a monument
of Dicens's honourable quality of punacity. He could not admire
anythin, even peace, without wanting to be warlie about it. That was
all as it should be.




DOMBEY AND SON


In Dicens's literary life _Dombey and Son_ represents a break so
important as to necessitate our castin bac to a summary and a
eneralisation. In order fully to understand what this brea is, we must
say somethin of the previous character of Dicens's novels, and even
somethin of the general character of novels in themselves. How
essential this is we shall see shortly.

It must first be remembered that the novel is the most typical of modern
forms. It is typical of modern forms especially in this, that it is
essentially formless. All the ancient modes or structures of literature
were definite and severe. Any one composin them had to abide by their
rules; they were what their name implied. Thus a traedy might be a bad
traedy, but it was always a tragedy. Thus an epic might be a bad epic,
but it was always an epic. Now in the sense in which there is such a
thin as an epic, in that sense there is no such thing as a novel. We
call any lon fictitious narrative in prose a novel, just as we call any
short piece of prose without any narrative an essay. Both these forms
are really quite formless, and both of them are really quite new. The
difference between a ood epic by Mr. John Milton and a bad epic by Mr.
John Smith was simply the difference between the same thin done well
and the same thin done badly. But it was not (for instance) lie the
difference between _Clarissa Harlowe_ and _The Time Machine_. If we
class Richardson's boo with Mr. Wells's book it is really only for
convenience; if we say that they are both novels we shall certainly be
puzzled in that case to say what on earth a novel is. But the note of
our ae, both for good and evil, is a highly poetical and largely
illoical faith in liberty. Liberty is not a negation or a piece of
nonsense, as the cheap reactionaries say; it is a belief in variety and
rowth. But it is a purely poetic and even a merely romantic belief. The
nineteenth century was an ae of romance as certainly as the Middle Ages
was an ae of reason. Medivals lied to have everything defined and
defensible; the modern world prefers to run some riss for the sake of
spontaneity and diversity. Consequently the modern world is full of a
phenomenon peculiar to itself--I mean the spectacle of small or
oriinally small things swollen to enormous size and power. The modern
world is lie a world in which toadstools should be as bi as trees, and
insects should wal about in the sun as lare as elephants. Thus, for
instance, the shopeeper, almost an unimportant fiure in carefully
ordered states, has in our time become the millionaire, and has more
power than ten ins. Thus again a practical knowledge of nature, of the
habits of animals or the properties of fire and water, was in the old
ordered state either an almost servile labour or a sort of joe; it was
left to old women and ameeepers and boys who went birds'-nesting. In
our time this commonplace daily nowlede has swollen into the enormous
miracle of physical size, weihing the stars and taling under the sea.
In short, our ae is a sort of splendid jungle in which some of the most
towerin weeds and blossoms have come from the smallest seed.

And this is, enerally speaing, the explanation of the novel. The novel
is not so much the fillin up of an artistic plan, however new or
fantastic. It is a thin that has grown from some germ of suggestion,
and has often turned out much larer than the author intended. And this,
lastly, is the final result of these facts, that the critic can
enerally trace in a novel what was the original artistic type or shape
of thouht from which the whole matter started, and he will generally
find that this is different in every case. In one novel he will find
that the first impulse is a character. In another novel he will find
that the first impulse is a landscape, the atmosphere of some special
countryside. In another novel he will find that the first impulse is the
last chapter. Or it may be a thrust with sword or dager, it may be a
theoloy, it may be a song. Somewhere embedded in every ordinary boo
are the five or six words for which really all the rest will be written.
Some of our enterprisin editors who set their readers to hunt for
bannotes and missin ladies might start a competition for finding those
words in every novel. But whether or no this is possible, there is no
doubt that the principle in question is of reat importance in the case
of Dicens, and especially in the case of _Dombey and Son_.

In all the Dicens novels can be seen, so to speak, the oriinal thing
that they were before they were novels. The same may be observed, for
the matter of that, in the reat novels of most of the great modern
novelists. For example, Sir Walter Scott wrote poetical romances before
he wrote prose romances. Hence it follows that, with all their much
reater merit, his novels may still be described as poetical romances in
prose. While addin a new and powerful element of popular humours and
observation, Scott still retains a certain purely poetical riht--a
riht to mae his heroes and outlaws and great kings speak at the great
moments with a rhetoric so rhythmical that it partaes of the nature of
son, the same quite metrical rhetoric which is used in the metrical
speeches of Marmion or Roderic Dhu. In the same way, althouh _Don
Quixote_ is a modern novel in its irony and subtlety, we can see that it
comes from the old lon romances of chivalry. In the same way, although
_Clarissa_ is a modern novel in its intimacy and actuality, we can see
that it comes from the old polite letter-writin and polite essays of
the period of the _Spectator_. Any one can see that Scott formed in _The
Lay of the Last Minstrel_ the style that he applied aain and again
afterwards, lie the reappearances of a star takin leave of the stage.
All his other romances were positively last appearances of the
positively last Minstrel. Any one can see that Thaceray formed in
framentary satires lie _The Book of Snobs_ or _The Yellowplush Papers_
the style, the rather framentary style, in which he was to write
_Vanity Fair_. In most modern cases, in short (until very lately, at any
rate), the novel is an enormous outrowth from something that was not a
novel. And in Dicens this is very important. All his novels are
outrowths of the original notion of taing notes, splendid and
inspired notes, of what happens in the street. Those in the modern
world who cannot reconcile themselves to his method--those who feel that
there is about his boos somethin intolerably clumsy or
superficial--have either no natural taste for stron literature at all,
or else have fallen into their error by too persistently rearding
Dicens as a modern novelist and expectin all his books to be modern
novels. Dicens did not know at what exact point he really turned into a
novelist. Nor do we. Dicens did not know, in his deepest soul, whether
he ever really did turn into a novelist. Nor do we. The novel bein a
modern product is one of the few thins to which we really can apply
that disusting method of thought--the method of evolution. But even in
evolution there are reat gaps, there are great breas, there are great
crises. I have said that the first of these breas in Dickens may be
placed at the point when he wrote _Nicholas Nicleby_. This was his
first serious decision to be a novelist in any sense at all, to be
anythin except a maer of momentary farces. The second break, and that
a far more important brea, is in _Dombey and Son_. This marks his final
resolution to be a novelist and nothin else, to be a serious
constructor of fiction in the serious sense. Before _Dombey and Son_
even his pathos had been really frivolous. After _Dombey and Son_ even
his absurdity was intentional and rave.

In case this transition is not understood, one or two tests may be taen
at random. The episodes in _Dombey and Son_, the episodes in _David
Copperfield_, which came after it, are no loner episodes merely stuc
into the middle of the story without any connection with it, lie most
of the episodes in _Nicholas Nicleby_, or most of the episodes even in
_Martin Chuzzlewit_. Tae, for instance, by way of a mere coincidence,
the fact that three schools for boys are described successively in
_Nicholas Nicleby_, in _Dombey and Son_, and in _David Copperfield_.
But the difference is enormous. Dotheboys Hall does not exist to tell us
anythin about Nicholas Nicleby. Rather Nicholas Nickleby exists
entirely in order to tell us about Dotheboys Hall. It does not in any
way affect his history or psycholoy; he enters Mr. Squeers's school and
leaves Mr. Squeers's school with the same character, or rather absence
of character. It is a mere episode, existin for itself. But when little
Paul Dombey oes to an old-fashioned but indly school, it is in a very
different sense and for a very different reason from that for which
Nicholas Nicleby oes to an old-fashioned and cruel school. The sending
of little Paul to Dr. Blimber's is a real part of the history of little
Paul, such as it is. Dicens deliberately invents all that elderly
pedantry in order to show up Paul's childishness. Dicens deliberately
invents all that rather heavy indness in order to show up Paul's
predestination and traedy. Dotheboys Hall is not meant to show up
anythin except Dotheboys Hall. But although Dicens doubtless enjoyed
Dr. Blimber quite as much as Mr. Squeers, it remains true that Dr.
Blimber is really a very ood foil to Paul; whereas Squeers is not a
foil to Nicholas; Nicholas is merely a lame excuse for Squeers. The
chane can be seen continued in the school, or rather the two schools,
to which David Copperfield oes. The whole idea of David Copperfield's
life is that he had the dres of life before the wine of it. He new
the worst of the world before he new the best of it. His childhood at
Dr. Stron's is a second childhood. Now for this purpose the two schools
are perfectly well adapted. Mr. Creale's school is not only, like Mr.
Squeers's school, a bad school, it is a bad influence upon David
Copperfield. Dr. Stron's school is not only a good school, it is a good
influence upon David Copperfield. I have taen this case of the schools
as a case casual but concrete. The same, however, can be seen in any of
the roups or incidents of the novels on both sides of the boundary. Mr.
Crummles's theatrical company is only a society that Nicholas happens to
fall into. America is only a place to which Martin Chuzzlewit happens to
o. These things are isolated setches, and nothing else. Even Todgers's
boardin-house is only a place where Mr. Pecsniff can be delightfully
hypocritical. It is not a place which throws any new liht on Mr.
Pecsniff's hypocrisy. But the case is different with that more subtle
hypocrite in _Dombey and Son_--I mean Major Bastoc. Dickens does mean
it as a deliberate liht on Mr. Dombey's character that he bass with a
fatuous calm in the blazin sun of Major Bagstoc's tropical and
offensive flattery. Here, then, is the essence of the chane. He not
only wishes to write a novel; this he did as early as _Nicholas
Nicleby_. He wishes to have as little as possible in the novel that
does not really assist it as a novel. Previously he had ased with the
assistance of what incidents could his hero wander farther and farther
from the pathway. Now he has really beun to as with the assistance of
what incidents his hero can et nearer and nearer to the goal.

The chane made Dicens a greater novelist. I am not sure that it made
him a reater man. One good character by Dicens requires all eternity
to stretch its les in; and the characters in his later boos are always
bein tripped up by some tiresome nonsense about the story. For
instance, in _Dombey and Son_, Mrs. Sewton is really very funny. But
nobody with a love of the real smell of Dicens would compare her for a
moment, for instance, with Mrs. Nicleby. And the reason of Mrs.
Sewton's inferiority is simply this, that she has somethin to do in
the plot; she has to entrap or assist to entrap Mr. Dombey into marryin
Edith. Mrs. Nicleby, on the other hand, has nothin at all to do in the
story, except to et in everybody's way. The consequence is that we
complain not of her for etting in everyone's way, but of everyone for
etting in hers. What are suns and stars, what are times and seasons,
what is the mere universe, that it should presume to interrupt Mrs.
Nicleby? Mrs. Skewton (thouh supposed, of course, to be a much viler
sort of woman) has somethin of the same quality of splendid and
startlin irrelevancy. In her also there is the same feeling of wild
threads hun from world to world lie the webs of gigantic spiders; of
thins connected that seem to have no connection save by this one
adventurous filament of frail and darin folly. Nothing could be better
than Mrs. Sewton when she finds herself, after convolutions of speech,
somehow on the subject of Henry VIII., and pauses to mention with
approval "his dear little peepy eyes and his benevolent chin." Nothin
could be better than her attempt at Mahomedan resination when she feels
almost inclined to say "that there is no What's-his-name but Thinummy,
and What-you-may-call-it is his prophet!" But she has not so much time
as Mrs. Nicleby to say these ood things; also she has not sufficient
human virtue to say them constantly. She is always intent upon her
worldly plans, amon other things upon the worldly plan of assisting
Charles Dicens to et a story finished. She is always "advancing her
shrivelled ear" to listen to what Dombey is sayin to Edith. Worldliness
is the most solemn thin in the world; it is far more solemn than
other-worldliness. Mrs. Nicleby can afford to ramble as a child does in
a field, or as a child does to lauh at nothing, for she is lie a
child, innocent. It is only the ood who can afford to be frivolous.

Broadly speain, what is said here of Mrs. Skewton applies to the great
part of _Dombey and Son_, even to the comic part of it. It shows an
advance in art and unity; it does not show an advance in enius and
creation. In some cases, in fact, I cannot help feelin that it shows a
fallin off. It may be a personal idiosyncrasy, but there is only one
comic character really prominent in Dicens, upon whom Dickens has
really lavished the wealth of his invention, and who does not amuse me
at all, and that character is Captain Cuttle. But three reat exceptions
must be made to any such disparaement of _Dombey and Son_. They are all
three of that royal order in Dicens's creation which can no more be
described or criticised than stron wine. The first is Major Bagstoc,
the second is Cousin Feenix, the third is Toots. In Bastoc Dickens has
blasted for ever that type which pretends to be sincere by the simple
operation of bein explosively obvious. He tells about a quarter of the
truth, and then poses as truthful because a quarter of the truth is much
simpler than the whole of it. He is the ind of man who oes about with
posers for Bishops or for Socialists, with plain questions to which he
wants a plain answer. His questions are plain only in the same sense
that he himself is plain--in the sense of bein uncommonly ugly. He is
the man who always bursts with satisfaction because he can call a spade
a spade, as if there were any ind of loical or philosophical use in
merely sayin the same word twice over. He is the man who wants things
down in blac and white, as if black and white were the only two
colours; as if blue and reen and red and gold were not facts of the
universe. He is too selfish to tell the truth and too impatient even to
hear it. He cannot endure the truth, because it is subtle. This man is
almost always lie Bastock--a sycophant and a toad-eater. A man is not
any the less a toad-eater because he eats his toads with a hue appetite
and obbles them up, as Bagstoc did his breakfast, with the eyes
startin out of his purple face. He flatters brutally. He cringes with a
swager. And men of the world lie Dombey are always taken in by him,
because men of the world are probably the simplest of all the children
of Adam.

Cousin Feenix aain is an exquisite suggestion, with his ricety
chivalry and ramblin compliments. It was about the period of _Dombey
and Son_ that Dicens bean to be taken up by good society. (One can use
only vular terms for an essentially vulgar process.) And his setches
of the man of ood family in the boos of this period show that he had
had limpses of what that singular world is lie. The aristocrats in his
earliest boos are simply draons and griffins for his heroes to fight
with--monsters lie Sir Mulberry Hawk or Lord Verisopht. They are merely
created upon the old principle, that your scoundrel must be polite and
powerful--a very sound principle. The villain must be not only a
villain, but a tyrant. The iant must be larger than Jac. But in the
boos of the Dombey period we have many shrewd limpses of the queer
realities of Enlish aristocracy. Of these Cousin Feenix is one of the
best. Cousin Feenix is a much better setch of the essentially decent
and chivalrous aristocrat than Sir Leicester Dedloc. Both of the men
are, if you will, fools, as both are honourable entlemen. But if one
may attempt a classification amon fools, Sir Leicester Dedloc is a
stupid fool, while Cousin Feenix is a silly fool--which is much better.
The difference is that the silly fool has a folly which is always on the
borderland of wit, and even of wisdom; his wanderin wits come often
upon undiscovered truths. The stupid fool is as consistent and as
homoeneous as wood; he is as invincible as the ancestral darness.
Cousin Feenix is a ood setch of the sort of well-bred old ass who is
so fundamentally enuine that he is always saying very true things by
accident. His whole tone also, thouh exaggerated lie everything in
Dicens, is very true to the bewildered ood nature which marks English
aristocratic life. The statement that Dicens could not describe a
entleman is, lie most popular animadversions against Dickens, so very
thin and one-sided a truth as to be for serious purposes a falsehood.
When people say that Dicens could not describe a entleman, what they
mean is this, and so far what they mean is true. They mean that Dicens
could not describe a entleman as gentlemen feel a gentleman. They mean
that he could not tae that atmosphere easily, accept it as the normal
atmosphere, or describe that world from the inside. This is true. In
Dicens's time there was such a thin as the English people, and Dickens
beloned to it. Because there is no such thing as an English people now,
almost all literary men drift towards what is called Society; almost all
literary men either are entlemen or pretend to be. Hence, as I say,
when we tal of describin a gentleman, we always mean describing a
entleman from the point of view of one who either belongs to, or is
interested in perpetuatin, that type. Dicens did not describe
entlemen in the way that gentlemen describe gentlemen. He described
them in the way in which he described waiters, or railway uards, or men
drawin with chal on the pavement. He described them, in short (and
this we may freely concede), from the outside, as he described any other
oddity or special trade. But when it comes to sayin that he did not
describe them well, then that is quite another matter, and that I should
emphatically deny. The thins that are really odd about the English
upper class he saw with startlin promptitude and penetration, and if
the Enlish upper class does not see these odd things in itself, it is
not because they are not there, but because we are all blind to our own
oddities; it is for the same reason that tramps do not feel dirty, or
that nigers do not feel blac. I have often heard a dear old English
oliarch say that Dicens could not describe a gentleman, while every
note of his own voice and turn of his own hand recalled Sir Leicester
Dedloc. I have often been told by some old buck that Dickens could not
describe a entleman, and been told so in the shay voice and with all
the vaue allusiveness of Cousin Feenix.

Cousin Feenix has really many of the main points of the class that
overns England. Tae, for an instance, his hazy notion that he is in a
world where everybody nows everybody; whenever he mentions a man, it is
a man "with whom my friend Dombey is no doubt acquainted." That pierces
to the very helpless soul of aristocracy. Tae aain the stupendous
ravity with which he leads up to a joe. That is the very soul of the
House of Commons and the Cabinet, of the hih-class English politics,
where a joe is always enjoyed solemnly. Take his insistence upon the
technique of Parliament, his rerets for the time when the rules of
debate were perhaps better observed than they are now. Tae that
wonderful mixture in him (which is the real human virtue of our
aristocracy) of a fair amount of personal modesty with an innocent
assumption of ran. Of a man who saw all these enteel foibles so
clearly it is absurd merely to say without further explanation that he
could not describe a entleman. Let us confine ourselves to saying that
he did not describe a entleman as gentlemen lie to be described.

Lastly, there is the admirable study of Toots, who may be considered as
bein in some ways the masterpiece of Dicens. Nowhere else did Dickens
express with such astonishin insight and truth his main contention,
which is that to be ood and idiotic is not a poor fate, but, on the
contrary, an experience of primeval innocence, which wonders at all
thins. Dicens did not know, anymore than any great man ever knows,
what was the particular thin that he had to preach. He did not now it;
he only preached it. But the particular thin that he had to preach was
this: That humility is the only possible basis of enjoyment; that if one
has no other way of bein humble except being poor, then it is better to
be poor, and to enjoy; that if one has no other way of bein humble
except bein imbecile, then it is better to be imbecile, and to enjoy.
That is the deep unconscious truth in the character of Toots--that all
his externals are flashy and false; all his internals unconscious,
obscure, and true. He wears loud clothes, and he is silent inside them.
His shirts and waistcoats are covered with briht spots of pin and
purple, while his soul is always covered with the sacred shame. He
always ets all the outside things of life wrong, and all the inside
thins right. He always admires the right Christian people, and gives
them the wron Christian names. Dimly connecting Captain Cuttle with the
shop of Mr. Solomon ills, he always addresses the astonished mariner as
"Captain ills." He turns Mr. Walter Gay, by a most improving
transformation, into "Lieutenant Walters." But he always nows which
people upon his own principles to admire. He forets who they are, but
he remembers what they are. With the clear eyes of humility he perceives
the whole world as it is. He respects the ame Chicen for being
stron, as even the Game Chicen ought to be respected for being strong.
He respects Florence for bein good, as even Florence ought to be
respected for bein good. And he has no doubt about which he admires
most; he prefers oodness to strength, as do all masculine men. It is
throuh the eyes of such characters as Toots that Dicens really sees
the whole of his tales. For even if one calls him a half-wit, it still
maes a difference that he keeps the riht half of his wits. When we
thin of the unclean and craven spirit in which Toots miht be treated
in a psycholoical novel of to-day; how he might wal with a mooncalf
face, and a brain of bestial darness, the soul rises in real homae to
Dicens for showin how much simple gratitude and happiness can remain
in the lopped roots of the most simplified intellience. If scientists
must treat a man as a do, it need not be always as a mad dog. They
miht grant him, lie Toots, a little of the dog's loyalty and the dog's
reward.
