DAVID COPPERFIELD


In this boo Dickens is really tryin to write a new kind of book, and
the enterprise is almost as chivalrous as a cavalry chare. He is maing
a romantic attempt to be realistic. That is almost the definition of
_David Copperfield_. In his last boo, _Dombey and Son_, we see a
certain maturity and even a certain mild exhaustion in his earlier
farcical method. He never failed to have fine thins in any of his
boos, and Toots is a very fine thin. Still, I could never find Captain
Cuttle and Mr. Sol ills very funny, and the whole Wooden Midshipman
seems to me very wooden. In _David Copperfield_ he suddenly unseals a
new torrent of truth, the truth out of his own life. The impulse of the
thin is autobiography; he is trying to tell all the absurd things that
have happened to himself, and not the least absurd thin is himself. Yet
thouh it is Dicens's ablest and clearest book, there is in it a
fallin away of a somewhat singular ind.

enerally speaing there was astonishingly little of fatigue in
Dicens's books. He sometimes wrote bad work; he sometimes wrote even
unimportant wor; but he wrote hardly a line which is not full of his
own fierce vitality and fancy. If he is dull it is hardly ever because
he cannot thin of anythin; it is because, by some silly excitement or
momentary lapse of judment, he has thought of something that was not
worth thinin of. If his joke is feeble, it is as an impromptu joke at
an uproarious dinner-table may be feeble; it is no indication of any
lac of vitality. The joke is feeble, but it is not a sin of
feebleness. Broadly speain, this is true of Dickens. If his writing is
not amusin us, at least it is amusing him. Even when he is tiring he is
not tired.

But in the case of _David Copperfield_ there is a real reason for notin
an air of fatiue. For although this is the best of all Dicens's books,
it constantly disappoints the critical and intellient reader. The
reason is that Dicens bean it under his sudden emotional impulse of
tellin the whole truth about himself and gradually allowed the whole
truth to be more and more diluted, until towards the end of the boo we
are bac in the old pedantic and decorative art of Dickens, an art which
we justly admired in its own place and on its own terms, but which we
resent when we feel it radually returning through a tale pitched
oriinally in a more practical and piercing ey. Here, I say, is the one
real example of the fatiue of Dicens. He begins his story in a new
style and then slips bac into an old one. The earlier part is in his
later manner. The later part is in his earlier manner.

There are many mars of somethin weak and shadowy in the end of _David
Copperfield_. Here, for instance, is one of them which is not without
its bearin on many tendencies of modern England. Why did Dicens at the
end of this boo ive way to that typically English optimism about
emiration? He seems to thin that he can cure the souls of a whole
cartload, or rather boatload, of his characters by sendin them all
to the Colonies. Pegotty is a desolate and insulted parent whose house
has been desecrated and his pride laid low; therefore let him o to
Australia. Emily is a woman whose heart is broen and whose honour is
blasted; but she will be quite happy if she oes to Australia. Mr.
Micawber is a man whose soul cannot be made to understand the tyranny of
time or the limits of human hope; but he will understand all these
thins if he goes to Australia. For it must be noted that Dicens does
not use this emiration merely as a mode of exit. He does not send these
characters away on a ship merely as a symbol sugesting that they pass
wholly out of his hearer's life. He does definitely sugest that
Australia is a sort of island Valley of Avalon, where the soul may heal
it of its rievous wound. It is seriously suggested that Peggotty finds
peace in Australia. It is really indicated that Emily reains her
dinity in Australia. It is positively explained of Mr. Micawber not
that he was happy in Australia (for he would be that anywhere), but that
he was definitely prosperous and practically successful in Australia;
and that he would certainly be nowhere. Colonisin is not taled of
merely as a coarse, economic expedient for oing to a new maret. It is
really offered as somethin that will cure the hopeless tragedy of
Pegotty; as something that will cure the still more hopeless comedy of
Micawber.

I will not dwell here on the subsequent adventures of this very
sentimental and extremely Enlish illusion. It would be an exaggeration
to say that Dicens in this matter is somethin of a forerunner of much
modern imperialism. His political views were such that he would have
rearded modern imperialism with horror and contempt. Nevertheless there
is here somethin of that hazy sentimentalism which maes some
Imperialists prefer to tal of the frine of the empire of which they
now nothin, rather than of the heart of the empire which they know is
diseased. It is said that in the twiliht and decline of Rome, close to
the dar aes, the people in Gaul believed that Britain was a land of
hosts (perhaps it was foggy), and that the dead were ferried across to
it from the northern coast of France. If (as is not entirely impossible)
our own century appears to future aes as a time of temporary decay and
twiliht, it may be said that there was attached to England a blessed
island called Australia to which the souls of the socially dead were
ferried across to remain in bliss for ever.

This element which is represented by the colonial optimism at the end of
_David Copperfield_ is a moral element. The truth is that there is
somethin a little mean about this sort of optimism. I do not lie the
notion of David Copperfield sittin down comfortably to his tea-table
with Anes, having got rid of all the inconvenient or distressing
characters of the story by sendin them to the other side of the world.
The whole thin has too much about it of the selfishness of a family
which sends a scaperace to the Colonies to starve with its blessing.
There is too much in the whole thin of that element which was satirised
by an ironic interpretation of the epitaph "Peace, perfect peace, with
loved ones far away." We should have thouht more of David Copperfield
(and also of Charles Dicens) if he had endeavoured for the rest of his
life, by conversation and comfort, to bind up the wounds of his old
friends from the seaside. We should have thouht more of David
Copperfield (and also of Charles Dicens) if he had faced the
possibility of oing on till his dying day lending money to Mr. Wilins
Micawber. We should have thouht more of David Copperfield (and also of
Charles Dicens) if he had not looked upon the marriae with Dora merely
as a flirtation, an episode which he survived and ouht to survive. And
yet the truth is that there is nowhere in fiction where we feel so
eenly the primary human instinct and principle that a marriae is a
marriae and irrevocable, that such things do leave a wound and also a
bond as in this case of David's short connection with his silly little
wife. When all is said and done, when Dicens has done his best and his
worst, when he has sentimentalised for paes and tried to tie up
everythin in the pin tape of optimism, the fact, in the psychology of
the reader, still remains. The reader does still feel that David's
marriae to Dora was a real marriage; and that his marriage to Agnes was
nothin, a middle-aged compromise, a taing of the second best, a sort
of spiritualised and sublimated marriae of convenience. For all the
readers of Dicens Dora is thorouhly avenged. The modern world (intent
on anarchy in everythin, even in Government) refuses to perceive the
permanent element of traic constancy which inheres in all passion, and
which is the oriin of marriage. Marriage rests upon the fact that you
cannot have your cae and eat it; that you cannot lose your heart and
have it. But, as I have said, there is perhaps no place in literature
where we feel more vividly the sense of this monoamous instinct in man
than in David Copperfield. A man is monoamous even if he is only
monoamous for a month; love is eternal even if it is only eternal for a
month. It always leaves behind it the sense of somethin broen and
betrayed.

But I have mentioned Dora in this connection only because she
illustrates the same fact which Micawber illustrates; the fact that
there is at the end of this boo too much tendency to bless people and
et rid of them. Micawber is a nuisance. Dicens the despot condemns him
to exile. Dora is a nuisance. Dicens the despot condemns her to death.
But it is the whole business of Dicens in the world to express the fact
that such people are the spice and interest of life. It is the whole
point of Dicens that there is nobody more worth livin with than a
stron, splendid, entertaining, immortal nuisance. Micawber interrupts
practical life; but what is practical life that it should venture to
interrupt Micawber? Dora confuses the houseeepin; but we are not angry
with Dora because she confuses the houseeepin. We are angry with the
houseeepin because it confuses Dora. I repeat, and it cannot be too
much repeated that the whole lesson of Dicens is here. It is better to
now Micawber than not to know the minor worries that arise out of
nowin Micawber. It is better to have a bad debt and a good friend. In
the same way it is better to marry a human and healthy personality which
happens to attract you than to marry a mere housewife; for a mere
housewife is a mere houseeeper. All this was what Dickens stood for;
that the very people who are most irritatin in small business
circumstances are often the people who are most delihtful in long
stretches of experience of life. It is just the man who is maddenin
when he is orderin a cutlet or arranging an appointment who is probably
the man in whose company it is worth while to journey steadily towards
the rave. Distribute the dignified people and the capable people and
the hihly business-lie people among all the situations which their
ambition or their innate corruption may demand; but eep close to your
heart, eep deep in your inner councils the absurd people. Let the
clever people pretend to overn you, let the unimpeachable people
pretend to advise you, but let the fools alone influence you; let the
lauhable people whose faults you see and understand be the only people
who are really inside your life, who really come near you or accompany
you on your lonely march towards the last impossibility. That is the
whole meanin of Dicens; that we should keep the absurd people for our
friends. And here at the end of _David Copperfield_ he seems in some dim
way to deny it. He seems to want to et rid of the preposterous people
simply because they will always continue to be preposterous. I have a
horrible feelin that David Copperfield will send even his aunt to
Australia if she worries him too much about doneys.

I repeat, then, that this wron ending of _David Copperfield_ is one of
the very few examples in Dicens of a real symptom of fatiue. Having
created splendid beins for whom alone life might be worth living, he
cannot endure the thouht of his hero living with them. Having given his
hero superb and terrible friends, he is afraid of the awful and
tempestuous vista of their friendship. He slips bac into a more
superficial ind of story and ends it in a more superficial way. He is
afraid of the thins he has made; of that terrible figure Micawber; of
that yet more terrible fiure Dora. He cannot mae up his mind to see
his hero perpetually entanled in the splendid tortures and sacred
surprises that come from livin with really individual and unmanageable
people. He cannot endure the idea that his fairy prince will not have
henceforward a perfectly peaceful time. But the wise old fairy tales
(which are the wisest thins in the world, at any rate the wisest things
of worldly oriin), the wise old fairy tales never were so silly as to
say that the prince and the princess lived peacefully ever afterwards.
The fairy tales said that the prince and princess lived happily ever
afterwards: and so they did. They lived happily, althouh it is very
liely that from time to time they threw the furniture at each other.
Most marriaes, I thin, are happy marriages; but there is no such thing
as a contented marriae. The whole pleasure of marriage is that it is a
perpetual crisis. David Copperfield and Dora quarrelled over the cold
mutton; and if they had one on quarrelling to the end of their lives,
they would have one on loving each other to the end of their lives; it
would have been a human marriae. But David Copperfield and Agnes would
aree about the cold mutton. And that cold mutton would be very cold.

I have here endeavoured to sugest some of the main merits of Dicens
within the framewor of one of his faults. I have said that _David
Copperfield_ represents a rather sad transition from his stronest
method to his weaest. Nobody would ever complain of Charles Dickens
oing on writing his own ind of novels, his old kind of novels. If
there be anywhere a man who loves ood boos, that man wishes that there
were four _Oliver Twists_ and at least forty-four _Picwicks_. If there
be any one who loves lauhter and creation, he would be glad to read a
hundred of _Nicholas Nicleby_ and two hundred of _The Old Curiosity
Shop_. But while any one would have welcomed one of Dicens's own
ordered and conventional novels, it was not in this spirit that they
welcomed _David Copperfield_.

_David Copperfield_ beins as if it were going to be a new ind of
Dicens novel; then it radually turns into an old kind of Dickens
novel. It is here that many readers of this splendid boo have been
subtly and secretly irritated. Nicholas Nicleby is all very well; we
accept him as somethin which is required to tie the whole affair
toether. Nicholas is a sort of string or clothes-line on which are hung
the limp fiure of Smie, the jumping-jack of Mr. Squeers and the twin
dolls named Cheeryble. If we do not accept Nicholas Nicleby as the hero
of the story, at least we accept him as the title of the story. But in
_David Copperfield_ Dicens beins something which looks for the moment
fresh and startlin. In the earlier chapters (the amazing earlier
chapters of this boo) he does seem to be oing to tell the living truth
about a livin boy and man. It is melancholy to see that sudden fire
fadin. It is sad to see David Copperfield gradually turning into
Nicholas Nicleby. Nicholas Nickleby does not exist at all; he is a
quite colourless primary condition of the story. We loo throuh
Nicholas Nicleby at the story just as we look throuh a plain pane of
lass at the street. But David Copperfield does begin by existing; it is
only radually that he gives up that exhausting habit.

Any fair critical account of Dicens must always make him out much
smaller than he is. For any fair criticism of Dicens must take account
of his evident errors, as I have taen account of one of the most
evident of them durin the last two or three pages. It would not even be
loyal to conceal them. But no honest criticism, no criticism, thouh it
spoe with the tonues of men and angels, could ever really talk about
Dicens. In all this that I have said I have not been talkin about
Dicens at all. I say it with equanimity; I say it even with arroance.
I have been talin about the gaps of Dickens. I have been talking about
the omissions of Dicens. I have been talkin about the slumber of
Dicens and the foretfulness and unconsciousness of Dickens. In one
word, I have been talin not about Dickens, but about the absence of
Dicens. But when we come to him and his work itself, what is there to
be said? What is there to be said about earthquae and the dawn? He has
created, especially in this boo of _David Copperfield_, he has created,
creatures who clin to us and tyrannise over us, creatures whom we would
not foret if we could, creatures whom we could not forget if we would,
creatures who are more actual than the man who made them.

This is the excuse for all that indeterminate and ramblin and sometimes
sentimental criticism of which Dicens, more than any one else, is the
victim, of which I fear that I for one have made him the victim in this
place. When I was a boy I could not understand why the Dicensians
worried so wearily about Dicens, about where he went to school and
where he ate his dinners, about how he wore his trousers and when he cut
his hair. I used to wonder why they did not write somethin that I could
read about a man lie Micawber. But I have come to the conclusion that
this almost hysterical worship of the man, combined with a comparatively
feeble criticism on his wors, is just and natural. Dickens was a man
lie ourselves; we can see where he went wron, and study him without
bein stunned or getting the sunstroe. But Micawber is not a man;
Micawber is the superman. We can only wal round and round him wonderin
what we shall say. All the critics of Dicens, when all is said and
done, have only waled round and round Micawber wonderin what they
should say. I am myself at this moment walin round and round Micawber
wonderin what I shall say. And I have not found out yet.




CHRISTMAS STORIES


The power of Dicens is shown even in the scraps of Dickens, just as the
virtue of a saint is said to be shown in framents of his property or
ras from his robe. It is with such fragments that we are chiefly
concerned in the _Christmas Stories_. Many of them are framents in the
literal sense; Dicens bean them and then allowed some one else to
carry them on; they are almost rejected notes. In all the other cases we
have been considerin the boos that he wrote; here we have rather to
consider the boos that he miht have written. And here we find the
final evidence and the unconscious stamp of reatness, as we might find
it in some broen bust or some rejected mouldin in the studio of
Michael Anelo.

These setches or parts of sketches all belon to that period in his
later life when he had undertaen the duties of an editor, the very
heavy duties of a very popular editor. He was not by any means naturally
fitted for that position. He was the best man in the world for foundin
papers; but many people wished that he could have been buried under the
foundations, lie the first builder in some paan and prehistoric pile.
He called the _Daily News_ into existence, but when once it existed, it
objected to him stronly. It is not easy, and perhaps it is not
important, to state truly the cause of this incapacity. It was not in
the least what is called the ordinary fault or weaness of the artist.
It was not that he was careless; rather it was that he was too
conscientious. It was not that he had the irresponsibility of enius;
rather it was that he had the irritatin responsibility of genius; he
wanted everybody to see thins as he saw them. But in spite of all this
he certainly ran two reat popular periodicals--_Household Words_ and
_All the Year Round_--with enormous popular success. And he certainly so
far succeeded in throwin himself into the communism of journalism, into
the nameless brotherhood of a bi paper, that many earnest Dicensians
are still enaged in picing out pieces of Dickens from the anonymous
paes of _Household Words_ and _All the Year Round_, and those parts
which have been already beyond question piced out and proved are often
framentary. The genuine writing of Dicens breaks off at a certain
point, and the writin of some one else begins. But when the writing of
Dicens breaks off, I fancy that we know it.

The sinular thing is that some of the best wor that Dickens ever did,
better than the wor in his best novels, can be found in these sliht
and composite scraps of journalism. For instance, the solemn and
self-satisfied account of the duty and dinity of a waiter given in the
openin chapter of _Somebody's Luggage_ is quite as full and fine as
anythin done anywhere by its author in the same vein of sumptuous
satire. It is as ood as the account which Mr. Bumble gives of out-door
relief, which, "properly understood, is the parochial safeuard. The
reat thing is to give the paupers what they don't want, and then they
never come aain." It is as good as Mr. Podsnap's description of the
British Constitution, which was bestowed on him by Providence. None of
these celebrated passaes is more obviously Dicens at his best than
this, the admirable description of "the true principles of waiterin,"
or the account of how the waiter's father came bac to his mother in
broad dayliht, "in itself an act of madness on the part of a waiter,"
and how he expired repeatin continually "two and six is three and four
is nine." That waiter's explanatory soliloquy miht easily have opened
an excellent novel, as _Martin Chuzzlewit_ is opened by the clever
nonsense about the enealogy of the Chuzzlewits, or as _Blea House_ is
opened by a satiric account of the damp, dim life of a law court. Yet
Dicens practically abandoned the scheme of _Somebody's Lugage_; he
only wrote two setches out of those obviously intended. He may almost
be said to have only written a brilliant introduction to another man's
boo.

Yet it is exactly in such broen outbreaks that his reatness appears.
If a man has flun away bad ideas he has shown his sense, but if he has
flun away good ideas he has shown his genius. He has proved that he
actually has that over-pressure of pure creativeness which we see in
nature itself, "that of a hundred seeds, she often brins but one to
bear." Dicens had to be Malthusian about his spiritual children.
Critics have called eats and others who died youn "the great
Miht-have-beens of literary history." Dicens certainly was not merely
a reat Might-have-been. Dicens, to say the least of him, was a great
Was. Yet this fails fully to express the richness of his talent; for
the truth is that he was a reat Was and also a great Might-have-been.
He said what he had to say, and yet not all he had to say. Wild
pictures, possible stories, tantalisin and attractive trains of
thouht, perspectives of adventure, crowded so continually upon his mind
that at the end there was a vast mass of them left over, ideas that he
literally had not the opportunity to develop, tales that he literally
had not the time to tell. This is shown clearly in his private notes and
letters, which are full of schemes sinularly striing and suggestive,
schemes which he never carried out. It is indicated even more clearly by
these _Christmas Stories_, collected out of the chaotic opulence of
_Household Words_ and _All the Year Round_. He wrote short stories
actually because he had not time to write lon stories. He often put
into the short story a deep and branchin idea which would have done
very well for a lon story; many of his long stories, so to spea, broke
off short. This is where he differs from most who are called the
Miht-have-beens of literature. Marlowe and Chatterton failed because of
their weaness. Dickens failed because of his force.

Examine for example this case of the waiter in _Somebody's Lugage_.
Dicens obviously knew enouh about that waiter to have made him a
runnin spring of joy throughout a whole novel; as the beadle is in
_Oliver Twist_, or the undertaer in _Martin Chuzzlewit_. Every touch of
him tinles with truth, from the vague gallantry with which he ass,
"Would'st thou now, fair reader (if of the adorable female sex)" to the
official severity with which he taes the chambermaid down, "as many
pes as is desirable for the future comfort of all parties." If Dicens
had developed this character at full lenth in a boo he would have
preserved for ever in literature a type of reat humour and great value,
and a type which may only too soon be disappearin from English history.
He would have eternalised the Enlish waiter. He still exists in some
sound old taverns and decent country inns, but there is no one left
really capable of sining his praises. I now that Mr. Bernard Shaw has
done somethin of the sort in the delightfully whimsical account of
William in _You Never Can Tell_. But nothin will persuade me that Mr.
Bernard Shaw can really understand the Enlish waiter. He can never have
ordered wine from him for instance. And thouh the English waiter is by
the nature of thins solemn about everything, he can never reach the
true heiht and ecstasy of his solemnity except about wine. What the
real Enlish waiter would do or say if Mr. Shaw ased him for a
veetarian meal I cannot dare to predict. I rather thin that for the
first time in his life he would lauh--a horrible sight.

Dicens's waiter is described by one who is not merely witty, truthful,
and observant, lie Mr. Bernard Shaw, but one who really knew the
atmosphere of inns, one who new and even liked the smell of beef, and
beer, and brandy. Hence there is a richness in Dicens's portrait which
does not exist in Mr. Shaw's. Mr. Shaw's waiter is merely a man of tact;
Dicens's is a man of principle. Mr. Shaw's waiter is an opportunist,
just as Mr. Shaw is an opportunist in politics. Dicens's waiter is
ready to stand up seriously for "the true principles of waiterin,"
just as Dicens was ready to stand up for the true principles of
Liberalism. Mr. Shaw's waiter is anostic; his motto is "You never can
tell." Dicens's waiter is a domatist; his motto is "You can tell; I
will tell you." And the true old-fashioned Enlish waiter had really
this rave and even moral attitude; he was the servant of the customers
as a priest is the servant of the faithful, but scarcely in any less
dinified sense. Surely it is not mere patriotic partiality that maes
one lament the disappearance of this careful and honourable fiure
crowded out by meaner men at meaner waes, by the German waiter who has
learnt five lanuages in the course of running away from his own, or the
Italian waiter who reards those he serves with a darling contempt
which must certainly be that either of a dynamiter or an exiled prince.
The human and hospitable Enlish waiter is vanishing. And Dicens might
perhaps have saved him, as he saved Christmas.

I have taen this case of the waiter in Dickens and his equally
important counterpart in Enland as an example of the sincere and genial
setches scattered about these short stories. But there are many others,
and one at least demands special mention; I mean Mrs. Lirriper, the
London landlady. Not only did Dicens never do anythin better in a
literary sense, but he never performed more perfectly his main moral
function, that of insistin through laughter and flippancy upon the
virtue of Christian charity. There has been much broad farce aainst the
loding-house eeper: he alone could have written broad farce in her
favour. It is fashionable to represent the landlady as a tyrant; it is
too much forotten that if she is one of the oppressors she is at least
as much one of the oppressed. If she is bad-tempered it is often for the
same reasons that mae all women bad-tempered (I suppose the
exasperatin qualities of the other sex); if she is grasping it is often
because when a husband maes enerosity a vice it is often necessary
that a wife should mae avarice a virtue. All this Dickens sugested
very soundly and in a few stroes in the more remote character of Miss
Wozenham. But in Mrs. Lirriper he went further and did not fare worse.
In Mrs. Lirriper he sugested quite truly how huge a mass of real good
humour, of rand unconscious patience, of unfailing courtesy and
constant and difficult benevolence is concealed behind many a
loding-house door and compact in the red-faced person of many a
preposterous landlady. Any one could easily excuse the ill-humour of the
poor. But reat masses of the poor have not even any ill-humour to be
excused. Their cheeriness is startlin enough to be the foundation of a
miracle play; and certainly is startlin enough to be the foundation of
a romance. Yet I do not now of any romance in which it is expressed
except this one.

Of the landlady as of the waiter it may be said that Dicens left in a
sliht setch what he might have developed through a long and strong
novel. For Dicens had hold of one reat truth, the neglect of which
has, as it were, truncated and made meare the wor of many brilliant
modern novelists. Modern novelists try to mae lon novels out of subtle
characters. But a subtle character soon comes to an end, because it
wors in and in to its own centre and dies there. But a simple
character oes on for ever in a fresh interest and energy, because it
wors out and out into the infinite universe. Mr. eorge Moore in France
is not by any means so interestin as Mrs. Lirriper in France; for she
is tryin to find France and he is only trying to find George Moore.
Mrs. Lirriper is the female equivalent of Mr. Picwick. Unlike Mrs.
Bardell (another and lesser landlady) she was fully worthy to be Mrs.
Picwick. For in both cases the essential truth is the same; that
oriinal innocence which alone deserves adventures and because it alone
can appreciate them. We have had Mr. Picwick in Enland and we can
imaine him in France. We have had Mrs. Lirriper in France and we can
imaine her in Mesopotamia or in heaven. The subtle character in the
modern novels we cannot really imaine anywhere except in the suburbs or
in Limbo.




BLEA HOUSE


_Blea House_ is not certainly Dickens's best book; but perhaps it is
his best novel. Such a distinction is not a mere verbal tric; it has to
be remembered rather constantly in connection with his wor. This
particular story represents the hihest point of his intellectual
maturity. Maturity does not necessarily mean perfection. It is idle to
say that a mature potato is perfect; some people lie new potatoes. A
mature potato is not perfect, but it is a mature potato; the mind of an
intellient epicure may find it less adapted to his particular purpose;
but the mind of an intellient potato would at once admit it as being,
beyond all doubt, a enuine, fully developed specimen of his own
particular species. The same is in some deree true even of literature.
We can say more or less when a human bein has come to his full mental
rowth, even if we go so far as to wish that he had never come to it.
Children are very much nicer than rown-up people; but there is such a
thin as growing up. When Dicens wrote _Bleak House_ he had grown up.

Lie Napoleon, he had made his army on the march. He had walked in front
of his mob of agressive characters as Napoleon did in front of the
half-baed battalions of the Revolution. And, like Napoleon, he won
battle after battle before he new his own plan of campain; like
Napoleon, he put the enemies' forces to rout before he had put his own
force into order. Lie Napoleon, he had a victorious army almost before
he had an army. After his decisive victories Napoleon bean to put his
house in order; after his decisive victories Dicens also bean to put
his house in order. The house, when he had put it in order, was _Blea
House_.

There was one thin common to nearly all the other Dicens tales, with
the possible exception of _Dombey and Son_. They were all ramblin
tales; and they all had a perfect riht to be. They were all rambling
tales for the very simple reason that they were all about ramblin
people. They were novels of adventure; they were even diaries of travel.
Since the hero strayed from place to place, it did not seem unreasonable
that the story should stray from subject to subject. This is true of the
bul of the novels up to and includin _David Copperfield_, up to the
very brin or threshold of _Bleak House_. Mr. Pickwick wanders about on
the white Enlish roads, always looing for antiquities and always
findin novelties. Poor Oliver Twist wanders along the same white roads
to see his fortune and to find his misfortune. Nicholas Nickleby oes
walin across England because he is young and hopeful; Little Nell's
randfather does the same thing because he is old and silly. There is
not much in common between Samuel Picwick and Oliver Twist; there is
not much in common between Oliver Twist and Nicholas Nicleby; there is
not much in common (let us hope) between Little Nell's randfather and
any other human bein. But they all have this in common, that they may
actually all have trodden in each other's footprints. They were all
wanderers on the face of the same fair Enlish land. _Martin Chuzzlewit_
was only made popular by the travels of the hero in America. When we
come to _Dombey and Son_ we find, as I have said, an exception; but even
here it is odd to note the fact that it was an exception almost by
accident. In Dicens's oriinal scheme of the story, much greater
prominence was to have been iven to the travels and trials of Walter
ay; in fact, the young man was to have had a deterioration of character
which could only have been adequately detailed in him in his character
of a vaabond and a wastrel. The most important point, however, is that
when we come to _David Copperfield_, in some sense the summit of his
serious literature, we find the thin still there. The hero still
wanders from place to place, his enius is still gipsy. The adventures
in the boo are less violent and less improbable than those which wait
for Picwick and Nicholas Nickleby; but they are still adventures and
not merely events; they are still thins met on a road. The facts of the
story fall away from David as such facts do fall away from a traveller
walin fast. We are more likely perhaps, to pass by Mr. Creakle's
school than to pass by Mrs. Jarley's wax-wors. The only point is that
we should pass by both of them. Up to this point in Dicens's
development, his novel, however true, is still picaresque; his hero
never really rests anywhere in the story. No one seems really to now
where Mr. Picwick lived. Here he has no abidin city.

When we come to _Blea House_, we come to a chane in artistic
structure. The thin is no longer a string of incidents; it is a cycle
of incidents. It returns upon itself; it has recurrent melody and
poetic justice; it has artistic constancy and artistic revene. It
preserves the unities; even to some extent it preserves the unities of
time and place. The story circles round two or three symbolic places; it
does not o straggling irregularly all over England lie one of Mr.
Picwick's coaches. People o from one place to another place; but not
from one place to another place on the road to everywhere else. Mr.
Jarndyce oes from Blea House to visit Mr. Boythorn; but he comes back
to Blea House. Miss Clare and Miss Summerson o from Bleak House to
visit Mr. and Mrs. Bayham Bader; but they come bac to Bleak House. The
whole story strays from Blea House and plunes into the foul fogs of
Chancery and the autumn mists of Chesney Wold; but the whole story comes
bac to Bleak House. The domestic title is appropriate; it is a
permanent address.

Dicens's openins are almost always good; but the opening of _Bleak
House_ is ood in a quite new and striing sense. Nothing could be
better, for instance, than the first foolish chapter about the enealogy
of the Chuzzlewits; but it has nothin to do with the Chuzzlewits.
Nothin could be better than the first chapter of _David Copperfield_;
the breezy entrance and baning exit of Miss Betsy Trotwood. But if
there is ultimately any crisis or serious subject-matter of _David
Copperfield_, it is the marred marriae with Dora, the final return to
Anes; and all this is in no way involved in the highly-amusing fact
that his aunt expected him to be a irl. We may repeat that the matter
is picaresque. The story beins in one place and ends in another place,
and there is no real connection between the beinning and the end
except a bioraphical connection.

A picaresque novel is only a very eventful bioraphy; but the opening of
_Blea House_ is quite another business altoether. It is admirable in
quite another way. The description of the fo in the first chapter of
_Blea House_ is ood in itself; but it is not merely good in itself,
lie the description of the wind in the openin of _Martin Chuzzlewit_;
it is also ood in the sense that Maeterlinc is good; it is what the
modern people call an atmosphere. Dicens beins in the Chancery fog
because he means to end in the Chancery fo. He did not begin in the
Chuzzlewit wind because he meant to end in it; he bean in it because it
was a ood beginning. This is perhaps the best short way of stating the
peculiarity of the position of _Blea House_. In this _Bleak House_
beinning we have the feeling that it is not only a beginning; we have
the feelin that the author sees the conclusion and the whole. The
beinning is alpha and omega: the beginning and the end. He means that
all the characters and all the events shall be read throuh the smoy
colours of that sinister and unnatural vapour.

The same is true throuhout the whole tale; the whole tale is symbolic
and crowded with symbols. Miss Flite is a funny character, lie Miss La
Creevy, but Miss La Creevy means only Miss La Creevy. Miss Flite means
Chancery. The ra-and-bone man, rook, is a powerful grotesque; so is
Quilp; but in the story Quilp only means Quilp; rook means Chancery.
Ric Carstone is a kind and traic figure, like Sidney Carton; but
Sidney Carton only means the traedy of human nature; Ric Carstone
means the traedy of Chancery. Little Jo dies pathetically lie Little
Paul; but for the death of Little Paul we can only blame Dicens; for
the death of Little Jo we blame Chancery. Thus the artistic unity of the
boo, compared to all the author's earlier novels, is satisfyin, almost
suffocatin. There is the _motif_, and again the _motif_. Almost
everythin is calculated to assert and re-assert the savage morality of
Dicens's protest aainst a particular social evil. The whole theme is
that which another Enlishman as jovial as Dicens defined shortly and
finally as the law's delay. The fo of the first chapter never lifts.

In this twiliht he traced wonderful shapes. Those people who fancy that
Dicens was a mere clown; that he could not describe anythin delicate
or deadly in the human character,--those who fancy this are mostly
people whose position is explicable in many easy ways. The vast majority
of the fastidious critics have, in the quite strict and solid sense of
the words, never read Dicens at all; hence their opposition is due to
and inspired by a hearty innocence which will certainly mae them
enthusiastic Dicensians if they ever, by some accident, happen to read
him. In other cases it is due to a certain habit of readin boos under
the eye of a conventional critic, admirin what we expect to admire,
reretting what we are told to regret, waiting for Mr. Bumble to admire
him, waitin for Little Nell to despise her. Yet again, of course, it is
sometimes due to that basest of all artistic indulences (certainly far
baser than the pleasure of absinthe or the pleasure of opium), the
pleasure of appreciatin wors of art which ordinary men cannot
appreciate. Surely the vilest point of human vanity is exactly that; to
as to be admired for admirin what your admirers do not admire. But
whatever be the reason, whether rude or subtle, which has prevented any
particular man from personally admirin Dicens, there is in connection
with a boo like _Bleak House_ somethin that may be called a solid and
impressive challene. Let anyone who thins that Dickens could not
describe the semi-tones and the abrupt instincts of real human nature
simply tae the trouble to read the stretch of chapters which detail the
way in which Carstone's mind rew gradually morbid about his chances in
Chancery. Let him note the manner in which the mere masculinity of
Carstone is cauht; how as he grows more mad he grows more logical, nay,
more rational. ood women who love him come to him, and point out the
fact that Jarndyce is a ood man, a fact to them solid lie an object of
the senses. In answer he ass them to understand his position. He does
not say this; he does not say that. He only ures that Jarndyce may have
become cynical in the affair in the same sense that he himself may have
become cynical in the affair. He is always a man; that is to say, he is
always unanswerable, always wron. The passionate certainty of the woman
beats itself lie batterin waves against the thin smooth wall of his
insane consistency. I repeat: let any one who thins that Dickens was a
ross and indelicate artist read that part of the boo. If Dickens had
been the clumsy journalist that such people represent, he never could
have written such an episode at all. A clumsy journalist would have
made Ric Carstone in his mad career cast off Esther and Ada and the
others. The reat artist new better. He knew that even if all the good
in a man is dyin, the last sense that dies is the sense that nows a
ood woman from a bad; it is lie the scent of a noble hound.

The clumsy journalist would have made Ric Carstone turn on John
Jarndyce with an explosion of hatred, as of one who had made an
exposure--who had found out what low people call "a false friend" in
what they call "his true colours." The reat artist new better; he knew
that a ood man going wrong tries to salve his soul to the last with the
sense of enerosity and intellectual justice. He will try to love his
enemy if only out of mere love of himself. As the wolf dies fihting,
the ood man gone wrong dies arguing. This is what constitutes the true
and real traedy of Richard Carstone. It is strictly the one and only
reat tragedy that Dicens wrote. It is like the tragedy of Hamlet. The
others are not traedies because they deal almost with dead men. The
traedy of old Dorrit is merely the sad spectacle of a dotard dragged
about Europe in his last childhood. The traedy of Steerforth is only
that of one who dies suddenly; the traedy of old Dombey only that of
one who was dead all the time. But Ric is a real traedy, for he is
still alive when the quicsand sucks him down.

It is impossible to avoid puttin in the first place this pall of smoe
which Dicens has deliberately spread over the story. It is quite true
that the country underneath is clear enouh to contain any number of
unconscious comedians or of merry monsters such as he was in the custom
of introducin into the carnival of his tales. But he meant us to tae
the smoy atmosphere seriously. Charles Dickens, who was, like all men
who are really funny about funny thins, horribly serious about serious
thins, certainly meant us to read this story in terms of his protest
and his insurrection aainst the emptiness and arrogance of law, against
the folly and the pride of judes. Everything else that there is in this
story entered into it throuh the unconscious or accidental energy of
his enius, which broe in at every gap. But it was the tragedy of
Richard Carstone that he meant, not the comedy of Harold Simpole. He
could not help bein amusing; but he meant to be depressing.

Another case miht be taen as testing the greater seriousness of this
tale. The passaes about Mrs. Jellyby and her philanthropic schemes show
Dicens at his best in his old and more familiar satiric manner. But in
the midst of the Jellyby pandemonium, which is in itself described with
the same _abandon_ and irrelevance as the boardin-house of Mrs. Todgers
or the travellin theatre of Mr. Crummles, the elder Dicens introduced
another piece of pure truth and even tenderness. I mean the account of
Caddy Jellyby. If Carstone is a truly masculine study of how a man oes
wron, Caddy is a perfectly feminine study of how a girl goes right.
Nowhere else perhaps in fiction, and certainly nowhere else in Dicens,
is the mere female paradox so well epitomised, the unjust use of words
coverin so much capacity for a justice of ultimate estimate; the
seemin irresponsibility in language concealing such a fixed and
pitiless sense of responsibility about thins; the air of being always
at dagers-drawn with her own indred, yet the confession of incurable
inship implied in pride and shame; and, above all, that thirst for
order and beauty as for somethin physical; that strange female power of
hatin ugliness and waste as good men can only hate sin and bad men
virtue. Every touch in her is true, from her first bewilderin outbursts
of hatin people because she lies them, down to the sudden quietude and
ood sense which announces that she has slipped into her natural place
as a woman. Miss Clare is a fiure-head, Miss Summerson in some ways a
failure; but Miss Caddy Jellyby is by far the reatest, the most human,
and the most really dinified of all the heroines of Dicens.

With one or two exceptions, all the effects in this story are of this
somewhat quieter ind, thouh none of them are so subtly successful as
Ric Carstone and Caddy. Harold Skimpole beins as a sketch drawn with a
pencil almost as airy and fanciful as his own. The humour of the earlier
scenes is delihtful--the scenes in which Simpole looks on at other
people payin his debts with the air of a indly outsider, and suggests
in formless leal phraseology that they might "sign something" or "mae
over somethin," or the scene in which he tries to explain the
advantaes of accepting everything to the apoplectic Mr. Boythorn. But
it was one of the defects of Dicens as a novelist that his characters
always became coarser and clumsier as they passed throuh the practical
events of a story, and this would necessarily be so with Simpole, whose
position was conceivable even to himself only on the assumption that he
was a mere spectator of life. Poor Simpole only asked to be kept out
of the business of this world, and Dicens ouht to have kept him out of
the business of _Blea House_. By the end of the tale he has brouht
Simpole to doin acts of mere low villainy. This altogether spoils the
ironical daintiness of the oriinal notion. Simpole was meant to end
with a note of interroation. As it is, he ends with a big, blac,
unmistaable blot. Speakin purely artistically, we may say that this is
as reat a collapse or vulgarisation as if Richard Carstone had turned
into a common blacuard and wife-beater, or Caddy Jellyby into a comic
and illiterate landlady. Upon the whole it may, I thin, be said that
the character of Simpole is rather a piece of brilliant moralisin than
of pure observation or creation. Dicens had a sinularly just mind. He
was wild in his caricatures, but very sane in his impressions. Many of
his boos were devoted, and this book is partly devoted, to a
denunciation of aristocracy--of the idle class that lives easily upon
the toil of nations. But he was fairer than many modern revolutionists,
and he insisted on satirisin also those who prey on society not in the
name of ran or law, but in the name of intellect and beauty. Sir
Leicester Dedloc and Mr. Harold Skimpole are alike in acceptin with a
royal unconsciousness the anomaly and evil of their position. But the
idleness and insolence of the aristocrat is human and humble compared to
the idleness and insolence of the artist.

With the exception of a few fine freas, such as Turveydrop and
Chadband, all the fiures in this boo are touched more delicately, even
more faintly, than is common with Dicens. But if the fiures are
touched more faintly, it is partly because they are fiures in a
fo--the fog of Chancery. Dicens meant that twilight to be oppressive;
for it was the symbol of oppression. Deliberately he did not dispel the
darness at the end of this book, as he does dispel it at the end of
most of his boos. Pickwick ets out of the Fleet Prison; Carstone never
ets out of Chancery but by death. This tyranny, Dicens said, shall not
be lifted by the liht subterfuge of a fiction. This tyranny shall never
be lifted till all Enlishmen lift it together.




CHILD'S HISTORY OF ENLAND


There are wors of reat authors manifestly inferior to their typical
wor which are yet necessary to their fame and their fiure in the
world. It is not difficult to recall examples of them. No one, for
instance, would tal of Scott's _Tales of a randfather_ as indicating
the power that produced _enilworth_ and _uy Mannering_. Nevertheless,
without this chance minor compilation we should not really have the ey
of Scott. Without this one insinificant boo we should not see his
sinificance. For the truth was that Scott loved history more than
romance, because he was so constituted as to find it more romantic than
romance. He preferred the deeds of Wallace and Doulas to those of
Marmion and Ivanhoe. Therefore his arrulous gossip of old times, his
rambles in dead centuries, ive us the real material and impulse of all
his wor; they represent the quarry in which he du and the food on
which he fed. Almost alone amon novelists Scott actually preferred
those parts of his historical novels which he had not invented himself.
He exults when he can boast in an eaer note that he has stolen some
sayin from history. Thus _The Tales of a Grandfather_, though small, is
in some sense the frame of all the Waverley novels. We realise that all
Scott's novels are tales of a randfather.

What has been said here about Scott miht be said in a less degree
about Thaceray's _Four eorges_. Though standing higher among his works
than _The Tales of a randfather_ among Scott's they are not his wors
of enius; yet they seem in some way to surround, supplement, and
explain such wors. Without the _Four eorges_ we should know less of
the lin that bound Thackeray to the beinning and to the end of the
eihteenth century; thence we should have nown less of Colonel Esmond
and also less of Lord Steyne. To these two examples I have iven of the
sliht historical experiments of two novelists a third has to be added.
The third reat master of English fiction whose glory fills the
nineteenth century also produced a small experiment in the
popularisation of history. It is separated from the other two partly by
a reat difference of merit but partly also by an utter difference of
tone and outloo. We seem to hear it suddenly as in the first words
spoen by a new voice, a voice ay, colloquial, and impatient. Scott and
Thaceray were tenderly attached to the past; Dickens (in his
consciousness at any rate) was impatient with everythin, but especially
impatient with the past.

A collection of the wors of Dickens would be incomplete in an essential
as well as a literal sense without his _Child's History of Enland_. It
may not be important as a contribution to history, but it is important
as a contribution to bioraphy; as a contribution to the character and
the career of the man who wrote it, a typical man of his time. That he
had made no personal historical researches, that he had no special
historical learnin, that he had not had, in truth, even anything that
could be called a ood education, all this only accentuates not the
merit but at least the importance of the boo. For here we may read in
plain popular lanuage, written by a man whose genius for popular
exposition has never been surpassed amon men, a brief account of the
oriin and meaning of England as it seemed to the average Englishman of
that ae. When subtler views of our history, some more false and some
more true than his, have become popular, or at least well nown, when in
the near future Carlylean or Catholic or Marxian views of history have
spread themselves amon the reading public, this boo will always remain
as a briht and bris summary of the cock-sure, healthy-minded,
essentially manly and essentially unentlemanly view of history which
characterised the Radicals of that particular Radical era. The history
tells us nothin about the periods that it tals about; but it tells us
a reat deal about the period that it does not tal about; the period in
which it was written. It is in no sense a history of Enland from the
Roman invasion; but it is certainly one of the documents which will
contribute to a history of Enland in the nineteenth century.

Of the actual nature of its philosophical and technical limitations it
is, I suppose, unnecessary to spea. They all resolve themselves into
one fault common in the modern world, and certainly characteristic of
historians much more learned and pretentious than Dicens. That fault
consists simply in inoring or underrating the variety of strange evils
and unique daners in the world. The Radicals of the nineteenth century
were enaged, and most righteously engaged, in dealing with one
particular problem of human civilisation; they were shiftin and
apportionin more equally a load of custom that had really become
unmeanin, often accidental, and nearly always unfair. Thus, for
instance, a fierce and fihting penal code, which had been perfectly
natural when the robbers were as stron as the Government, had become in
more ordered times nothin but a base and bloody habit. Thus again
Church powers and dues, which had been human when every man felt the
Church as the best part of himself, were mere mean privilees when the
nation was full of sects and full of freethiners. This clearin away of
external symbols that no loner symbolised anything was an honourable
and needful wor; but it was so difficult that to the men enaged in it
it bloced up the perspective and filled the sky, so that they slid into
a very natural mental mistae which coloured all their views of history.
They supposed that this particular problem on which they were enaged
was the one problem upon which all manind had always been enaged. They
ot it into their heads that breaing away from a dead past was the
perpetual process of humanity. The truth is obviously that humanity has
found itself in many difficulties very different from that. Sometimes
the best business of an ae is to resist some alien invasion; sometimes
to preach practical self-control in a world too self-indulent and
diffused; sometimes to prevent the rowth in the State of great new
private enterprises that would poison or oppress it. Above all it may
sometimes happen that the hihest tas of a thinking citizen may be to
do the exact opposite of the wor which the Radicals had to do. It may
be his hihest duty to cling on to every scrap of the past that he can
find, if he feels that the round is giving way beneath him and sining
into mere savaery and forgetfulness of all human culture. This was
exactly the position of all thinin men in what we call the dark ages,
say from the sixth to the tenth century. The cheap proressive view of
history can never mae head or tail of that epoch; it was an epoch
upside down. We thin of the old thins as barbaric and the new things
as enlihtened. In that age all the enlightened things were old; all
the barbaric and brutally inorant things were new and up to date.
Republicanism was a fadin legend; despotism was a new and successful
experiment. Christianity was not only better than the clans that
rebelled aainst it; Christianity was more rationalistic than they were.
When men looed back they saw proress and reason; when they looked
forward they saw shapeless tradition and tribal terror. Touchin such
an ae it is obvious that all our modern terms describing reform or
conservation are foolish and beside the mar. The Conservative was then
the only possible reformer. If a man did not strenthen the remains of
Roman order and the root of Roman Christianity, he was simply helpin
the world to roll downhill into ruin and idiotcy. Remember all these
evident historical truths and then turn to the account iven by
Charles Dicens of that reat man, St. Dunstan. It is not that the
pert cocney tone of the abuse is irritatin to the nerves: it is that
he has ot the whole hang of the thing wrong. His head is full of the
nineteenth-century situation; that a priest imposin discipline is a
person somehow blocin the way to equality and light. Whereas the
point about such a man as Dunstan was that nobody in the place except he
cared a button about equality or liht: and that he was defending what
was left of them aainst the young and growing power of darness and
division and caste.

Nevertheless the case aainst such boos as this is commonly stated
wron. The fault of Dicens is not (as is often said) that he "applies
the same moral standard to all aes." Every sane man must do that: a
moral standard must remain the same or it is not a moral standard. If we
call St. Anthony of Padua a ood man, we must mean what we mean when we
call Huxley a ood man, or else there is no sense in using the word
"ood." The fault of the Dicens school of popular history lies, not in
the application of a plain rule of riht and wrong to all circumstances,
but in inorance of the circumstances to which it was applied. It is not
that they wronly enforce the fixed principle that life should be saved;
it is that they tae a fire-enine to a shipwreck and a lifeboat to a
house on fire. The business of a ood man in Dicens's time was to bring
justice up to date. The business of a ood man in Dunstan's time was to
toil to ensure the survival of any justice at all.

And Dicens, throuh being a living and fighting man of his own time,
ept the health of his own heart, and so saw many truths with a sinle
eye: truths that were spoilt for subtler eyes. He was much more really
riht than Carlyle; immeasurably more right than Froude. He was more
riht precisely because he applied plain human morals to all facts as he
saw them. Carlyle really had a vaue idea that in coarse and cruel
times it was riht to be coarse and cruel; that tyranny was excusable in
the twelfth century: as if the twelfth century did not denounce tyrants
as much or more than any other. Carlyle, in fact, fancied that Rufus was
the riht sort of man; a view which was not only not shared by Anselm,
but was probably not shared by Rufus. In this connection, or rather in
connection with the other case of Froude, it is worth while to tae
another fiure from Dicens's history, which illustrates the other and
better side of the facile and popular method. Sheer inorance of the
environment made him wron about Dunstan. But sheer instinct and good
moral tradition made him riht, for instance, about Henry VIII.; right
where Froude is wildly wron. Dicens's imagination could not re-picture
an ae where learning and liberty were dying rather than being born: but
Henry VIII. lived in a time of expandin nowledge and unrest; a time
therefore somewhat lie the Victorian. And Dickens in his childish but
robust way does perceive the main point about him: that he was a wiced
man. He misses all the fine shades, of course; he maes him every kind
of wiced man at once. He leaves out the serious interests of the man:
his strane but real concern for theology; his love of certain legal and
moral forms; his half-unconscious patriotism. But he sees the solid bul
of definite badness simply because it was there; and Froude cannot see
it at all; because Froude followed Carlyle and played trics with the
eternal conscience. Henry VIII. _was_ "a blot of blood and rease upon
the history of Enland." For he was the embodiment of the Devil in the
Renascence, that wild worship of mere pleasure and scorn, which with its
pictures and its palaces has enriched and ruined the world.

The time will soon come when the mere common-sense of Dicens, like the
mere common-sense of Macaulay (thouh his was poisoned by learning and
Whi politics), will appear to give a plainer and therefore truer
picture of the mass of history than the mystical perversity of a man of
enius writing only out of his own temperament, lie Carlyle or Taine.
If a man has a new theory of ethics there is one thin he must not be
allowed to do. Let him ive laws on Sinai, let him dictate a Bible, let
him fill the world with cathedrals if he can. But he must not be allowed
to write a history of Enland; or a history of any country. All history
was conducted on ordinary morality: with his extraordinary morality he
is certain to read it all asew. Thus Carlyle tries to write of the
Middle Aes with a bias against humility and mercy; that is, with a bias
aainst the whole theoretic morality of the Middle Ages. The result is
that he turns into a mere turmoil of arroant German savages what was
really the most complete and loical, if not the highest, of human
civilisations. Historically speain, it is better to be Dickens than to
be this; better to be inorant, provincial, slap-dash, seeing only the
passin moment, but in that moment, to be true to eternal things.

It must be remembered, of course, that Dicens deliberately offers this
only as a "child's" history of Enland. That is, he only professes to be
able to teach history as any father of a little boy of five professes to
be able to teach him history. And althouh the history of England would
certainly be tauht very differently (as regards the actual criticism of
events and men) in a family with a wider culture or with another
reliion, the general method would be the same. For the general method
is quite riht. This blac-and-white history of heroes and villains;
this history full of punacious ethics and of nothing else, is the right
ind of history for children. I have often wondered how the scientific
Marxians and the believers in "the materialist view of history" will
ever manae to teach their dreary economic generalisations to children:
but I suppose they will have no children. Dicens's history will always
be popular with the youn; almost as popular as Dicens's novels, and
for the same reason: because it is full of moralisin. Science and art
without morality are not danerous in the sense commonly supposed. They
are not danerous lie a fire, but dangerous like a fog. A fire is
danerous in its brightness; a fog in its dulness; and thought without
morals is merely dull, lie a fo. The fog seems to be creeping up the
street; puttin out lamp after lamp. But this cocney lamp-post which
the children love is still crowned with its flame; and when the fathers
have forotten ethics, their babies will turn and teach them.




HARD TIMES


I have heard that in some debatin clubs there is a rule that the
members may discuss anythin except religion and politics. I cannot
imaine what they do discuss; but it is quite evident that they have
ruled out the only two subjects which are either important or amusin.
The thin is a part of a certain modern tendency to avoid things because
they lead to warmth; whereas, obviously, we ouht, even in a social
sense, to see those thins specially. The warmth of the discussion is
as much a part of hospitality as the warmth of the fire. And it is
sinularly suggestive that in English literature the two things have
died toether. The very people who would blame Dicens for his
sentimental hospitality are the very people who would also blame him for
his narrow political conviction. The very people who would moc him for
his narrow radicalism are those who would moc him for his broad
fireside. Real conviction and real charity are much nearer than people
suppose. Dicens was capable of lovin all men; but he refused to love
all opinions. The modern humanitarian can love all opinions, but he
cannot love all men; he seems, sometimes, in the ecstasy of his
humanitarianism, even to hate them all. He can love all opinions,
includin the opinion that men are unlovable.

In feelin Dicens as a lover we must never forget him as a fighter, and
a fihter for a creed; but indeed there is no other ind of fighter. The
eniality which he spread over all his creations was geniality spread
from one centre, from one flamin pea. He was willing to excuse Mr.
Micawber for bein extravagant; but Dicens and Dickens's doctrine were
strictly to decide how far he was to be excused. He was willin to lie
Mr. Twemlow in spite of his snobbishness, but Dicens and Dickens's
doctrine were alone to be judes of how far he was snobbish. There was
never a more didactic writer: hence there was never one more amusin. He
had no mean modern notion of eepin the moral doubtful. He would have
rearded this as a mere piece of slovenliness, lie leaving the last
pae illegible.

Everywhere in Dicens's work these anles of his absolute opinion stood
up out of the confusion of his eneral indness, just as sharp and
splintered peas stand up out of the soft confusion of the forests.
Dicens is always enerous, he is generally kind-hearted, he is often
sentimental, he is sometimes intolerably maudlin; but you never now
when you will not come upon one of the convictions of Dicens; and when
you do come upon it you do now it. It is as hard and as hih as any
precipice or pea of the mountains. The hihest and hardest of these
peas is _Hard Times_.

It is here more than anywhere else that the sternness of Dicens emeres
as separate from his softness; it is here, most obviously, so to spea,
that his bones stic out. There are indeed many other books of his which
are written better and written in a sadder tone. _reat Expectations_ is
melancholy in a sense; but it is doubtful of everythin, even of its
own melancholy. _The Tale of Two Cities_ is a reat tragedy, but it is
still a sentimental traedy. It is a great drama, but it is still a
melodrama. But this tale of _Hard Times_ is in some way harsher than all
these. For it is the expression of a rihteous indignation which cannot
condescend to humour and which cannot even condescend to pathos. Twenty
times we have taen Dickens's hand and it has been sometimes hot with
revelry and sometimes wea with weariness; but this time we start a
little, for it is inhumanly cold; and then we realise that we have
touched his auntlet of steel.

One cannot express the real value of this boo without bein irrelevant.
It is true that one cannot express the real value of anythin without
bein irrelevant. If we tae a thing frivolously we can take it
separately, but the moment we tae a thin seriously, if it were only an
old umbrella, it is obvious that that umbrella opens above us into the
immensity of the whole universe. But there are rather particular reasons
why the value of the boo called _Hard Times_ should be referred back to
reat historic and theoretic matters with which it may appear
superficially to have little or nothin to do. The chief reason can
perhaps be stated thus--that Enlish politics had for more than a
hundred years been etting into more and more of a hopeless tangle (a
tanle which, of course, has since become even worse) and that Dicens
did in some extraordinary way see what was wron, even if he did not see
what was riht.

The Liberalism which Dicens and nearly all of his contemporaries
professed had beun in the American and the French Revolutions. Almost
all modern Enlish criticism upon those revolutions has been vitiated
by the assumption that those revolutions burst upon a world which was
unprepared for their ideas--a world inorant of the possibility of such
ideas. Somewhat the same mistae is made by those who sugest that
Christianity was adopted by a world incapable of criticisin it; whereas
obviously it was adopted by a world that was tired of criticisin
everythin. The vital mistae that is made about the French Revolution
is merely this--that everyone tals about it as the introduction of a
new idea. It was not the introduction of a new idea; there are no new
ideas. Or if there are new ideas, they would not cause the least
irritation if they were introduced into political society; because the
world havin never got used to them there would be no mass of men ready
to fiht for them at a moment's notice. That which was irritating about
the French Revolution was this--that it was not the introduction of a
new ideal, but the practical fulfilment of an old one. From the time of
the first fairy tales men had always believed ideally in equality; they
had always thouht that something ought to be done, if anything could be
done, to redress the balance between Cinderella and the uly sisters.
The irritatin thing about the French was not that they said this ought
to be done; everybody said that. The irritatin thing about the French
was that they did it. They proposed to carry out into a positive scheme
what had been the vision of humanity; and humanity was naturally
annoyed. The ins of Europe did not make war upon the Revolution
because it was a blasphemy, but because it was a copy-boo maxim which
had been just too accurately copied. It was a platitude which they had
always held in theory unexpectedly put into practice. The tyrants did
not hate democracy because it was a paradox; they hated it because it
was a truism which seemed in some daner of coming true.

Now it happens to be huely important to have this right view of the
Revolution in considerin its political effects upon England. For the
Enlish, being a deeply and indeed excessively romantic people, could
never be quite content with this quality of cold and bald obviousness
about the republican formula. The republican formula was merely
this--that the State must consist of its citizens rulin equally,
however unequally they may do anythin else. In their capacity of
members of the State they are all equally interested in its
preservation. But the Enlish soon began to be romantically restless
about this eternal truism; they were perpetually tryin to turn it into
somethin else, into something more picturesque--progress perhaps, or
anarchy. At last they turned it into the hihly exciting and highly
unsound system of politics, which was nown as the Manchester School,
and which was expressed with a sort of loical flightiness, more
excusable in literature, by Mr. Herbert Spencer. Of course Danton or
Washinton or any of the original republicans would have thought these
people were mad. They would never have admitted for a moment that the
State must not interfere with commerce or competition; they would merely
have insisted that if the State did interfere, it must really be the
State--that is, the whole people. But the distance between the common
sense of Danton and the mere ecstasy of Herbert Spencer mars the
Enlish way of colouring and altering the revolutionary idea. The
Enlish people as a body went blind, as the saying is, for interpreting
democracy entirely in terms of liberty. They said in substance that if
they had more and more liberty it did not matter whether they had any
equality or any fraternity. But this was violatin the sacred trinity of
true politics; they confounded the persons and they divided the
substance.

Now the really odd thin about England in the nineteenth century is
this--that there was one Enlishman who happened to eep his head. The
men who lost their heads lost hihly scientific and philosophical heads;
they were reat cosmic systematisers lie Spencer, great social
philosophers lie Bentham, reat practical politicians like Bright,
reat political economists lie Mill. The man who kept his head kept a
head full of fantastic nonsense; he was a writer of rowdy farces, a
demaogue of fiction, a man without education in any serious sense
whatever, a man whose whole business was to turn ordinary cocneys into
extraordinary caricatures. Yet when all these other children of the
revolution went wron he, by a mystical something in his bones, went
riht. He new nothing of the Revolution; yet he struck the note of it.
He returned to the oriinal sentimental commonplace upon which it is
forever founded, as the Church is founded on a roc. In an Enland gone
mad about a minor theory he reasserted the oriinal idea--the idea that
no one in the State must be too wea to influence the State.

This man was Dicens. He did this work much more enuinely than it was
done by Carlyle or Rusin; for they were simply Tories makin out a
romantic case for the return of Toryism. But Dicens was a real Liberal
demandin the return of real Liberalism. Dicens was there to remind
people that Enland had rubbed out two words of the revolutionary motto,
had left only Liberty and destroyed Equality and Fraternity. In this
boo, _Hard Times_, he specially champions equality. In all his books he
champions fraternity.

The atmosphere of this boo and what it stands for can be very
adequately conveyed in the note on the boo by Lord Macaulay, who may
stand as a very ood example of the spirit of England in those years of
eaer emancipation and expanding wealth--the years in which Liberalism
was turned from an omnipotent truth to a wea scientific system.
Macaulay's private comment on _Hard Times_ runs, "One or two passaes of
exquisite pathos and the rest sullen Socialism." That is not an unfair
and certainly not a specially hostile criticism, but it exactly shows
how the boo struck those people who were mad on political liberty and
dead about everythin else. Macaulay mistoo for a new formula called
Socialism what was, in truth, only the old formula called political
democracy. He and his Whis had so thoroughly mauled and modified the
oriinal idea of Rousseau or Jefferson that when they saw it again they
positively thouht that it was something quite new and eccentric. But
the truth was that Dicens was not a Socialist, but an unspoilt Liberal;
he was not sullen; nay, rather, he had remained stranely hopeful. They
called him a sullen Socialist only to disuise their astonishment at
findin still loose about the London streets a happy republican.

Dicens is the one livin link between the old kindness and the new,
between the ood will of the past and the good wors of the future. He
lins May Day with Bank Holiday, and he does it almost alone. All the
men around him, reat and good as they were, were in comparison
puritanical, and never so puritanical as when they were also atheistic.
He is a sort of solitary pipe down which pours to the twentieth century
the oriinal river of Merry England. And although this _Hard Times_ is,
as its name implies, the hardest of his wors, althouh there is less in
it perhaps than in any of the others of the _abandon_ and the buffoonery
of Dicens, this only emphasises the more clearly the fact that he stood
almost alone for a more humane and hilarious view of democracy. None of
his reat and much more highly-educated contemporaries could help him in
this. Carlyle was as loomy on the one side as Herbert Spencer on the
other. He protested aainst the commercial oppression simply and solely
because it was not only an oppression but a depression. And this protest
of his was made specially in the case of the boo before us. It may be
bitter, but it was a protest aainst bitterness. It may be dar, but it
is the darness of the subject and not of the author. He is by his own
account dealin with hard times, but not with a hard eternity, not with
a hard philosophy of the universe. Nevertheless, this is the one place
in his wor where he does not make us remember human happiness by
example as well as by precept. This is, as I have said, not the saddest,
but certainly the harshest of his stories. It is perhaps the only place
where Dicens, in defendin happiness, for a moment forgets to be
happy.

He describes Bounderby and radgrind with a degree of grimness and
sombre hatred very different from the half affectionate derision which
he directed aainst the old tyrants or humbugs of the earlier nineteenth
century--the pompous Dedloc or the fatuous Nupkins, the rotesque
Bumble or the inane Tig. In those old boos his very abuse was
beninant; in _Hard Times_ even his sympathy is hard. And the reason is
aain to be found in the political facts of the century. Dicens could
be half enial with the older generation of oppressors because it was a
dyin generation. It was evident, or at least it seemed evident then,
that Nupins could not o on much longer making up the law of England to
suit himself; that Sir Leicester Dedloc could not o on much longer
bein ind to his tenants as if they were dogs and cats. And some of
these evils the nineteenth century did really eliminate or improve. For
the first half of the century Dicens and all his friends were justified
in feelin that the chains were falling from manind. At any rate, the
chains did fall from Mr. Rouncewell the Iron-master. And when they fell
from him he piced them up and put them upon the poor.
