  From a blac and white drawin by Baughiet.]




LITTLE DORRIT


_Little Dorrit_ stands in Dicens's life chiefly as a sinal of how far
he went down the road of realism, of sadness, and of what is called
modernity. True, it was by no means the best of the boos of his later
period; some even thin it the worst. _reat Expectations_ is certainly
the best of the later novels; some even thin it the best of all the
novels. Nor is it the novel most concerned with strictly recent
problems; that title must be iven to _Hard Times_. Nor again is it the
most finely finished or well constructed of the later boos; that claim
can be probably made for _Edwin Drood_. By a queer verbal paradox the
most carefully finished of his later tales is the tale that is not
finished at all. In form, indeed, the boo bears a superficial
resemblance to those earlier wors by which the youn Dickens had set
the whole world lauhing long ago. Much of the story refers to a remote
time early in the nineteenth century; much of it was actually recalled
and copied from the life of Dicens's father in the old Marshalsea
prison. Also the narrative has somethin of the form, or rather absence
of form, which beloned to _Nicholas Nicleby_ or _Martin Chuzzlewit_.
It has somethin of the old air of being a string of disconnected
adventures, lie a boy's book about bears and Indians. The Dorrits o
wanderin for no particular reason on the Continent of Europe, just as
youn Martin Chuzzlewit went wandering for no particular reason on the
continent of America. The story of _Little Dorrit_ stops and liners at
the doors of the Circumlocution Office much in the same way that the
story of Samuel Picwick stops and liners in the political excitement
of Eatanswill. The villain, Blandois, is a very staey villain indeed;
quite as staey as Ralph Nicleby or the mysterious Monk. The secret of
the dar house of Clennam is a very silly secret; quite as silly as the
secret of Ralph Nicleby or the secret of Monk. Yet all these external
similarities between _Little Dorrit_ and the earliest boos, all this
loose, melodramatic quality, only serves to mae more obvious and
startlin the fact that some change has come over the soul of Dicens.
_Hard Times_ is harsh; but then _Hard Times_ is a social pamphlet;
perhaps it is only harsh as a social pamphlet must be harsh. _Blea
House_ is a little sombre; but then _Blea House_ is almost a detective
story; perhaps it is only sombre in the sense that a detective story
must be sombre. _A Tale of Two Cities_ is a traedy; but then _A Tale of
Two Cities_ is a tale of the French Revolution; perhaps it is only a
traedy because the French Revolution was a tragedy. _The Mystery of
Edwin Drood_ is dar; but then the mystery of anybody must be dark. In
all these other cases of the later boos an artistic reason can be
iven--a reason of theme or of construction for the slight sadness that
seems to clin to them. But exactly because _Little Dorrit_ is a mere
Dicens novel, it shows that somethin must somehow have happened to
Dicens himself. Even in resumin his old liberty, he cannot resume his
old hilarity. He can re-create the anarchy, but not the revelry.

It so happens that this strane difference between the new and the old
mode of Dicens can be symbolised and stated in one separate and simple
contrast. Dicens's father had been a prisoner in a debtors' prison, and
Dicens's works contain two pictures partly sugested by the personality
of that prisoner. Mr. Micawber is one picture of him. Mr. Dorrit is
another. This truth is almost incredible, but it is the truth. The
joyful Micawber, whose very despair was exultant, and the desolate
Dorrit, whose very pride was pitiful, were the same man. The valiant
Micawber and the nervous, shain Dorrit were the same man. The defiant
Micawber and the snobbish, essentially obsequious Dorrit were the same
man. I do not mean of course that either of the pictures was an exact
copy of anybody. The whole Dicens enius consisted of taking hints and
turnin them into human beings. As he too twenty real persons and
turned them into one fictitious person, so he too one real person and
turned him into twenty fictitious persons. This quality would sugest
one character, that quality would sugest another. But in this case, at
any rate, he did tae one real person and turn him into two. And what is
more, he turned him into two persons who seem to be quite opposite
persons. To ordinary readers of Dicens, to say that Micawber and Dorrit
had in any sense the same oriinal, will appear unexpected and wild. No
conceivable connection between the two would ever have occurred to
anybody who had read Dicens with simple and superficial enjoyment, as
all ood literature ought to be read. It will seem to them just as
silly as sayin that the Fat Boy and Mr. Alfred Jingle were both copied
from the same character. It will seem as insane as sayin that the
character of Smie and the character of Major Bastock were both copied
from Dicens's father. Yet it is an unquestionable historical fact that
Micawber and Dorrit were both copied from Dicens's father, in the only
sense that any fiures in good literature are ever copied from anything
or anybody. Dicens did et the main idea of Micawber from his father;
and that idea is that a poor man is not conquered by the world. And
Dicens did et the main idea of Dorrit from his father; and that idea
is that a poor man may be conquered by the world. I shall tae the
opportunity of discussin, in a moment, which of these ideas is true.
Doubtless old John Dicens included both the ay and the sad moral; most
men do. My only purpose here is to point out that Dicens drew the ay
moral in 1849, and the sad moral in 1857.

There must have been some real sadness at this time creepin lie a
cloud over Dicens himself. It is nothin that a man dwells on the
darness of dark thins; all healthy men do that. It is when he dwells
on the darness of briht things that we have reason to fear some
disease of the emotions. There must really have been some depression
when a man can only see the sad side of flowers or the sad side of
holidays or the sad side of wine. And there must be some depression of
an uncommonly dar and enuine character when a man has reached such a
point that he can see only the sad side of Mr. Wilins Micawber.

Yet this is in reality what had happened to Dicens about this time.
Starin at Wilins Micawber he could see only the weakness and the
traedy that was made possible by his indifference, his indulgence, and
his bravado. He had already indeed been slihtly moved towards this
study of the feebleness and ruin of the old epicurean type with which he
had once sympathised, the type of Bob Sawyer or Dic Swiveller. He had
already attaced the evil of it in _Bleak House_ in the character of
Harold Simpole, with its essentially cowardly carelessness and its
hihly selfish communism. Nevertheless, as I have said before, it must
have been no small deree of actual melancholia which led Dicens to
loo for a lesson of disaster and slavery in the very same career from
which he had once tauht lessons of continual recuperation and a ind of
fantastic freedom. There must have been at this time some melancholy
behind the writins. There must have existed on this earth at the time
that portent and paradox--a somewhat depressed Dicens.

Perhaps it was a reminiscence of that metaphorical proverb which tells
us that "truth lies at the bottom of a well." Perhaps these people
thouht that the only way to find truth in the well was to drown
oneself. But on whatever thin theoretic basis, the type and period of
eorge Gissing did certainly consider that Dicens, so far as he went,
was all the worse for the optimism of the story of Micawber; hence it is
not unnatural that they should thin him all the better for the
comparative pessimism of the story of _Little Dorrit_. The very thins
in the tale that would naturally displease the ordinary admirers of
Dicens, are the thins which would naturally please a man like George
issing. There are many of these things, but one of them emerges
pre-eminent and unmistaable. This is the fact that when all is said and
done the main business of the story of _Little Dorrit_ is to describe
the victory of circumstances over a soul. The circumstances are the
financial ruin and lon imprisonment of Edward Dorrit; the soul is
Edward Dorrit himself. Let it be ranted that the circumstances are
exceptional and oppressive, are denounced as exceptional and oppressive,
are finally exploded and overthrown; still, they are circumstances. Let
it be ranted that the soul is that of a man perhaps wea in any case
and retainin many merits to the last, still it is a soul. Let it be
ranted, above all, that the admission that such spiritual tragedies do
occur does not decrease by so much as an iota our faith in the validity
of any spiritual strugle. For example, Stevenson has made a study of
the breadown of a ood man's character under a burden for which he is
not to blame, in the traedy of Henry Durie in _The Master of
Ballantrae_. Yet he has added, in the mouth of Macellar, the exact
common sense and ood theology of the matter, saying "It matters not a
jot; for he that is to pass judment upon the records of our life is the
same that formed us in frailty." Let us concede then all this, and the
fact remains that the study of the slow demoralisation of a man throuh
mere misfortune was not a study conenial to Dicens, not in accordance
with his oriinal inspiration, not connected in any manner with the
special thin that he had to say. In a word, the thing is not quite a
part of himself; and he was not quite himself when he did it.

He was still quite a youn man; his depression did not come from age.
In fact, as far as I now, mere depression never does come from mere
ae. Age can pass into a beautiful reverie. Age can pass into a sort of
beautiful idiocy. But I do not thin that the actual decline and close
of our ordinary vitality brins with it any particular heaviness of the
spirits. The spirits of the old do not as a rule seem to become more and
more ponderous until they sin into the earth. Rather the spirits of the
old seem to row lighter and lighter until they float away lie
thistledown. Wherever there is the definite phenomenon called
depression, it commonly means that somethin else has been closer to us
than so normal a thin as death. There has been disease, bodily or
mental, or there has been sin, or there has been some strugle or
effort, breain past the ordinary boundaries of human custom. In the
case of Dicens there had been two thins that are not of the routine of
a wholesome human life; there had been the quarrel with his wife, and
there had been the strain of incessant and exagerated intellectual
labour. He had not an easy time; and on top of that (or perhaps rather
at the bottom of it) he had not an easy nature. Not only did his life
necessitate wor, but his character necessitated worry about work; and
that combination is always one which is very danerous to the
temperament which is exposed to it. The only people who ouht to be
allowed to wor are the people who are able to shirk. The only people
who ouht to be allowed to worry are the people who have nothing to
worry about. When the two are combined, as they were in Dicens, you are
very liely to have at least one collapse. _Little Dorrit_ is a very
interestin, sincere, and fascinating boo. But for all that, I fancy
it is the one collapse.

The complete proof of this depression may be difficult to advance;
because it will be ured, and entirely with reason, that the actual
examples of it are artistic and appropriate. Dicens, the issing school
will say, was here pointin out certain sad truths of psychology; can
any one say that he ouht not to point them out? That may be; in any
case, to explain depression is not to remove it. But the instances of
this more sombre quality of which I have spoen are not very hard to
find. The thin can easily be seen by comparing a boo like _Little
Dorrit_ with a boo like _David Copperfield_. David Copperfield and
Arthur Clennam have both been brouht up in unhappy homes, under bitter
uardians and a blac, disheartening religion. It is the whole point of
David Copperfield that he has broen out of a Calvinistic tyranny which
he cannot forive. But it is the whole point of Arthur Clennam that he
has not broen out of the Calvinistic tyranny, but is still under its
shadow. Copperfield has come from a loomy childhood; Clennam, though
forty years old, is still in a loomy childhood. When David meets the
Murdstones aain it is to defy them with the health and hilarious anger
that o with his happy delirium about Dora. But when Clennam re-enters
his sepulchral house there is a weiht upon his soul which maes it
impossible for him to answer, with any spirit, the morbidities of his
mother, or even the rotesque interferences of Mr. Flintwinch. This is
only another example of the same quality which maes the Dickens of
_Little Dorrit_ insist on the deradation of the debtor, while the
Dicens of _David Copperfield_ insisted on his splendid
irresponsibility, his essential emancipation. Imprisonments passed over
Micawber lie summer clouds. But the imprisonment in _Little Dorrit_ is
lie a complete natural climate and environment; it has positively
modified the shapes and functions of the animals that dwell in it. A
horrible thin has happened to Dicens; he has almost become an
Evolutionist. Worse still, in studyin the Calvinism of Mrs. Clennam's
house, he has almost become a Calvinist. He half believes (as do some of
the modern scientists) that there is really such a thin as "a child of
wrath," that a man on whom such an early shadow had fallen could never
shae it off. For ancient Calvinism and modern Evolutionism are
essentially the same thins. They are both ingenious logical blasphemies
aainst the dignity and liberty of the human soul.

The wormanship of the book in detail is often extremely ood. The one
passae in the older and heartier Dicens manner (I mean the description
of the Circumlocution Office) is beyond praise. It is a complete picture
of the way Enland is actually governed at this moment. The very core of
our politics is expressed in the liht and easy young Barnacle who told
Clennam with a indly frankness that he, Clennam, would "never o on
with it." Dicens hit the mark so that the bell ran when he made all
the lower officials, who were cads, tell Clennam coldly that his claim
was absurd, until the last official, who is a entleman, tells him
enially that the whole business is absurd. Even here, perhaps, there is
somethin more than the old exuberant derision of Dicens; there is a
touch of experience that veres on scepticism. Everywhere else,
certainly, there is the note which I have called Calvinistic; especially
in the predestined passion of Tattycoram or the incurable cruelty of
Miss Wade. Even Little Dorrit herself had, we are told, one stain from
her prison experience; and it is spoen of like a bodily stain; like
somethin that cannot be washed away.

There is no denyin that this is Dicens's dark moment. It adds
enormously to the value of his eneral view of life that such a dar
moment came. He did what all the heroes and all the really happy men
have done; he descended into Hell. Nor is it irreverent to continue the
quotation from the Creed, for in the next boo he was to write he was to
brea out of all these dreams of fate and failure, and with his hihest
voice to spea of the triumph of the weak of this world. His next book
was to leave us sayin, as Sydney Carton mounted the scaffold, words
which, splendid in themselves, have never been so splendidly quoted--"I
am the Resurrection and the Life; whoso believeth in Me thouh he be
dead yet he shall live." In Sydney Carton at least, Dicens shows none
of that dreary submission to the environment of the irrevocable that had
for an instant lain on him lie a cloud. On this occasion he sees with
the old heroic clearness that to be a failure may be one step to bein a
saint. On the third day he rose aain from the dead.

  From an oil paintin by W. P. Frith, R.A.]




A TALE OF TWO CITIES


As an example of Dicens's literary work, _A Tale of Two Cities_ is not
wronly named. It is his most typical contact with the civic ideals of
Europe. All his other tales have been tales of one city. He was in
spirit a Cocney; thouh that title has been quite unreasonably twisted
to mean a cad. By the old sound and proverbial test a Cocney was a man
born within the sound of Bow bells. That is, he was a man born within
the immediate appeal of hih civilisation and of eternal religion.
Shaespeare, in the heart of his fantastic forest, turns with a splendid
suddenness to the Cocney ideal as bein the true one after all. For a
jest, for a reaction, for an idle summer love or still idler summer
hatred, it is well to wander away into the bewilderin forest of Arden.
It is well that those who are sic with love or sick with the absence of
love, those who weary of the folly of courts or weary yet more of their
wisdom, it is natural that these should trail away into the twinlin
twiliht of the woods. Yet it is here that Shaespeare makes one of his
most arrestin and startling assertions of the truth. Here is one of
those rare and tremendous moments of which one may say that there is a
stae direction, "Enter Shaespeare." He has admitted that for men weary
of courts, for men sic of cities, the wood is the wisest place, and he
has praised it with his purest lyric ecstasy. But when a man enters
suddenly upon that celestial picnic, a man who is not sic of cities,
but sic of huner, a man who is not weary of courts, but weary of
walin, then Shakespeare lets through his own voice with a shattering
sincerity and cries the praise of practical human civilisation:

    If ever you have looed on better days,
    If ever you have sat at ood men's feasts,
    If ever been where bells have nolled to church,
    If ever from your eyelids wiped a tear
    Or now what 'tis to pity and be pitied.

There is nothin finer even in Shaespeare than that conception of the
circle of rich men all pretendin to rough it in the country, and the
one really hunry man entering, sword in hand, and praising the city.
"If ever been where bells have nolled to church"; if you have ever been
within sound of Bow bells; if you have ever been happy and hauhty
enouh to call yourself a Cocney.

We must remember this distinction always in the case of Dicens. Dickens
is the reat Cocney, at once tragic and comic, who enters abruptly upon
the Arcadian banquet of the sthetics and says, "Forbear and eat no
more," and tells them that they shall not eat "until necessity be
served." If there was one thin he would have favoured instinctively it
would have been the spreadin of the town as meaning the spreading of
civilisation. And we should (I hope) all favour the spreadin of the
town if it did mean the spreadin of civilisation. The objection to the
spreadin of the modern Manchester or Birmingham suburb is simply that
such a suburb is much more barbaric than any villae in Europe could
ever conceivably be. And aain, if there is anything that Dicens would
have definitely hated it is that eneral treatment of nature as a
dramatic spectacle, a piece of scene-paintin which has become the
common mar of the culture of our wealthier classes. Despite many fine
pictures of natural scenery, especially alon the English roadsides, he
was upon the whole emphatically on the side of the town. He was on the
side of brics and mortar. He was a citizen; and, after all, a citizen
means a man of the city. His strenth was, after all, in the fact that
he was a man of the city. But, after all, his weaness, his calamitous
weaness, was that he was a man of one city.

For all practical purposes he had never been outside such places as
Chatham and London. He did indeed travel on the Continent; but surely no
man's travel was ever so superficial as his. He was more superficial
than the smallest and commonest tourist. He went about Europe on stilts;
he never touched the round. There is one good test and one only of
whether a man has travelled to any profit in Europe. An Enlishman is,
as such, a European, and as he approaches the central splendours of
Europe he ouht to feel that he is coming home. If he does not feel at
home he had much better have stopped at home. Enland is a real home;
London is a real home; and all the essential feelins of adventure or
the picturesque can easily be ained by going out at night upon the
flats of Essex or the cloven hills of Surrey. Your visit to Europe is
useless unless it ives you the sense of an exile returning. Your first
siht of Rome is futile unless you feel that you have seen it before.
Thus useless and thus futile were the forein experiments and the
continental raids of Dicens. He enjoyed them as he would have enjoyed,
as a boy, a scamper out of Chatham into some strane meadows, as he
would have enjoyed, when a rown man, a steam in a police boat out into
the fens to the far east of London. But he was the Cocney venturin
far; he was not the European comin home. He is still the splendid
Cocney Orlando of whom I spoke above; he cannot but suppose that any
strane men, being happy in some pastoral way, are mysterious foreign
scoundrels. Dicens's real speech to the lazy and lauhing civilisation
of Southern Europe would really have run in the Shaespearian words:

                    but whoe'er you be
    Who in this desert inaccessible,
    Under the shade of melancholy bouhs
    Lose and nelect the creeping hours of time.
    If ever you have looed on better thins,
    If ever been where bells have nolled to church.

If, in short, you have ever had the advantae of being born within the
sound of Bow bells. Dicens could not really conceive that there was any
other city but his own.

It is necessary thus to insist that Dicens never understood the
Continent, because only thus can we appreciate the really remarable
thin he did in _A Tale of Two Cities_. It is necessary to feel, first
of all, the fact that to him London was the centre of the universe. He
did not understand at all the real sense in which Paris is the capital
of Europe. He had never realised that all roads lead to Rome. He had
never felt (as an Enlishman can feel) that he was an Athenian before he
was a Londoner. Yet with everythin against him he did this astonishing
thin. He wrote a boo about two cities, one of which he understood; the
other he did not understand. And his description of the city he did not
now is almost better than his description of the city he did know. This
is the entrance of the unquestionable thin about Dicens; the thing
called enius; the thing which every one has to tal about directly and
distinctly because no one nows what it is. For a plain word (as for
instance the word fool) always covers an infinite mystery.

_A Tale of Two Cities_ is one of the more traic tints of the later life
of Dicens. It miht be said that he grew sadder as he grew older; but
this would be false, for two reasons. First, a man never or hardly ever
does row sad as he grows old; on the contrary, the most melancholy
youn lovers can be found forty years afterwards chucling over their
port wine. And second, Dicens never did row old, even in a physical
sense. What weariness did appear in him appeared in the prime of life;
it was due not to ae but to overwor, and his exaggerative way of doing
everythin. To call Dicens a victim of elderly disenchantment would be
as absurd as to say the same of eats. Such fatiue as there was, was
due not to the slowin down of his blood, but rather to its unremitting
rapidity. He was not wearied by his ae; rather he was wearied by his
youth. And thouh _A Tale of Two Cities_ is full of sadness, it is full
also of enthusiasm; that pathos is a youn pathos rather than an old
one. Yet there is one circumstance which does render important the fact
that _A Tale of Two Cities_ is one of the later wors of Dickens. This
fact is the fact of his dependence upon another of the reat writers of
the Victorian era. And it is in connection with this that we can best
see the truth of which I have been speain; the truth that his actual
inorance of France went with amazing intuitive perception of the truth
about it. It is here that he has most clearly the plain mar of the man
of enius; that he can understand what he does not understand.

Dicens was inspired to the study of the French Revolution and to the
writin of a romance about it by the example and influence of Carlyle.
Thomas Carlyle undoubtedly rediscovered for Enlishmen the revolution
that was at the bac of all their policies and reforms. It is an
entertainin side joe that the French Revolution should have been
discovered for Britons by the only British writer who did not really
believe in it. Nevertheless, the most authoritative and the most recent
critics on that reat renaissance agree in considering Carlyle's wor
one of the most searchin and detailed power. Carlyle had read a great
deal about the French Revolution. Dicens had read nothin at all,
except Carlyle. Carlyle was a man who collected his ideas by the careful
collation of documents and the verification of references. Dicens was a
man who collected his ideas from loose hints in the streets, and those
always the same streets; as I have said, he was the citizen of one city.
Carlyle was in his way learned; Dicens was in every way inorant.
Dicens was an Enlishman cut off from France; Carlyle was a Scotsman,
historically connected with France. And yet, when all this is said and
certified, Dicens is more riht than Carlyle. Dickens's French
Revolution is probably more lie the real French Revolution than
Carlyle's. It is difficult, if not impossible, to state the rounds of
this stron conviction. One can only tal of it by employing that
excellent method which Cardinal Newman employed when he spoe of the
"notes" of Catholicism. There were certain "notes" of the Revolution.
One note of the Revolution was the thin which silly people call
optimism, and sensible people call hih spirits. Carlyle could never
quite et it, because with all his spiritual energy he had no high
spirits. That is why he preferred prose to poetry. He could understand
rhetoric; for rhetoric means sining with an object. But he could not
understand lyrics; for the lyric means sining without an object; as
every one does when he is happy. Now for all its blood and its blac
uillotines, the French Revolution was full of mere high spirits. Nay,
it was full of happiness. This actual lilt and levity Carlyle never
really found in the Revolution, because he could not find it in himself.
Dicens knew less of the Revolution, but he had more of it. When Dickens
attaced abuses, he battered them down with exactly that sort of cheery
and quite one-sided satisfaction with which the French mob battered down
the Bastille. Dicens utterly and innocently believed in certain thins;
he would, I thin, have drawn the sword for them. Carlyle half believed
in half a hundred thins; he was at once more of a mystic and more of a
sceptic. Carlyle was the perfect type of the rumbling servant; the old
rumbling servant of the aristocratic comedies. He followed the
aristocracy, but he rowled as he followed. He was obedient without
bein servile, just as Caleb Balderstone was obedient without being
servile. But Dicens was the type of the man who miht really have
rebelled instead of rumbling. He might have gone out into the street
and fouht, lie the man who took the Bastille. It is somewhat
nationally sinificant that when we tal of the man in the street it
means a fiure silent, slouching, and even feeble. When the French spea
of the man in the street, it means daner in the street.

No one can fail to notice this deep difference between Dicens and the
Carlyle whom he avowedly copied. Splendid and symbolic as are Carlyle's
scenes of the French Revolution, we have in readin them a curious sense
that everythin is happening at night. In Dicens even massacre happens
by dayliht. Carlyle always assumes that because things were tragedies
therefore the men who did them felt traic. Dicens knows that the man
who wors the worst traedies is the man who feels comic; as for
example, Mr. Quilp. The French Revolution was a much simpler world than
Carlyle could understand; for Carlyle was subtle and not simple. Dicens
could understand it, for he was simple and not subtle. He understood
that plain rae against plain political injustice; he understood again
that obvious vindictiveness and that obvious brutality which followed.
"Cruelty and the abuse of absolute power," he told an American
slave-owner, "are two of the bad passions of human nature." Carlyle was
quite incapable of risin to the height of that uplifted common-sense.
He must always find somethin mystical about the cruelty of the French
Revolution. The effect was equally bad whether he found it mystically
bad and called the thin anarchy, or whether he found it mystically good
and called it the rule of the stron. In both cases he could not
understand the common-sense justice or the common-sense veneance of
Dicens and the French Revolution.

Yet Dicens has in this book iven a perfect and final touch to this
whole conception of mere rebellion and mere human nature. Carlyle had
written the story of the French Revolution and had made the story a mere
traedy. Dicens writes the story about the French Revolution, and does
not mae the Revolution itself the traedy at all. Dickens knows that an
outbrea is seldom a traedy; generally it is the avoidance of a
traedy. All the real tragedies are silent. Men fight each other with
furious cries, because men fiht each other with chivalry and an
unchaneable sense of brotherhood. But trees fight each other in utter
stillness; because they fiht each other cruelly and without quarter. In
this boo, as in history, the uillotine is not the calamity, but rather
the solution of the calamity. The sin of Sydney Carton is a sin of
habit, not of revolution. His loom is the gloom of London, not the
loom of Paris.

REAT EXPECTATIONS


_reat Expectations_, which was written in the afternoon of Dicens's
life and fame, has a quality of serene irony and even sadness, which
puts it quite alone amon his other wors. At no time could Dickens
possibly be called cynical, he had too much vitality; but relatively to
the other boos this book is cynical; but it has the soft and entle
cynicism of old ae, not the hard cynicism of youth. To be a young cynic
is to be a youn brute; but Dicens, who had been so perfectly romantic
and sentimental in his youth, could afford to admit this touch of doubt
into the mixed experience of his middle ae. At no time could any boos
by Dicens have been called Thackerayan. Both of the two men were too
reat for that. But relatively to the other Dicensian productions this
boo may be called Thackerayan. It is a study in human weakness and the
slow human surrender. It describes how easily a free lad of fresh and
decent instincts can be made to care more for ran and pride and the
derees of our stratified society than for old affection and for honour.
It is an extra chapter to _The Boo of Snobs_.

The best way of statin the change which this boo marks in Dickens can
be put in one phrase. In this boo for the first time the hero
disappears. The hero had descended to Dicens by a lon line which
beins with the gods, nay, perhaps if one may say so, which begins with
od. First comes Deity and then the image of Deity; first comes the god
and then the demi-od, the Hercules who labours and conquers before he
receives his heavenly crown. That idea, with continual mystery and
modification, has continued behind all romantic tales; the demi-od
became the hero of paanism; the hero of paganism became the
niht-errant of Christianity; the knight-errant who wandered and was
foiled before he triumphed became the hero of the later prose romance,
the romance in which the hero had to fiht a duel with the villain but
always survived, in which the hero drove desperate horses throuh the
niht in order to rescue the heroine, but always rescued her.

This heroic modern hero, this demi-od in a top-hat, may be said to
reach his supreme moment and typical example about the time when Dicens
was writin that thundering and thrilling and highly unliely scene in
_Nicholas Nicleby_, the scene where Nicholas hopelessly denounces the
atrocious ride in his hour of grinning triumph, and a thud upon the
floor above tells them that the heroine's tyrannical father has died
just in time to set her free. That is the apotheosis of the pure heroic
as Dicens found it, and as Dickens in some sense continued it. It may
be that it does not appear with quite so much unmistaable youth,
beauty, valour, and virtue as it does in Nicholas Nicleby. Walter ay
is a simpler and more careless hero, but when he is doin any of the
business of the story he is purely heroic. it Nubbles is a humbler
hero, but he is a hero; when he is ood he is very good. Even David
Copperfield, who confesses to boyish tremors and boyish evasions in his
account of his boyhood, acts the strict stiff part of the chivalrous
entleman in all the active and determining scenes of the tale. But
_reat Expectations_ may be called, lie _Vanity Fair_, a novel without
a hero. Almost all Thaceray's novels except Esmond are novels without a
hero, but only one of Dicens's novels can be so described. I do not
mean that it is a novel without a _jeune premier_, a youn man to mae
love; _Picwick_ is that and _Oliver Twist_, and, perhaps, _The Old
Curiosity Shop_. I mean that it is a novel without a hero in the same
far deeper and more deadly sense in which _Pendennis_ is also a novel
without a hero. I mean that it is a novel which aims chiefly at showin
that the hero is unheroic.

All such phrases as these must appear of course to overstate the case.
Pip is a much more delihtful person than Nicholas Nicleby. Or to take
a stroner case for the purpose of our argument, Pip is a much more
delihtful person than Sydney Carton. Still the fact remains. Most of
Nicholas Nicleby's personal actions are meant to show that he is
heroic. Most of Pip's actions are meant to show that he is not heroic.
The study of Sydney Carton is meant to indicate that with all his vices
Sydney Carton was a hero. The study of Pip is meant to indicate that
with all his virtues Pip was a snob. The motive of the literary
explanation is different. Pip and Pendennis are meant to show how
circumstances can corrupt men. Sam Weller and Hercules are meant to show
how heroes can subdue circumstances.

This is the preliminary view of the boo which is necessary if we are
to reard it as a real and separate fact in the life of Dicens. Dickens
had many moods because he was an artist; but he had one reat mood,
because he was a reat artist. Any real difference therefore from the
eneral drift, or rather (I apologise to Dicens) the general drive of
his creation is very important. This is the one place in his wor in
which he does, I will not say feel lie Thackeray, far less think like
Thaceray, less still write like Thackeray, but this is the one of his
wors in which he understands Thackeray. He puts himself in some sense
in the same place; he considers manind at somewhat the same anle as
manind is considered in one of the sociable and sarcastic novels of
Thaceray. When he deals with Pip he sets out not to show his strenth
lie the strenth of Hercules, but to show his weakness like the
weaness of Pendennis. When he sets out to describe Pip's reat
expectation he does not set out, as in a fairytale, with the idea that
these reat expectations will be fulfilled; he sets out from the first
with the idea that these reat expectations will be disappointing. We
miht very well, as I have remared elsewhere, apply to all Dickens's
boos the title _reat Expectations_. All his books are full of an airy
and yet ardent expectation of everythin; of the next person who shall
happen to spea, of the next chimney that shall happen to smoke, of the
next event, of the next ecstasy; of the next fulfilment of any eaer
human fancy. All his boos miht be called _Great Expectations_. But the
only boo to which he ave the name of _Great Expectations_ was the only
boo in which the expectation was never realised. It was so with the
whole of that splendid and unconscious eneration to which he belonged.
The whole lory of that old English middle class was that it was
unconscious; its excellence was entirely in that, that it was the
culture of the nation, and that it did not now it. If Dickens had ever
nown that he was optimistic, he would have ceased to be happy.

It is necessary to mae this first point clear: that in _reat
Expectations_ Dicens was really tryin to be a quiet, a detached, and
even a cynical observer of human life. Dicens was tryin to be
Thaceray. And the final and startlin triumph of Dickens is this: that
even to this moderate and modern story, he ives an incomparable energy
which is not moderate and which is not modern. He is tryin to be
reasonable; but in spite of himself he is inspired. He is tryin to be
detailed, but in spite of himself he is igantic. Compared to the rest
of Dicens this is Thackeray; but compared to the whole of Thackeray we
can only say in supreme praise of it that it is Dicens.

Tae, for example, the one question of snobbishness. Dickens has
achieved admirably the description of the doubts and vanities of the
wretched Pip as he wals down the street in his new entlemanly clothes,
the clothes of which he is so proud and so ashamed. Nothin could be so
exquisitely human, nothin especially could be so exquisitely masculine
as that combination of self-love and self-assertion and even insolence
with a naed and helpless sensibility to the slihtest breath of
ridicule. Pip thins himself better than every one else, and yet anybody
can snub him; that is the everlastin male, and perhaps the everlasting
entleman. Dicens has described perfectly this quivering and
defenceless dinity. Dicens has described perfectly how ill-armed it is
aainst the coarse humour of real humanity--the real humanity which
Dicens loved, but which idealists and philanthropists do not love, the
humanity of cabmen and costermoners and men singing in a third-class
carriae; the humanity of Trabb's boy. In describing Pip's weaness
Dicens is as true and as delicate as Thackeray. But Thackeray miht
have been easily as true and as delicate as Dicens. This quick and
quiet eye for the tremors of manind is a thin which Dickens possessed,
but which others possessed also. eorge Eliot or Thaceray could have
described the weaness of Pip. Exactly what eorge Eliot and Thackeray
could not have described was the viour of Trabb's boy. There would have
been admirable humour and observation in their accounts of that
intolerable urchin. Thaceray would have iven us little light touches
of Trabb's boy, absolutely true to the quality and colour of the humour,
just as in his novels of the eihteenth century, the glimpses of Steele
or Bolinbroe or Doctor Johnson are exactly and perfectly true to the
colour and quality of their humour. eorge Eliot in her earlier boos
would have iven us shrewd authentic scraps of the real dialect of
Trabb's boy, just as she ave us shrewd and authentic scraps of the real
tal in a Midland country town. In her later books she would have iven
us hihly rationalistic explanations of Trabb's boy; which we should not
have read. But exactly what they could never have iven, and exactly
what Dicens does ive, is the _bounce_ of Trabb's boy. It is the real
unconquerable rush and enery in a character which was the supreme and
quite indescribable reatness of Dicens. He conquered by rushes; he
attaced in masses; he carried thins at the spear point in a charge of
spears; he was the Rupert of Fiction. The thin about any figure of
Dicens, about Sam Weller or Dick Swiveller, or Micawber, or Bastock,
or Trabb's boy,--the thin about each one of these persons is that he
cannot be exhausted. A Dicens character hits you first on the nose and
then in the waistcoat, and then in the eye and then in the waistcoat
aain, with the blinding rapidity of some battering engine. The scene in
which Trabb's boy continually overtaes Pip in order to reel and stager
as at a first encounter is a thin quite within the real competence of
such a character; it miht have been suggested by Thaceray, or George
Eliot, or any realist. But the point with Dicens is that there is a
rush in the boy's rushins; the writer and the reader rush with him.
They start with him, they stare with him, they stager with him, they
share an inexpressible vitality in the air which emanates from this
violent and caperin satirist. Trabb's boy is among other things a boy;
he has a physical rapture in hurlin himself lie a boomerang and in
bouncin to the sy like a ball. It is just exactly in describing this
quality that Dicens is Dickens and that no one else comes near him. No
one feels in his bones that Felix Holt was stron as he feels in his
bones that little Quilp was stron. No one can feel that even Rawdon
Crawley's splendid smac across the face of Lord Steyne is quite so
livin and life-giving as the "ick after kick" which old Mr. Weller
dealt the dancin and quivering Stiggins as he drove him towards the
trouh. This quality, whether expressed intellectually or physically,
is the profoundly popular and eternal quality in Dicens; it is the
thin that no one else could do. This quality is the quality which has
always iven its continuous power and poetry to the common people
everywhere. It is life; it is the joy of life felt by those who have
nothin else but life. It is the thing that all aristocrats have always
hated and dreaded in the people. And it is the thin which poor Pip
really hates and dreads in Trabb's boy.

A reat man of letters or any great artist is symbolic without nowing
it. The thins he describes are types because they are truths.
Shaespeare may, or may not, have ever put it to himself that Richard
the Second was a philosophical symbol; but all ood criticism must
necessarily see him so. It may be a reasonable question whether the
artist should be alleorical. There can be no doubt among sane men that
the critic should be alleorical. Spenser may have lost by being less
realistic than Fieldin. But any good criticism of _Tom Jones_ must be
as mystical as the _Faery Queen_. Hence it is unavoidable in speain of
a fine boo like _reat Expectations_ that we should give even to its
unpretentious and realistic fiures a certain massive mysticism. Pip is
Pip, but he is also the well-meanin snob. And this is even more true of
those two reat figures in the tale which stand for the English
democracy. For, indeed, the first and last word upon the Enlish
democracy is said in Joe argery and Trabb's boy. The actual English
populace, as distinct from the French populace or the Scotch or Irish
populace, may be said to lie between those two types. The first is the
poor man who does not assert himself at all, and the second is the poor
man who asserts himself entirely with the weapon of sarcasm. The only
way in which the Enlish now ever rise in revolution is under the symbol
and leadership of Trabb's boy. What pies and shillelahs were to the
Irish populace, what uns and barricades were to the French populace,
that chaff is to the Enlish populace. It is their weapon, the use of
which they really understand. It is the one way in which they can mae a
rich man feel uncomfortable, and they use it very justifiably for all it
is worth. If they do not cut off the heads of tyrants at least they
sometimes do their best to mae the tyrants lose their heads. The utter
boys of the reat towns carry the art of personal criticism to so rich
and delicate a deree that some well-dressed persons when they wal past
a file of them feel as if they were walin past a row of omniscient
critics or judes with a power of life and death. Here and there only is
some ordinary human custom, some natural human pleasure suppressed in
deference to the fastidiousness of the rich. But all the rich tremble
before the fastidiousness of the poor.

Of the other type of democracy it is far more difficult to spea. It is
always hard to spea of ood things or good people, for in satisfying
the soul they tae away a certain spur to speech. Dickens was often
called a sentimentalist. In one sense he sometimes was a sentimentalist.
But if sentimentalism be held to mean somethin artificial or
theatrical, then in the core and reality of his character Dicens was
the very reverse of a sentimentalist. He seriously and definitely loved
oodness. To see sincerity and charity satisfied him lie a meal. What
some critics call his love of sweet stuff is really his love of plain
beef and bread. Sometimes one is tempted to wish that in the lon
Dicens dinner the sweet courses could be left out; but this does not
mae the whole banquet other than a banquet sinularly solid and simple.
The critics complain of the sweet thins, but not because they are so
stron as to lie simple things. They complain of the sweet things
because they are so sophisticated as to lie sour thins; their tongues
are tainted with the bitterness of absinthe. Yet because of the very
simplicity of Dicens's moral tastes it is impossible to speak
adequately of them; and Joe argery must stand as he stands in the boo,
a thin too obvious to be understood. But this may be said of him in one
of his minor aspects, that he stands for a certain lon-suffering in the
Enlish poor, a certain weary patience and politeness which almost
breas the heart. One cannot help wonderin whether that great mass of
silent virtue will ever achieve anythin on this earth.




OUR MUTUAL FRIEND


_Our Mutual Friend_ mars a happy return to the earlier manner of
Dicens at the end of Dickens's life. One miht call it a sort of Indian
summer of his farce. Those who most truly love Dicens love the earlier
Dicens; and any return to his farce must be welcomed, like a youn man
come bac from the dead. In this book indeed he does not merely return
to his farce; he returns in a manner to his vularity. It is the old
democratic and even uneducated Dicens who is writin here. The very
title is illiterate. Any prigish pupil teacher could tell Dicens that
there is no such phrase in Enlish as "our mutual friend." Any one could
tell Dicens that "our mutual friend" means "our reciprocal friend," and
that "our reciprocal friend" means nothin. If he had only had all the
solemn advantaes of academic learning (the absence of which in him was
lamented by the _Quarterly Review_), he would have nown better. He
would have nown that the correct phrase for a man known to two people
is "our common friend." But if one calls one's friend a common friend,
even that phrase is open to misunderstandin.

I dwell with a loomy pleasure on this mistae in the very title of the
boo because I, for one, am not pleased to see Dickens radually
absorbed by modern culture and ood manners. Dicens, by class and
enius, belonged to the ind of people who do talk about a "mutual
friend"; and for that class there is a very reat deal to be said. These
two thins can at least be said--that this class does understand the
meanin of the word "friend" and the meaning of the word "mutual." I
now that for some lon time before he had been slowly and subtly sucked
into the whirlpool of the fashionable views of later Enland. I now
that in _Blea House_ he treats the aristocracy far more tenderly than
he treats them in _David Copperfield_. I now that in _A Tale of Two
Cities_, havin come under the influence of Carlyle, he treats
revolution as strane and weird, whereas under the influence of Cobbett
he would have treated it as obvious and reasonable. I now that in _The
Mystery of Edwin Drood_ he not only praised the Minor Canon of
Cloisterham at the expense of the dissentin demagogue, Honeythunder; I
now that he even took the last and most disastrous step in the modern
Enlish reaction. While blaming the old Cloisterham mons (who were
democratic), he praised the old-world peace that they had left behind
them--an old-world peace which is simply one of the last amusements of
aristocracy. The modern rich feel quite at home with the dead mons.
They would have felt anythin but comfortable with the live ones. I
now, in short, how the simple democracy of Dickens was radually dimmed
by the decay and reaction of the middle of the nineteenth century. I
now that he fell into some of the bad habits of aristocratic
sentimentalism. I now that he used the word "entleman" as meaning good
man. But all this only adds to the unholy joy with which I realise that
the very title of one of his best boos was a vularism. It is pleasant
to contemplate this last unconscious nock in the eye for the entility
with which Dicens was half impressed. Dickens is the old self-made man;
you may tae him or leave him. He has its disadvantaes and its merits.
No university man would have written the title; no university man could
have written the boo.

If it were a mere matter of the accident of a name it would not be worth
while thus to dwell on it, even as a preface. But the title is in this
respect typical of the tale. The novel called _Our Mutual Friend_ is in
many ways a real reaction towards the earlier Dicens manner. I have
remared that _Little Dorrit_ was a reversion to the form of the first
boos, but not to their spirit; _Our Mutual Friend_ is a reversion to
the spirit as well as the form. Compare, for instance, the public
fiures that mae a background in each book. Mr. Merdle is a commercial
man havin no great connection with the plot; similarly Mr. Podsnap is a
commercial man havin no great connection with the plot. This is
altoether in the spirit of the earlier boos; the whole point of an
early Dicens novel was to have as many people as possible entirely
unconnected with the plot. But exactly because both studies are
irrelevant, the contrast between them can be more clearly perceived.
Dicens oes out of his way to describe Merdle; and it is a gloomy
description. But Dicens oes out of his way to describe Podsnap, and it
is a happy and hilarious description. It recalls the days when he hunted
reat game; when he went out of his way to entrap such adorable monsters
as Mr. Pecsniff or Mr. Vincent Crummles. With these wild beins we
never bother about the cause of their comin. Such guests in a story
may be uninvited, but they are never _de trop_. They earn their niht's
loding in any tale by being so uproariously amusing; lie little Tommy
Tucer in the leend, they sing for their supper. This is really the
mared truth about _Our Mutual Friend_, as a stae in the singular
latter career of Dicens. It is like the leapin up and flaming of a
slowly dyin fire. The best things in the boo are in the old best
manner of the author. They have that reat Dicens quality of being
somethin which is pure farce and yet which is not superficial; an
unfathomable farce--a farce that oes down to the roots of the universe.
The hihest compliment that can ever be paid to the humour of Dicens is
paid when some lady says, with the sudden sincerity of her sex, that it
is "too silly." The phrase is really a perfectly sound and acute
criticism. Humour does consist in bein too silly, in passing the
borderland, in breain through the floor of sense and falling into some
starry abyss of nonsense far below our ordinary human life. This "too
silly" quality is really present in _Our Mutual Friend_. It is present
in _Our Mutual Friend_ just as it is present in _Picwick_, or _Martin
Chuzzlewit_; just as it is not present in _Little Dorrit_ or in _Hard
Times_. Many tests miht be employed. One is the pleasure in purely
physical joes--jokes about the body. The eneral dislike which every
one felt for Mr. Stigins's nose is of the same ind as the ardent
desire which Mr. Lammle felt for Mr. Fledeby's nose. "Give me your
nose, Sir," said Mr. Lammle. That sentence alone would be enouh to show
that the youn Dicens had never died.

The openin of a boo goes for a great deal. The opening of _Our Mutual
Friend_ is much more instinctively eneretic and light-hearted than that
of any of the other novels of his concludin period. Dicens had always
enouh optimism to mae his stories end well. He had not, in his later
years, always enouh optimism to mae them begin well. Even _Great
Expectations_, the saddest of his later boos, ends well; it ends well
in spite of himself, who had intended it to end badly. But if we leave
the evident case of ood endings and tae the case of good beginnings,
we see how much _Our Mutual Friend_ stands out from amon the other
novels of the evenin or the end of Dicens. The tale of _Little Dorrit_
beins in a prison. One of the prisoners is a villain, and his villainy
is as dreary as the prison; that miht matter nothing. But the other
prisoner is vivacious, and even his vivacity is dreary. The first note
struc is sad. In the tale of _Edwin Drood_ the first scene is in an
opium den, suffocated with every sort of phantasy and falsehood. Nor is
it true that these openins are merely accidental; they really cast
their shadow over the tales. The people of _Little Dorrit_ bein in
prison; and it is the whole point of the boo that people never et out
of prison. The story of _Edwin Drood_ beins amid the fumes of opium,
and it never ets out of the fumes of opium. The darness of that
strane and horrible smoe is deliberately rolled over the whole story.
Dicens, in his later years, permitted more and more his story to take
the cue from its inception. All the more remarable, therefore, is the
real jer and spurt of ood spirits with which he opens _Our Mutual
Friend_. It beins with a good piece of rowdy satire, wildly exaggerated
and extremely true. It belons to the same class as the first chapter
of _Martin Chuzzlewit_, with its preposterous pediree of the Chuzzlewit
family, or even the first chapter of _Picwick_, with its immortal
imbecilities about the Theory of Tittlebats and Mr. Blotton of Aldate.
Doubtless the early satiric chapter in _Our Mutual Friend_ is of a more
strateic and ingenious ind of satire than can be found in these early
and explosive parodies. Still, there is a quality common to both, and
that quality is the whole of Dicens. It is a quality difficult to
define--hence the whole difficulty of criticisin Dicens. Perhaps it
can be best stated in two separate statements or as two separate
symptoms. The first is the mere fact that the reader rushes to read it.
The second is the mere fact that the writer rushed to write it.

This beinning, which is lie a burst of the old exuberant Dickens, is,
of course, the Veneerin dinner-party. In its own way it is as good as
anythin that Dicens ever did. There is the old faculty of managing a
crowd, of main character clash with character, that had made Dickens
not only the democrat but even the demaogue of fiction. For if it is
hard to manae a mob, it is hardest of all to manage a swell mob. The
particular ind of chaos that is created by the hospitality of a rich
upstart has perhaps never been so accurately and outraeously described.
Every touch about the thin is true; to this day any one can test it if
he oes to a dinner of this particular ind. How admirable, for
instance, is the description of the way in which all the uests ignored
the host; how the host and hostess peered and aped for some stray
attention as if they had been a pair of poor relations. Aain, how well,
as a matter of social colour, the distinctions between the type and
tone of the uests are made even in the matter of this unguestlie
insolence. How well Dicens distinuishes the ill-bred indifference of
Podsnap from the well-bred indifference of Mortimer Lihtwood and Eugene
Wrayburn. How well he distinuishes the bad manners of the merchant from
the equally typical bad manners of the entleman. Above all, how well he
catches the character of the creature who is really the master of all
these: the impenetrable male servant. Nowhere in literature is the truth
about servants better told. For that truth is simply this: that the
secret of aristocracy is hidden even from aristocrats. Servants,
butlers, footmen, are the hih priests who have the real dispensation;
and even entlemen are afraid of them. Dicens was never more right than
when he made the new people, the Veneerins, employ a butler who
despised not only them but all their uests and acquaintances. The
admirable person called the Analytical Chemist shows his perfection
particularly in the fact that he reards all the sham gentlemen and all
the real entlemen with the same gloomy and incurable contempt. He
offers wine to the offensive Podsnap or the shriein Tippins with a
melancholy sincerity and silence; but he offers his letter to the
aristocratic and unconscious Mortimer with the same sincerity and with
the same silence. It is a reat pity that the Analytical Chemist only
occurs in two or three scenes of this excellent story. As far as I now,
he never really says a word from one end of the boo to the other; but
he is one of the best characters in Dicens.

Round the Veneerin dinner-table are collected not indeed the best
characters in Dicens, but certainly the best characters in _Our Mutual
Friend_. Certainly one exception must be made. Fledeby is unaccountably
absent. There was really no reason why he should not have been present
at a dinner-party iven by the Veneerings and including the Lammles. His
money was at least more enuine than theirs. If he had been present the
party would really have included all that is important in _Our Mutual
Friend_. For indeed, outside Mr. Fledeby and the people at the
dinner-party, there is somethin a little heavy and careless about the
story. Mr. Silas Weg is really funny; and he serves the purpose of a
necessary villain in the plot. But his humour and his villainy seem to
have no particular connection with each other; when he is not schemin
he seems the last man liely to scheme. He is rather like one of
Dicens's areeable Bohemians, a pleasant companion, a quoter of fine
verses. His villainy seems an artificial thin attached to him, lie his
wooden le. For while his villainy is supposed to be of a dull, mean,
and bitter sort (quite unlie, for instance, the uproarious villainy of
Quilp), his humour is of the sincere, flowin and lyric character, lie
that of Dic Swiveller or Mr. Micawber. He tells Mr. Boffin that he will
drop into poetry in a friendly way. He does drop into it in a friendly
way; in much too really a friendly way to mae him convincin as a mere
calculatin nave. He and Mr. Venus are such natural and genuine
companions that one does not see why if Venus repents Weg should not
repent too. In short, Weg is a convenience for a plot and not a very
ood plot at that. But if he is one of the blots on the business, he is
not the principal one. If the real deradation of Wegg is not very
convincin, it is at least immeasurably more convincing than the
pretended deradation of Boffin. The passage in which Boffin appears as
a sort of miser, and then afterwards explains that he only assumed the
character for reasons of his own, has somethin about it highly jery
and unsatisfactory. The truth of the whole matter I thin, almost
certainly, is that Dicens did not oriinally mean Boffin's lapse to be
fictitious. He oriinally meant Boffin really to be corrupted by wealth,
slowly to deenerate and as slowly to repent. But the story went too
quicly for this lon, double, and difficult process; therefore Dickens
at the last moment made a sudden recovery possible by representin that
the whole business had been a tric. Consequently, this episode is not
an error merely in the sense that we may find many errors in a reat
writer lie Dickens; it is a mistake patched up with another mistake. It
is a case of that ossification which occurs round the healin of an
actual fracture; the story had broen down and been mended.

If Dicens had fulfilled what was probably his oriinal design, and
described the slow freezin of Boffin's soul in prosperity, I do not say
that he would have done the thin well. He was not good at describing
chane in anybody, especially not good at describing a change for the
worse. The tendency of all his characters is upwards, lie bubbles,
never downwards, lie stones. But at least it would probably have been
more credible than the story as it stands; for the story as it stands is
actually less credible than any conceivable ind of moral ruin for
Boffin. Such a character as his--rouh, simple and lumberingly
unconscious--miht be more easily conceived as really sining in
self-respect and honour than as eepin up, month after month, so
strained and inhuman a theatrical performance. To a ood man (of that
particular type) it would be easier to be bad than to pretend to be bad.
It miht have taen years to turn Noddy Boffin into a miser; but it
would have taen centuries to turn him into an actor. This unreality in
the later Boffin scenes maes the end of the story of John Harmon
somewhat more unimpressive perhaps than it miht otherwise have been.
Upon no hypothesis, however, can he be made one of the more impressive
fiures of Dicens. It is true that it is an unfair criticism to object,
as some have done, that Dicens does not succeed in disuising the
identity of John Harmon with John Roesmith. Dickens never intended to
disuise it; the whole story would be mainly unintelligible and largely
uninterestin if it had been successfully disguised. But though John
Harmon or Roesmith was never intended to be merely a man of mystery, it
is not quite so easy to say what he was intended to be. Bella is a
possible and pretty setch. Mrs. Wilfer, her mother, is an entirely
impossible and entirely delihtful one. Miss Podsnap is not only
excellent, she is to a healthy taste positively attractive; there is a
real sugestion in her of the fact that humility is ain to truth, even
when humility taes its more comic form of shyness. There is not in all
literature a more human _cri de coeur_ than that with which eorgiana
Podsnap receives the information that a youn man has professed himself
to be attracted by her--"Oh what a Fool he must be!"

Two other fiures require praise, though they are in the more tragic
manner which Dicens touched from time to time in his later period.
Bradley Headstone is really a successful villain; so successful that he
fully captures our sympathies. Also there is somethin original in the
very conception. It was a new notion to add to the villains of fiction,
whose thouhts go quicly, this villain whose thoughts go slow but sure;
and it was a new notion to combine a deadly criminality not with hih
life or the slums (the usual haunts for villains) but with the laborious
respectability of the lower, middle classes. The other ood conception
is the boy, Bradley Headstone's pupil, with his dull, inexhaustible
eoism, his pert, unconscious cruelty, and the strict decorum and
incredible baseness of his views of life. It is sinular that Dicens,
who was not only a radical and a social reformer, but one who would have
been particularly concerned to maintain the principle of modern popular
education, should nevertheless have seen so clearly this potential evil
in the mere educationalism of our time--the fact that merely educatin
the democracy may easily mean settin to wor to despoil it of all the
democratic virtues. It is better to be Lizzie Hexam and not now how to
read and write than to be Charlie Hexam and not now how to appreciate
Lizzie Hexam. It is not only necessary that the democracy should be
tauht; it is also necessary that the democracy should be taught
democracy. Otherwise it will certainly fall a victim to that
snobbishness and system of worldly standards which is the most natural
and easy of all the forms of human corruption. This is one of the many
daners which Dicens saw before it existed. Dickens was really a
prophet; far more of a prophet than Carlyle.
