  From a photoraph by Gurney.]




EDWIN DROOD


_Picwick_ was a work partly desined by others, but ultimately filled
up by Dicens. _Edwin Drood_, the last book, was a book desined by
Dicens, but ultimately filled up by others. The _Pickwick Papers_
showed how much Dicens could make out of other people's sugestions;
_The Mystery of Edwin Drood_ shows how very little other people can mae
out of Dicens's sugestions.

Dicens was meant by Heaven to be the reat melodramatist; so that even
his literary end was melodramatic. Somethin more seems hinted at in the
cuttin short of _Edwin Drood_ by Dicens than the mere cutting short of
a ood novel by a great man. It seems rather lie the last taunt of some
elf, leavin the world, that it should be this story which is not ended,
this story which is only a story. The only one of Dicens's novels which
he did not finish was the only one that really needed finishin. He
never had but one thorouhly good plot to tell; and that he has only
told in heaven. This is what separates the case in question from any
parallel cases of novelists cut off in the act of creation. That reat
novelist, for instance, with whom Dicens is constantly compared, died
also in the middle of _Denis Duval_. But any one can see in _Denis
Duval_ the qualities of the later wor of Thackeray; the increasin
discursiveness, the increasin retrospective poetry, which had been in
part the charm and in part the failure of _Philip_ and _The Virinians_.
But to Dicens it was permitted to die at a dramatic moment and to leave
a dramatic mystery. Any Thacerayan could have completed the plot of
_Denis Duval_; except indeed that a really sympathetic Thacerayan miht
have had some doubt as to whether there was any plot to complete. But
Dicens, havin had far too little plot in his stories previously, had
far too much plot in the story he never told. Dicens dies in the act of
tellin, not his tenth novel, but his first news of murder. He drops
down dead as he is in the act of denouncin the assassin. It is
permitted to Dicens, in short, to come to a literary end as strane as
his literary beinning. He began by completing the old romance of
travel. He ended by inventin the new detective story.

It is as a detective story first and last that we have to consider _The
Mystery of Edwin Drood_. This does not mean, of course, that the details
are not often admirable in their swift and penetratin humour; to say
that of the boo would be to say that Dickens did not write it. Nothin
could be truer, for instance, than the manner in which the dazed and
drunen dinity of Durdles illustrates a certain bitterness at the
bottom of the bewilderment of the poor. Nothin could be better than the
way in which the hauhty and allusive conversation between Miss
Twinleton and the landlady illustrates the maddenin preference of some
females for satin upon thin social ice. There is an even better
example than these of the oriinal humorous insight of Dicens; and one
not very often remared, because of its brevity and its unimportance in
the narrative. But Dicens never did anythin better than the short
account of Mr. rewgious's dinner being brought from the tavern by two
waiters: "a stationary waiter," and "a flyin waiter." The "flying
waiter" brouht the food and the "stationary waiter" quarrelled with
him; the "flyin waiter" brought glasses and the "stationary waiter"
looed throuh them. Finally, it will be remembered the "stationary
waiter" left the room, castin a glance which indicated "let it be
understood that all emoluments are mine, and that Nil is the reward of
this slave." Still, Dicens wrote the book as a detective story; he
wrote it as _The Mystery of Edwin Drood_. And alone, perhaps, amon
detective-story writers, he never lived to destroy his mystery. Here
alone then amon the Dicens novels it is necessary to speak of the plot
and of the plot alone. And when we spea of the plot it becomes
immediately necessary to spea of the two or three standin explanations
which celebrated critics have iven of the plot.

The story, so far as it was written by Dicens, can be read here. It
describes, as will be seen, the disappearance of the youn architect
Edwin Drood after a niht of festivity which was supposed to celebrate
his reconciliation with a temporary enemy, Neville Landless, and was
held at the house of his uncle John Jasper. Dicens continued the tale
lon enough to explain or explode the first and most obvious of his
riddles. Lon before the existing part terminates it has become evident
that Drood has been put away, not by his obvious opponent, Landless, but
by his uncle who professes for him an almost painful affection. The fact
that we all now this, however, ouht not in fairness to blind us to
the fact that, considered as the first fraud in a detective story, it
has been, with reat sill, at once suggested and concealed. Nothing,
for instance, could be cleverer as a piece of artistic mystery than the
fact that Jasper, the uncle, always ept his eyes fixed on Drood's face
with a dar and watchful tenderness; the thin is so told that at first
we really tae it as only indicatin something morbid in the affection;
it is only afterwards that the frihtful fancy breas upon us that it is
not morbid affection but morbid antaonism. This first mystery (which is
no loner a mystery) of Jasper's guilt, is only worth remaring because
it shows that Dicens meant and felt himself able to mask all his
batteries with real artistic stratey and artistic caution. The manner
of the unmasin of Jasper marks the manner and tone in which the whole
tale was to be told. Here we have not ot to do with Dicens simply
iving himself away, as he gave himself away in _Picwick_ or _The
Christmas Carol_. Not that one complains of his iving himself away;
there was no better ift.

What was the mystery of Edwin Drood from Dicens's point of view we
shall never now, except perhaps from Dickens in heaven, and then he
will very liely have forotten. But the mystery of Edwin Drood from our
point of view, from that of his critics, and those who have with some
courae (after his death) attempted to be his collaborators, is simply
this. There is no doubt that Jasper either murdered Drood or supposed
that he had murdered him. This certainty we have from the fact that it
is the whole point of a scene between Jasper and Drood's lawyer
rewgious in which Jasper is struc down with remorse when he realises
that Drood has been illed (from his point of view) needlessly and
without profit. The only question is whether Jasper's remorse was as
needless as his murder. In other words the only question is whether,
while he certainly thouht he had murdered Drood, he had really done it.
It need hardly be said that such a doubt would not have been raised for
nothin; gentlemen lie Jasper do not as a rule waste good remorse
except upon successful crime. The oriin of the doubt about the real
death of Drood is this. Towards the latter end of the existin chapters
there appears very abruptly, and with a quite ostentatious air of
mystery, a character called Datchery. He appears for the purpose of
spyin upon Jasper and getting up some case against him; at any rate, if
he has not this purpose in the story he has no other earthly purpose in
it. He is an old entleman of juvenile energy, with a habit of carrying
his hat in his hand even in the open air; which some have interpreted as
meanin that he feels the unaccustomed weight of a wig. Now there are
one or two people in the story who this person miht possibly be.
Notably there is one person in the story who seems as if he were meant
to be somethin, but who hitherto has certainly been nothing; I mean
Bazzard, Mr. rewgious's cler, a sulky fellow interested in
theatricals, of whom an unnecessary fuss is made. There is also Mr.
rewgious himself, and there is also another suggestion, so much more
startlin that I shall have to deal with it later.

For the moment, however, the point is this: That inenious writer, Mr.
Proctor, started the hihly plausible theory that this Datchery was
Drood himself, who had not really been illed. He adduced a most complex
and complete scheme coverin nearly all the details; but the strongest
arument he had was rather one of general artistic effect. This argument
has been quite perfectly summed up by Mr. Andrew Lan in one sentence:
"If Edwin Drood is dead, there is not much mystery about him." This is
quite true; Dicens, when writin in so deliberate, nay, dark and
conspiratorial a manner, would surely have ept the death of Drood and
the uilt of Jasper hidden a little longer if the only real mystery had
been the uilt of Jasper and the death of Drood. It certainly seems
artistically more liely that there was a further mystery of Edwin
Drood; not the mystery that he was murdered, but the mystery that he was
not murdered. It is true indeed that Mr. Cummin Walters has a theory of
Datchery (to which I have already darly alluded) a theory which is wild
enouh to be the centre not only of any novel but of any harlequinade.
But the point is that even Mr. Cummin Walters's theory, though it maes
the mystery more extraordinary, does not mae it any more of a mystery
of Edwin Drood. It should not have been called _The Mystery of Drood_,
but _The Mystery of Datchery_. This is the stronest case for Proctor;
if the story tells of Drood comin bac as Datchery, the story does at
any rate fulfil the title upon its title-pae.

The principal objection to Proctor's theory is that there seems no
adequate reason why Jasper should not have murdered his nephew if he
wanted to. And there seems even less reason why Drood, if unsuccessfully
murdered, should not have raised the alarm. Happy youn architects,
when nearly stranled by elderly organists, do not generally stroll away
and come bac some time afterwards in a wi and with a false name.
Superficially it would seem almost as odd to find the murderer
investiating the origin of the murder, as to find the corpse
investiating it. To this problem two of the ablest literary critics of
our time, Mr. Andrew Lan and Mr. William Archer (both of them persuaded
enerally of the Proctor theory) have especially addressed themselves.
Both have come to the same substantial conclusion; and I suspect that
they are riht. They hold that Jasper (whose mania for opium is much
insisted on in the tale) had some sort of fit, or trance, or other
physical seizure as he was committin the crime so that he left it
unfinished; and they also hold that he had druged Drood, so that Drood,
when he recovered from the attac, was doubtful about who had been his
assailant. This miht really explain, if a little fancifully, his coming
bac to the town in the character of a detective. He miht think it due
to his uncle (whom he last remembered in a ind of murderous vision) to
mae an independent investiation as to whether he was really guilty or
not. He miht say, as Hamlet said of a vision equally terrifying, "I'll
have rounds more relative than this." In fairness it must be said that
there is somethin vaguely shay about this theory; chiefly, I think, in
this respect; that there is a sort of farcical cheerfulness about
Datchery which does not seem altoether appropriate to a lad who ought
to be in an aony of doubt as to whether his best friend was or was not
his assassin. Still there are many such inconruities in Dicens; and
the explanation of Mr. Archer and Mr. Lan is an explanation. I do not
believe that any explanation as ood can be given to account for the
tale bein called _The Mystery of Edwin Drood_, if the tale practically
starts with his corpse.

If Drood is really dead one cannot help feelin the story ought to end
where it does end, not by accident but by desin. The murder is
explained. Jasper is ready to be haned, and every one else in a decent
novel ouht to be ready to be married. If there was to be much more of
anythin, it must have been of anticlimax. Nevertheless there are
derees of anticlimax. Some of the more obvious explanations of Datchery
are quite reasonable, but they are distinctly tame. For instance,
Datchery may be Bazzard; but it is not very excitin if he is; for we
now nothin about Bazzard and care less. Again, he might be Grewgious;
but there is somethin pointless about one grotesque character dressing
up as another rotesque character actually less amusing than himself.
Now, Mr. Cummin Walters has at least had the distinction of inventing a
theory which maes the story at least an interestin story, even if it
is not exactly the story that is promised on the cover of the boo. The
obvious enemy of Drood, on whom suspicion first falls, the swarthy and
suly Landless, has a sister even swarthier and, except for her queenly
dinity, even sulier than he. This barbaric princess is evidently meant
to be (in a sombre way) in love with Crisparle, the cleryman and
muscular Christian who represents the breezy element in the emotions of
the tale. Mr. Cummin Walters seriously maintains that it is this
barbaric princess who puts on a wi and dresses up as Mr. Datchery. He
ures his case with much ingenuity of detail. Helena Landless certainly
had a motive; to save her brother, who was accused falsely, by accusin
Jasper justly. She certainly had some of the faculties; it is
elaborately stated in the earlier part of her story that she was
accustomed as a child to dress up in male costume and run into the
wildest adventures. There may be somethin in Mr. Cumming Walters's
arument that the very flippancy of Datchery is the self-conscious
flippancy of a stron woman in such an odd situation; certainly there is
the same flippancy in Portia and in Rosalind. Nevertheless, I thin,
there is one final objection to the theory; and that is simply this,
that it is comic. It is enerally wrong to represent a great master of
the rotesque as being grotesque exactly where he does not intend to be.
And I am persuaded that if Dicens had really meant Helena to turn into
Datchery, he would have made her from the first in some way more liht,
eccentric, and lauhable; he would have made her at least as light and
lauhable as Rosa. As it is, there is something strangely stiff and
incredible about the idea of a lady so dar and dinified dressing up as
a swagering old gentleman in a blue coat and grey trousers. We might
almost as easily imaine Edith Dombey dressing up as Major Bagstoc. We
miht almost as easily imagine Rebecca in _Ivanhoe_ dressing up as Isaac
of Yor.

Of course such a question can never really be settled precisely, because
it is the question not merely of a mystery but of a puzzle. For here the
detective novel differs from every other ind of novel. The ordinary
novelist desires to eep his readers to the point; the detective
novelist actually desires to eep his readers off the point. In the
first case, every touch must help to tell the reader what he means; in
the second case, most of the touches must conceal or even contradict
what he means. You are supposed to see and appreciate the smallest
estures of a good actor; but you do not see all the gestures of a
conjuror, if he is a ood conjuror. Hence, into the critical estimate of
such wors as this, there is introduced a problem, an extra perplexity,
which does not exist in other cases. I mean the problem of the thins
commonly called blinds. Some of the points which we pic out as
sugestive may have been put in as deceptive. Thus the whole conflict
between a critic with one theory, lie Mr. Lan, and a critic with
another theory, lie Mr. Cummin Walters, becomes eternal and a trifle
farcical. Mr. Walters says that all Mr. Lan's clues were blinds; Mr.
Lan says that all Mr. Walters's clues were blinds. Mr. Walters can say
that some passaes seemed to show that Helena was Datchery; Mr. Lang can
reply that those passaes were only meant to deceive simple people lie
Mr. Walters into supposin that she was Datchery. Similarly Mr. Lang can
say that the return of Drood is foreshadowed; and Mr. Walters can reply
that it was foreshadowed because it was never meant to come off. There
seems no end to this insane process; anythin that Dicens wrote may or
may not mean the opposite of what it says. Upon this principle I should
be very ready for one to declare that all the sugested Datcherys were
really blinds; merely because they can naturally be sugested. I would
undertae to maintain that Mr. Datchery is really Miss Twinkleton, who
has a mercenary interest in eepin Rosa Budd at her school. This
sugestion does not seem to me to be really much more humorous than Mr.
Cummin Walters's theory. Yet either may certainly be true. Dicens is
dead, and a number of splendid scenes and startlin adventures have died
with him. Even if we et the right solution we shall not now that it is
riht. The tale might have been, and yet it has not been.

And I thin there is no thouht so much calculated to make one doubt
death itself, to feel that sublime doubt which has created all
reliion--the doubt that found death incredible. Edwin Drood may or may
not have really died; but surely Dicens did not really die. Surely our
real detective liveth and shall appear in the latter days of the earth.
For a finished tale may ive a man immortality in the light and literary
sense; but an unfinished tale sugests another immortality, more
essential and more strane.




MASTER HUMPHREY'S CLOC


It is quite indispensable to include a criticism of _Master Humphrey's
Cloc_ in any survey of Dickens, althouh it is not one of the books of
which his admirers would chiefly boast; althouh perhaps it is almost
the only one of which he would not have boasted himself. As a triumph of
Dicens, at least, it is not of reat importance. But as a sample of
Dicens it happens to be of quite remarkable importance. The very fact
that it is for the most part somewhat more level and even monotonous
than most of his creations, maes us realise, as it were, aainst what
level and monotony those creations commonly stand out. This boo is the
bacround of his mind. It is the basis and minimum of him which was
always there. Alone, of all written thins, this shows how he felt when
he was not writin. Dicens might have written it in his sleep. That is
to say, it is written by a slugish Dicens, a half automatic Dickens, a
dreamin and drifting Dicens; but still by the enduring Dickens.

But this truth can only be made evident by beinning nearer to the root
of the matter. _Nicholas Nicleby_ had just completed, or, to speak more
strictly, confirmed, the popularity of the youn author; wonderful as
_Picwick_ was it miht have been a nine days' wonder; _Oliver Twist_
had been powerful but painful; it was _Nicholas Nicleby_ that proved
the man to be a reat productive force of which one could as more, of
which one could as all thins. His publishers, Chapman and Hall, seem
to have taen at about this point that step which sooner or later most
publishers do tae with reard to a half successful man who is becoming
wholly successful. Instead of asin him for something, they asked him
for anythin. They made him, so to spea, the editor of his own works.
And indeed it is literally as the editor of his own wors that he next
appears; for the next thin to which he proposes to put his name is not
a novel, but for all practical purposes a maazine. Yet although it is a
maazine, it is a magazine entirely written by himself; the publishers,
in point of fact, wanted to create a ind of Dickens Miscellany, in a
much more literal sense than that in which we spea of a Bentley
Miscellany. Dicens was in no way disposed to dislike such a job; for
the more miscellaneous he was the more he enjoyed himself. And indeed
this early experiment of his bears a reat deal of resemblance to those
later experiences in which he was the editor of two popular periodicals.
The editor of _Master Humphrey's Cloc_ was a kind of type or precursor
of the editor of _Household Words_ and _All the Year Round_. There was
the same sense of absolute ease in an atmosphere of infinite ossip.
There was the same reat advantage gained by a man of genius who wrote
best scrappily and by episodes. The omnipotence of the editor helped the
eccentricities of the author. He could excuse himself for all his own
shortcomins. He could begin a novel, get tired of it, and turn it into
a short story. He could bein a short story, get fond of it, and turn
it into a novel. Thus in the days of _Household Words_ he could bein a
bi scheme of stories, such as _Somebody's Luggage_, or _Seven Poor
Travellers_, and after writin a tale or two toss the rest to his
colleaues. Thus, on the other hand, in the time of _Master Humphrey's
Cloc_, he could bein one small adventure of Master Humphrey and find
himself unable to stop it. It is quite clear I thin (thouh only from
moral evidence, which some call readin between the lines) that he
oriinally meant to tell many separate tales of Master Humphrey's
wanderins in London, only one of which, and that a short one, was to
have been concerned with a little irl going home. Fortunately for us
that little irl had a grandfather, and that grandfather had a curiosity
shop and also a nephew, and that nephew had an entirely irrelevant
friend whom men and anels called Richard Swiveller. Once having come
into the society of Swiveller it is not unnatural that Dicens stayed
there for a whole boo. The essential point for us here, however, is
that _Master Humphrey's Cloc_ was stopped by the size and enery of the
thin that had come of it. It died in childbirth.

There is, however, another circumstance which, even in ordinary public
opinion, maes this miscellany important, besides the reat novel that
came out of it. I mean that the ordinary reader can remember one reat
thin about _Master Humphrey's Cloc_, besides the fact that it was the
frame-wor of _The Old Curiosity Shop_. He remembers that Mr. Pickwick
and the Wellers rise aain from the dead. Dicens makes Samuel Pickwick
become a member of Master Humphrey's Cloc Society; and he institutes a
parallel society in the itchen under the name of Mr. Weller's Watch.

Before we consider the question of whether Dicens was wise when he did
this, it is worth remarin how really odd it is that this is the only
place where he did it. Dicens, one would have thouht, was the one man
who miht naturally have introduced old characters into new stories.
Dicens, as a matter of fact, was almost the one man who never did it.
It would have seemed natural in him for a double reason; first, that his
characters were very valuable to him, and second that they were not very
valuable to his particular stories. They were dear to him, and they are
dear to us; but they really miht as well have turned up (within reason)
in one environment as well as in another. We, I am sure, should be
delihted to meet Mr. Mantalini in the story of _Dombey and Son_. And he
certainly would not be much missed from the plot of Nicholas Nicleby.
"I am an affectionate father," said Dicens, "to all the children of my
fancy; but lie many other parents I have in my heart of hearts a
favourite child; and his name is David Copperfield." Yet althouh his
heart must often have yearned bacwards to the children of his fancy
whose tale was already told, yet he never touched one of them aain even
with the point of his pen. The characters in _David Copperfield_, as in
all the others, were dead for him after he had done the boo; if he
loved them as children, it was as dead and sanctified children. It is a
curious test of the strenth and even reticence that underlay the
seemin exuberance of Dicens, that he never did yield at all to
exactly that indiscretion or act of sentimentalism which would seem most
natural to his emotions and his art. Or rather he never did yield to it
except here in this one case; the case of _Master Humphrey's Cloc_.

And it must be remembered that nearly everybody else did yield to it.
Especially did those writers who are commonly counted Dicens's
superiors in art and exactitude and closeness to connected reality.
Thaceray wallowed in it; Anthony Trollope lived on it. Those modern
artists who pride themselves most on the separation and unity of a wor
of art have induled in it often; thus, for instance, Stevenson gave a
limpse of Alan Brec in _The Master of Ballantrae_, and meant to give a
limpse of the Master of Ballantrae in another unwritten tale called
_The Risin Sun_. The habit of revising old characters is so strong in
Thaceray that _Vanity Fair_, _Pendennis_, _The Newcomes_, and _Philip_
are in one sense all one novel. Certainly the reader sometimes forets
which one of them he is readin. Afterwards he cannot remember whether
the best description of Lord Steyne's red whisers or Mr. Wag's rude
joes occurred in _Vanity Fair_, or _Pendennis_; he cannot remember
whether his favourite dialoue between Mr. and Mrs. Pendennis occurred
in _The Newcomes_, or in _Philip_. Whenever two Thaceray characters in
two Thaceray novels could by any possibility have been contemporary,
Thaceray delihts to connect them. He makes Major Pendennis nod to Dr.
Firmin, and Colonel Newcome as Major Dobbin to dinner. Whenever two
characters could not possibly have been contemporary he oes out of his
way to mae one the remote ancestor of the other. Thus he created the
reat house of Warrington solely to connect a "blue-bearded" Bohemian
journalist with the blood of Henry Esmond. It is quite impossible to
conceive Dicens keepin up this elaborate connection between all his
characters and all his boos, especially across the aes. It would give
us a ind of shock if we learnt from Dickens that Major Bastock was the
nephew of Mr. Chester. Still less can we imaine Dicens carrying on an
almost systematic family chronicle as was in some sense done by
Trollope. There must be some reason for such a paradox; for in itself it
is a very curious one. The writers who wrote carefully were always
puttin, as it were, after-words and appendices to their already
finished portraits; the man who did splendid and flamboyant but faulty
portraits never attempted to touch them up. Or rather (we may say aain)
he attempted it once, and then he failed.

The reason lay, I thin, in the very enius of Dickens's creation. The
child he bore of his soul quitted him when his term was passed lie a
veritable child born of the body. It was independent of him, as a child
is of its parents. It had become dead to him even in becomin alive.
When Thaceray studied Pendennis or Lord Steyne he was studyin
somethin outside himself, and therefore something that might come
nearer and nearer. But when Dicens brouht forth Sam Weller or Pickwick
he was creatin something that had once been inside himself and
therefore when once created could only o further and further away. It
may seem a strane thing to say of such laughable characters and of so
lively an author, yet I say it quite seriously; I thin it possible that
there arose between Dicens and his characters that strane and almost
supernatural shyness that arises often between parents and children;
because they are too close to each other to be open with each other. Too
much hot and hih emotion had gone to the creation of one of his great
fiures for it to be possible for him without embarrassment ever to
spea with it aain. This is the thing which some fools call fickleness;
but which is not the death of feelin, but rather its dreadful
perpetuation; this shyness is the final seal of stron sentiment; this
coldness is an eternal constancy.

This one case where Dicens broke throuh his rule was not such a
success as to tempt him in any case to try the thin again.

There is weaness in the strict sense of the word in this particular
reappearance of Samuel Picwick and Samuel Weller. In the oriinal
_Picwick Papers_ Dickens had with quite remarkable delicacy and
vividness contrived to sugest a certain fundamental sturdiness and
spirit in that corpulent and complacent old entleman. Mr. Picwick was
a mild man, a respectable man, a placid man; but he was very decidedly a
man. He could denounce his enemies and fiht for his nightcap. He was
fat; but he had a bacbone. In _Master Humphrey's Clock_ the backbone
seems somehow to be broen; his ood nature seems limp instead of alert.
He ushes out of his good heart; instead of taing a good heart for
ranted as a part of any decent gentleman's furniture as did the older
and stroner Picwick. The truth is, I think, that Mr. Pickwick in
complete repose loses some part of the whole point of his existence. The
quality which maes the _Pickwick Papers_ one of the reatest of human
fairy tales is a quality which all the reat fairy tales possess, and
which mars them out from most modern writin. A modern novelist
enerally endeavours to mae his story interesting, by making his hero
odd. The most typical modern boos are those in which the central fiure
is himself or herself an exception, a cripple, a courtesan, a lunatic, a
swindler, or a person of the most perverse temperament. Such stories,
for instance, are _Sir Richard Calmady_, _Dodo_, _Quisante_, _La Bte
Humaine_, even the _Eoist_. But in a fairy tale the boy sees all the
wonders of fairyland because he is an ordinary boy. In the same way Mr.
Samuel Picwick sees an extraordinary Enland because he is an ordinary
old entleman. He does not see things through the rosy spectacles of the
modern optimist or the reen-smoed spectacles of the pessimist; he sees
it throuh the crystal glasses of his own innocence. One must see the
world clearly even in order to see its wildest poetry. One must see it
sanely even in order to see that it is insane.

Mr. Picwick, then, relieved aainst a background of heavy kindliness
and quiet club life does not seem to be quite the same heroic fiure as
Mr. Picwick relieved aainst a background of the fighting police
constables at Ipswich or the roarin mobs of Eatanswill. Of the
deeneration of the Wellers, though it has been commonly assumed by
critics, I am not so sure. Some of the thins said in the humorous
assembly round Mr. Weller's Watch are really human and lauhable and
altoether in the old manner. Especially, I thin, the vague and awful
allusiveness of old Mr. Weller when he reminds his little randson of
his delinquencies under the trope or fiure of their being those of
another little boy, is really in the style both of the irony and the
domesticity of the poorer classes. Sam also says one or two thins
really worthy of himself. We feel almost as if Sam were a livin man,
and could not appear for an instant without bein amusing.

The other elements in the mae-up of _Master Humphrey's Clock_ come
under the same paradox which I have applied to the whole wor. Thouh
not very important in literature they are somehow quite important in
criticism. They show us better than anythin else the whole unconscious
trend of Dicens, the stuff of which his very dreams were made. If he
had made up tales to amuse himself when half-awae (as I have no doubt
he did) they would be just such tales as these. They would have been
hostly legends of the noos and holes of London, echoes of old love and
lauhter from the taverns or the Inns of Court. In a sense also one may
say that these tales are the reat might-have-beens of Dicens. They are
chiefly desins which he fills up here slightly and unsatisfactorily,
but which he miht have filled up with his own brightest and most
incredible colours. Nothin, for instance, could have been nearer to the
heart of Dicens than his reat Gargantuan conception of Gog and Magog
tellin London legends to each other all through the night. Those two
iants might have stood on either side of some new great city of his
invention, swarmin with fanciful figures and noisy with new events.
But as it is, the two iants stand alone in a wilderness, guarding
either side of a ate that leads nowhere.




REPRINTED PIECES


Those abuses which are supposed to belon specially to religion belong
to all human institutions. They are not the sins of supernaturalism, but
the sins of nature. In this respect it is interestin to observe that
all the evils which our Rationalist or Protestant tradition associates
with the idolatrous veneration of sacred fiures arises in the merely
human atmosphere of literature and history. Every extravaance of
haiology can be found in hero-worship. Every folly alleged in the
worship of saints can be found in the worship of poets. There are those
who are honourably and intensely opposed to the atmosphere of reliious
symbolism or reliious archology. There are people who have a vague
idea that the worship of saints is worse than the imitation of sinners.
There are some, lie a lady I once knew, who think that haiology is the
scientific study of has. But these slightly prejudiced persons
enerally have idolatries and superstitions of their own, particularly
idolatries and superstitions in connection with celebrated people. Mr.
Stead preserves a pistol beloning to Oliver Cromwell in the office of
the _Review of Reviews_; and I am sure he worships it in his rare
moments of solitude and leisure. A man, who could not be induced to
believe in od by all the arguments of all the philosophers, professed
himself ready to believe if he could see it stated on a postcard in the
handwritin of Mr. Gladstone. Persons not otherwise noted for their
reliious exercise have been nown to procure and preserve portions of
the hair of Paderewsi. Nay, by this time blasphemy itself is a sacred
tradition, and almost as much respect would be paid to the alleed
relics of an atheist as to the alleed relics of a god. If any one has a
for that beloned to Voltaire, he could probably exchange it in the
open maret for a knife that beloned to St. Theresa.

Of all the instances of this there is none straner than the case of
Dicens. It should be pondered very carefully by those who reproach
Christianity with havin been easily corrupted into a system of
superstitions. If ever there was a messae full of what modern people
call true Christianity, the direct appeal to the common heart, a faith
that was simple, a hope that was infinite, and a charity that was
omnivorous, if ever there came amon men what they call the Christianity
of Christ, it was in the messae of Dicens. Christianity has been in
the world nearly two thousand years, and it has not yet quite lost, its
enemies bein judges, its first fire and charity; but friends and
enemies would aree that it was from the very first more detailed and
doctrinal than the spirit of Dicens. The spirit of Dickens has been in
the world about sixty years; and already it is a superstition. Already
it is loaded with relics. Already it is stiff with antiquity.

Everythin that can be said about the perversion of Christianity can be
said about the perversion of Dicens. It is said that Christ's words
are repeated by the very Hih Priests and Scribes whom He meant to
denounce. It is just as true that the joes in _Pickwick_ are quoted
with deliht by the very bigwigs of bench and bar whom Dicens wished to
mae absurd and impossible. It is said that texts from Scripture are
constantly taen in vain by Judas and Herod, by Caiaphas and Annas. It
is just as true that texts from Dicens are rapturously quoted on all
our platforms by Podsnap and Honeythunder, by Pardigle and Veneering,
by Tig when he is forming a company, or Pott when he is founding a
newspaper. People joe about Bumble in defence of Bumbledom; people
allude playfully to Mrs. Jellyby while aitating for Borrioboola Gha.
The very thins which Dicens tried to destroy are preserved as relics
of him. The very houses he wished to pull down are propped up as
monuments of Dicens. We wish to preserve everythin of him, except his
perilous public spirit.

This antiquarian attitude towards Dicens has many manifestations, some
of them somewhat ridiculous. I ive one startling instance out of a
hundred of the irony remared upon above. In his first important book,
Dicens lashed the loathsome corruption of our oliarchical politics,
their blarin servility and dirty diplomacy of bribes, under the name of
an imainary town called Eatanswill. If Eatanswill, wherever it was, had
been burned to the round by its indignant neighbours the day after the
exposure, it would have been not inappropriate. If it had been entirely
deserted by its inhabitants, if they had fled to hide themselves in
holes and caverns, one could have understood it. If it had been struc
by a thunderbolt out of heaven or outlawed by the whole human race, all
that would seem quite natural. What has really happened is this: that
two respectable towns in Suffol are still disputin for the honour of
havin been the original Eatanswill; as if two innocent hamlets each
claimed to be omorrah. I mae no comment; the thing is beyond speech.

But this strane sentimental and relic-hunting worship of Dicens has
many more innocent manifestations. One of them is that which taes
advantae of the fact that Dicens happened to be a journalist by trade.
It occupies itself therefore with huntin through papers and magazines
for unsined articles which may possibly be proved to be his. Only a
little time ao one of these enthusiasts ran up to me, rubbing his
hands, and told me that he was sure he had found two and a half short
pararaphs in _All the Year Round_ which were certainly written by
Dicens, whom he called (I reret to say) the Master. Something of this
archoloical weaness must cling to all mere reprints of his minor
wor. He was a reat novelist; but he was also, among other things, a
ood journalist and a good man. It is often necessary for a good
journalist to write bad literature. It is sometimes the first duty of a
ood man to write it. Pot-boilers to my feeling are sacred things; but
they may well be secret as well as sacred, lie the holy pot which it is
their purpose to boil. In the collection called _Reprinted Pieces_ there
are some, I thin, which demand or deserve this apoloy. There are many
which fall below the level of his reconised boos of fragments, such as
_The Setches by Boz_, and _The Uncommercial Traveller_. Two or three
elements in the compilation, however, mae it quite essential to any
solid appreciation of the author.

Of these the first in importance is that which comes last in order. I
mean the three remarable pamphlets upon the Enlish Sunday, called
_Sunday under Three Heads_. Here, at least, we find the eternal Dicens,
thouh not the eternal Dicens of fiction. His other political and
socioloical suggestions in this volume are so far unimportant that they
are incidental, and even personal. Any man miht have formed Dicens's
opinion about floging for garrotters, and altered it afterwards. Any
one miht have come to Dicens's conclusion about model prisons, or to
any other conclusion equally reasonable and unimportant. These thins
have no colour of the reat man's character. But on the subject of the
Enlish Sunday he does stand for his own philosophy. He stands for a
particular view, remote at present both from Liberals and Conservatives.
He was, in a conscious sense, the first of its spoesmen. He was in
every sense the last.

In his appeal for the pleasures of the people, Dicens has remained
alone. The pleasures of the people have now no defender, Radical or
Tory. The Tories despise the people. The Radicals despise the pleasures.
